## **Plant Breeder’s Rights** 

The majority of the varieties mentioned in this guide are protected by Plant Breeder’s Rights where the variety name is followed by the symbol[A] , however this symbol has been omitted in this bulletin. 

Plant Breeder’s Rights are exclusive commercial rights for a registered variety. In most instances the breeder will license these rights to a selected seed company (the licensee). Any unauthorised commercial propagation or any sale, conditioning, import, export or stocking of propagation material of these varieties is an infringement under the _Plant Breeder’s Rights Act 1994_ . 

Exceptions to Plant Breeder’s Rights under Section 17 of the Act, are the rights of farmers to save seed for sowing future commercial crops. However, harvested material derived from farm saved seed remains subject to the End Point Royalty (EPR) applicable to that variety. Where EPRs apply, growers will be required to enter into arrangements with the breeder or licensee whereby royalties are paid on delivery of the grain. Some varieties may have a Seed Royalty (SR) paid on purchase of seed rather than an EPR. Further details can be found at http://www.varietycentral.com.au/ . Royalties collected are used to support ongoing research and the breeding of new and improved varieties. 

**3** 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Introduction 

## **Interpreting resistance classifications** 

Below is an explanation of the resistance ratings used in this guide for foliar diseases, nematodes and crown rot and how they should be interpreted. These classifications are only a guide and yield losses will depend on environmental and seasonal conditions. Regional and national differences in disease resistance may also occur for different pathotypes. 

## **Cereal resistance classifications (foliar diseases)** 

- **R Resistant:** the disease will not multiply or cause any damage. 

- **MR Moderately resistant:** the disease may be visible and will multiply slightly but will not cause significant yield loss. 

- **MS Moderately susceptible:** the disease may cause yield losses up to 15% or more in very severe cases. 

- **S Susceptible:** the disease can be severe and yield losses of 15 to 50% can occur. 

- **VS Very susceptible:** the variety should not be grown in areas where a disease is likely to be a problem. Yield losses higher than 50% are possible and the build-up of inoculum will create problems for other growers. 

## **Pulse resistance classifications (foliar diseases)** 

No pulse varieties are immune to disease and fungicide application may therefore be required under severe disease pressure. 

- **R Resistant:** no symptoms visible, no fungicides are required. 

- **MR Moderately resistant:** the disease may be visible but will not cause significant plant damage or loss. However, under high disease pressure or highly favourable environmental conditions fungicide applications may be required e.g. to prevent seed staining. 

- **MS Moderately susceptible:** disease symptoms are moderate to severe and will cause significant yield and seed quality loss (but not complete crop loss) in conducive seasons in the absence of fungicides. 

- **S Susceptible** : the disease symptoms are severe and in conducive conditions will cause significant yield and seed quality loss, including complete crop loss in the absence of fungicides. 

- **VS Very susceptible:** growing very susceptible varieties in areas where a disease is likely to be present is very high risk. Without control significant yield and seed quality losses, including complete crop loss, can be expected and the increase in inoculum may create problems for other growers. 

## **Nematode resistance classifications** 

**PLEASE NOTE:** Resistance ratings for _Pratylenchus neglectus_ for all pulses (except chickpeas) and wheat varieties released since 2018 have not been tested in WA and should be used as a guide only. Chickpea resistance ratings to _P. neglectus_ are from trials conducted in WA. Resistance ratings for _P. quasitereoides_ are also from trials conducted in WA. 

- **R Resistant:** nematode numbers will decrease when resistant varieties are grown. 

- **MR Moderately resistant:** nematode numbers are expected to decrease slightly in most seasons when moderately resistant varieties are grown. 

- **MS Moderately susceptible:** nematode numbers are expected to increase slightly in most seasons when moderately susceptible varieties are grown. 

- **S Susceptible:** nematode numbers will increase when susceptible varieties are grown. 

- **VS Very susceptible:** a large increase in nematode numbers can occur when very susceptible varieties are grown. 

## **Colour range** 

**VS SVS S MSS MS MRMS MR RMR R** 

**4 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Introduction 

## **Introduction** 

**Welcome to the 2026 edition of the Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide, which introduces 24 new variety releases: 6 canola, 3 lentils, 2 vetches, 2 feed barley, 3 barley varieties undergoing malt accreditation and 8 wheat varieties.** 

The 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide has been compiled by officers in the Department of Primary Industries and Regional Development. It provides information to support variety decisions for each of the major crops for the upcoming season. 

## **Seasonal conditions between 2024 and 2023** 

were very different, more so in some regions than others, hence it is important to recognize how this may influence a variety/crop’s performance.  It is critical to view variety performance over multiple years to better understand how a variety might perform across variable seasons. 

**Variety release with limited or no NVT data** has been common with new canola varieties in the past and is now occurring with wheat and barley varieties. Many aspects of variety performance, as well as disease and agronomic traits should be reviewed prior to increasing the scale of such varieties. 

**Marketing** of a commodity or a particular variety can influence the decision to change a variety, particularly for barley. To help with barley decisions, market feedback from GIWA can be found on page 79 of this guide. Before making a barley variety choice, it is important to consider market demand, pricing signals, location of segregation sites and the risk associated of not meeting the specifications for malt-grade barley. 

In the past, **CL Plus wheat varieties** were not free to trade due to the Clearfield[®] licence agreements. This has recently changed, allowing grower-togrower sales of seed. Various breeding companies 

may have specific conditions for grower-togrower sales and it is recommended to check the conditions for your variety. 

## **Incidence of smut diseases** has been 

increasing over the last few seasons in both barley (loose smut) and wheat (flag smut); due to conducive seasons, inadequate seed coverage of fungicide seed dressing, insufficient uptake of fungicide seed dressings due to the dry conditions at seeding, use of untreated seed and less varietal resistance. There is increasing evidence that many of the newly released barley and wheat varieties are more susceptible, which has prompted a new DPIRD/GRDC project to update smut ratings on newer varieties. The main management option for these diseases is to use effective seed dressings and to also be aware of the risk of self-sown susceptible varieties which are likely to carry the infection and prolong the soil-inoculum. 

In many instances, varietal resistance to a particular disease can play a vital role in its management, thus resistance ratings are listed in the guide for the various crop types. Varietal disease resistance ratings vary over time due to seasonal changes in disease pressure, regional disease spread, climatic conditions, stubble retention and the development of new pathotypes/races. As a result, resistance ratings of a particular variety can vary between sowing guides. Fungicides are increasingly the main ‘go-to’ option for disease management. To ensure their continued effectiveness, fungicide resistance 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 5** 

Introduction 

should be managed to reduce future resistance development by rotating fungicide groups or using fungicide mixtures containing different modes of action. For more detailed information on fungicide resistance (incidence and management) and other, non-chemical, options for minimising disease potential, see the Australian Fungicide Resistance Network’s website ( afren.com.au/) . 

**Frost** can have a devastating effect on crop yield, and matching variety maturity with sowing time remains the most reliable way of reducing yield losses. The relative maturities of wheat, barley and oat varieties are provided in their respective sections of this guide to help match sowing opportunities with the best variety. Flower Power is also a useful tool to match the predicted flowering times of wheat, barley and oats with sowing date in your area ( https://fp.dpird.app ). 

No wheat or barley variety is tolerant to frost, and other crops vary in their susceptibility. Strategies for managing frost are available on the GRDC and DPIRD websites. 

Additional information to support crop variety decisions is listed in each section. Advisers can provide locally relevant information and growers are encouraged to use this publication as a guide to support discussions with consultants, agronomists and marketing agents. 

It is important for growers and consultants to review disease resistance ratings in autumn 2026 to confirm variety resistance ratings for new varieties and any changes to existing varieties. The latest NVT data will be available early in 2026 via the NVT website and the Long Term MET Yield Reporter tool. 



**6 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

## **Wheat** 

## **Introduction** 

## **Brenda Shackley and Dion Nicol (DPIRD)** 

The 2024 season ended up with the third largest tonnage on record with an estimated wheat production of 12.4 million tonnes. WA’s wheat production of 7.6 million tonnes in 2023 was nearly 55% lower than the state’s largest ever wheat crop of 13.9 million tonnes in 2022 (source: GIWA crop estimates). For many growers the total rainfall received in 2024 was well down or well above previous years; however, improvements in varieties, sowing strategies and timing in current production systems are providing a buffer in the poorer seasons and push grain yields beyond 

records in the good seasons. For much of the state’s wheat production areas in 2023 and 2024, there were limited early germination opportunities. This means that bias can exist in the data from trial series such as the National Variety Trials (NVT) because most of the data is from an end-ofMay germination. Consideration of maturity types, intended use and sowing dates are therefore required when reviewing the NVT data and making your variety choices. 

With the continued influx of recently released wheat varieties from the major Australian wheat breeding companies, information presented in this section is designed to assist growers in determining what wheat varieties to grow. 



**7** 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

Scepter accounted for roughly 35% of the area sown to wheat in 2025, making it still the most popular variety grown in WA. As such, it is used as the comparison variety for yield (in the variety snapshots) and days to flowering data (Table 14). The reasons for diversifying from Scepter are yield advantages and different types of maturities to accommodate a wider range of sowing opportunities. Other reasons for variety change include agronomic traits, disease resistances, weed control options (primarily tolerance to imidazolinone (IMI)) and the potential for premium prices for alternate quality types such as noodle wheats. 

Environmental conditions are a major driver of crop performance, making it important to review variety performance over multiple years to better understand how a variety might perform across variable seasons in a specific location. For example; the wetter season and soft/late finish experienced in 2022 saw the improved relative performance of the slower maturing varieties. The later germination with a dry finish to the season in 2023 was the opposite, with improved performance of the quick maturing varieties and poorer relative performance of the slower maturing varieties. The 2024 season was generally a lower decile rainfall year, however the 

northern part of the grainbelt had a late start but finished with 7+ decile rainfall. Varietal differences can also be used to mitigate the risk of frost or terminal drought by matching the maturity rating of a variety with its optimum sowing time (and likely germination). Finally, it is important to be aware of whether a variety can access specific quality segregations and what the value of the segregation may be for the season. 

A new variety should provide: 

- an improvement in yield, grain quality and/or disease traits 

- diversity or risk mitigation within a farming system 

- suitable characteristics for current markets. 

The WA wheat industry is supported by significant private and public R&D that underpins decisions on variety selection and management. The wheat section of the guide summarises the yield performance, disease ratings and best practice agronomy of varieties in the GRDC NVT and DPIRD agronomy research. Variety snapshots for 26 of the most common and recently released wheat varieties can be found at the back of the guide. 



**8** 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

## **New varieties for 2026** 

## **AGT-Carnac** 

AGT-Carnac (WAGT1170) is an Australian Noodle Wheat (ANW) released by AGT in 2025. AGT-Carnac has only been included in the 2024 NVT, yielding more than Ninja and Zen, similar to Scepter and slightly less than the highest yielding ANW variety Rottnest which AGT released in 2024. Maturity scores from NVT indicate that AGT-Carnac and Rottnest may have similar maturity in between Ninja and Zen. AGT-Carnac has a provisionally more robust disease package than Ninja or Zen with a moderately resistant moderately susceptible (MRMS _p_ ) for stem rust and yellow spot, resistant to moderately resistant (RMR _p_ ) for stripe rust, moderately susceptible to susceptible (MSS _p_ ) for leaf rust and susceptible (S _p_ ) to powdery mildew. 

## **AGT-Colt** 

AGT-Colt (RAC3254) is a slow maturity, Australian Hard (AH) variety released by AGT in 2025. Early season NVT in 2024 suggests that AGT-Colt is slightly lower yielding than Denison, an APW with a potentially similar maturity. AGT-Colt has strong resistance ratings to foliar diseases with resistant (R) to stripe rust and moderately resistant (MR _p_ ) to stem and leaf rust, powdery mildew and flag smut. Yellow spot is MRMS _p_ . 

## **AGT-Hamelin** 

AGT-Hamelin (WAGT1159) is a slow spring wheat with an AH classification release by AGT in 2025. It has only been included in the main and early season NVT since 2024, where it has yielded similar to RockStar or slightly higher in the earlier sowing NVT. AGT-Hamelin potentially has a maturity between Catapult and Denison. Provisionally, AGT-Hamelin has a more robust disease package than RockStar, Catapult and Denison, with a higher leaf rust rating of MR _p_ and MSS _p_ to powdery mildew. Other ratings are MRMS _p_ for stem rust and yellow spot, and RMR for stripe rust. Data from 2024 NVT show high hectolitre weights and below average screenings. 

## **Dale** 

Dale (IGW6993) is an AH released by InterGrain in 2025, suggested to be similar maturity to Scepter. 

Dale has only been included in the WA NVT for the first time in 2025, so information is limited to that provided by InterGrain at the time of publication. Disease ratings are to be confirmed by GRDC and DPIRD later in 2025 or early 2026. 

## **Kestrel** 

Kestrel (IGW6821) is a potentially quick maturity, AH released by InterGrain in 2025. Kestrel has only been included in the WA NVT for the first time in 2025, so information is limited to that provided by InterGrain at the time of publication. Disease ratings are to be confirmed by GRDC and DPIRD later in 2025 or early 2026. 

## **LRPB Sonic** 

LRPB Sonic (LPB20-10855) was released by LongReach in 2025. It is an AH with a LRPB Cobra background. It is suggested to be a quick–mid maturity, only slightly quicker than Scepter. LRPB Sonic has only been included in the 2024 NVT where it was one of the leading varieties for yield, particularly in high yield potential sites. Disease ratings are moderately susceptible (MS _p_ ) for leaf rust, MR _p_ for stripe rust and MRMS _p_ for stem rust and yellow spot and MSS _p_ for powdery mildew. 

## **RGT Marsh** 

RGT Marsh (H16Q3x0336.SCI-097D) is a new potential AH released by RAGT Australia in 2025. Final classification is still pending. RGT Marsh is potentially similar to Scepter and Calibre in yield and maturity. It is MRMS for stem rust, RMR for stripe rust, MSS for leaf rust, S _p_ for powdery mildew and MSS for yellow spot. It has an MR _p_ rating for flag smut as well as MRMS _p_ for nodorum blotch (leaf and glume). 

## **Wallaroo** 

Wallaroo (SWW21.001) is a quick winter wheat with an Australian Premium White (APW) classification now available from Trigall Australia and The SeedGroup. It has been included in the early season NVT since 2022, where it has yielded higher than the current benchmark quick winter variety, Illabo. Wallaroo has a robust rust package with all 3 rusts rated as RMR. Powdery mildew is rated as MSS and yellow spot is MRMS. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 9** 

Wheat 

## **Variety classification Source: Grains Australia** 

## **Removal of varieties** 

Grains Australia maintains the Wheat Variety Master List with annual reviews of varieties that are more than 10 years old and which have accounted for less than 0.25% of deliveries over previous 5 seasons for the hard classes and less than 5.0% of smaller classes (ANW, ADR and ASFT) in a classification zone. The soft-wheat variety Bullaring was removed from the list in 2025, due to decreasing production thresholds. Varieties planned for removal in 2026 include Espada, Fortune and Gladius, and Harper in 2027. Varieties removed from the master list can still be delivered as feed. 

## **Australian Premium White Noodle (APWN)** 

APWN is a quality class created to allow varietal control of the hard wheat component of export blends with ANW, and to optimise end-use quality for the premium Japanese udon noodle market. APWN classification has been determined for the following AH and APW varieties: Brumby, Chief CL Plus, Cutlass, Devil, Dozer CL Plus, EGA Bonnie Rock, Hammer CL Plus, King Rock, LRPB Avenger, LRPB Havoc, LRPB Trojan, LRPB Scout, Mace, RockStar, Vixen, Westonia and Wyalkatchem. Specifications are similar to APW1 but selected varieties also need to be within a grain protein range of 10.0% to 11.5% instead of a minimum of 10.5%. 

## **Please see the CBH Harvest Guide and website for up-to-date delivery details.** 



**10 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

## **Recent trends in WA wheat** 

The challenge with the rapid release of varieties is that specific weaknesses and strengths may not be identified by the time growers are looking to adopt these varieties at scale. Seasonal issues such as low falling number, terminal drought or other stresses impacting physical grain quality and disease pressures will influence the retention of varieties and their impact on ease of cultivation and risk will vary across farming systems and regionally. 

Since 2019, Scepter has been the dominant variety for WA. It has generally maintained the best or equivalent yields in all regions and had an excellent overall agronomic package in terms of grain receival standards, disease and maintaining a good falling number. Scepter’s dominance peaked at 53% of the crop in 2020 and did not reach nor maintain the dominance of its predecessor Mace (70%), due to a more competitive suite of varieties that have demonstrated niches of value to growers regionally or across sowing windows. However, a number of the Scepter replacements are already starting to decline as seasonal conditions may show various weaknesses as well as improved alternatives. For example, Calibre, Rockstar and Vixen are already seeing declining planting areas since their peak areas in 2023 or 2024 (Table 1). Promising new varieties like Tomahawk CL Plus and LRPB Vortex are already increasing in planting areas. 

Clearfield[®] (particularly two-gene CL Plus) varieties as a subset have seen the greatest gains in yield. Despite the traits reputed to have no yield impact, it has taken time for breeding programs to close the yield gap. The total overall proportion of varieties that are IMI tolerant has remained at around 10% but this may change with Tomahawk CL Plus already at 4.4% in 2025, as it remains one of the current overall highest yielding wheat varieties in NVT data. Other CL Plus wheats are likely to continue to diminish, given the yield difference to Tomahawk CL Plus. 

ANW have ranged from 8.5 to 16% of the WA wheat crop in the past 5 years but with relative stability for the major varieties. Zen has remained relatively stable due to its physical grain quality and ease of achieving specification even in stressful conditions, Kinsei for earlier sowing and Ninja as the higher yielding main season ANW. AGT have now entered the ANW variety market with Rottnest and AGT-Carnac as well as the release of Firefly and Splendid from InterGrain.

Varieties that were released prior to 2015 (including Mace) now total less than 4% of the WA wheat crop. With the rapid release of new varieties, it is anticipated that uptake will be swift, but if yield data and the overall agronomic package of a new variety does not demonstrate sustained improvements, it will disappear just as fast. Many aspects of variety performance, as well as disease and agronomic traits, are not available when varieties are released, so this will need reviewing before increased scale of planting of these varieties. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 11** 

Wheat 

## **What variety should I grow?** 

There are a large number of wheat varieties available to growers with diverse yield performance, quality attributes, agronomic characteristics, and disease resistance profiles. When selecting wheat varieties for your farm, it is important to consider: 

- yield performance in a specific environment over multiple seasons 

- how variety maturity will match to a targeted sowing time (refer to suggested sowing window – Table 14) 

- varietal herbicide tolerance and weed control options 

- varietal disease resistance ratings, particularly for prevalent diseases. 

   - _Please note that due to the incursion of new strains and mutations of pathogens already present in WA, it is essential to review disease ratings of existing varieties each year as these can change (Table 17)_ 

- risk of downgrades due to physical quality (i.e. screenings and hectolitre weight) 

- susceptibility to pre-harvest sprouting (presented as falling number index ratings) and black point, particularly if sowing into April or early May where the risk is higher (Table 21). 

While farming operations may also seek to limit the number of varieties on farm, there are benefits to diversification of varieties to increase production and/or reduce risk. For example: 

- selecting varieties of slower or quicker maturity to optimise yield potential across a range of sowing time/germination opportunities and frost risk profiles 

- selecting varieties with improved disease resistance ratings to reduce disease risk and in-season management challenges when diseases may increase in prevalence 

- growing varieties of multiple quality grades to respond to different pricing signals. 

Tables 2 to 6 provide a clear visual comparison of key traits among different maturity and classification niches. In Tables 2 to 6, the statewide multi environment trial (MET) yield (presented as a percentage of site mean) is combined across the 6 Agzones, and a 5-year weighted average has been calculated from the MET data. Caution should be exercised when examining the weighted average as it can mask important variety-by-environment interactions (i.e. how variety performance changes under different seasonal conditions). Refer to Tables 7 to 12 for a more precise estimate of variety performance in specific regions for NVT main season sowings. 



**12 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

Yield performance from April sowing (early season NVT) are provided in Tables 6 and 13. 

The content of the Tables 2 to 6 has changed in this edition due to the general improvement of resistance to a number of diseases such as stem and stripe rust and a shifting of disease resistances and changing regional incidence. Given the prevalence of yellow spot resistant cultivars, growers are encouraged to check suspected infection as it may be physiological yellowing, herbicide damage, nutrition or other similar foliar diseases like nodorum or septoria tritici blotch. 

Generally, the priority disease for many WA wheat growers is powdery mildew given the recent prevalence of susceptible varieties. Leaf rust remains important in some regions and growers should note the considerable differences in resistance to new leaf rust strains between the varieties. Flag smut has been incorporated in Tables 2 to 6 due to the increase in prevalence in some regions and there are significant differences in susceptibility among wheat varieties. While the majority of registered seed dressings continue to prevent yield loss, the repeated cultivation of very susceptible varieties has meant that some growers may need to consider incorporating resistant varieties into their program as the disease continues to proliferate, possibly due to susceptible volunteer wheat and/or poor coverage of seed dressings. 

Interpretation of resistance classifications for cereal foliar diseases is provided on page 4, however the classifications are only a guide. Varieties with minimum ratings of MS for powdery mildew and leaf rust are recommended; this doesn’t mean the disease won’t impact yield, but it will be less often and the improved resistance allows for more time to manage the disease if present. 

Falling number index ratings are not available for newly released varieties. The ratings are still presented for existing varieties as some highrisk varieties have increased in popularity. Black point ratings from NVT are also presented. Both black point and falling number risk increase with wet spring conditions and with earlier sowing/ germination. 

Physical grain quality (screenings and hectolitre weight), while largely environmentally driven, differs significantly between varieties. The average screenings (% grain < 2mm) across all sites is presented for the 2024 NVT data. For hectolitre weight, the relative trend across all sites was 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Wheat<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


compared against the mean of all varieties and used to predict the varieties performance at a site mean of 74 kg/hl. For more details, please see page 39. 

## **AH and APW quick–mid season varieties** 

LRPB Vortex, Tomahawk CL Plus and LRPB Sonic (2024 NVT only) currently are the highest average yielding wheat varieties across all Agzones (Table 2). Comparing these varieties, Tomahawk CL Plus generally had superior performance across NVT sites with a site mean yield below 3.5 t/ha, while LRPB Vortex had superior performance across NVT sites with a site mean yield above 3.5 t/ha. Other varieties that exceed Scepter for average yield in NVT include Shotgun, Vixen, Calibre, Thumper, RGT Marsh and Brumby. With the exception of RGT Marsh (pending classification at the time of publication), the quality classifications of these varieties are presented in Table 2. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 13** 

Wheat 

Powdery mildew has been relatively prevalent in recent years, particularly given the large planting areas of Scepter (rated S). Brumby has the highest resistance rating of these varieties (R). Compared to Scepter, LRPB Vortex has a useful improvement in resistance (rated MS), while Calibre (MSS), LRPB Sonic (MSS _p_ ) and Shotgun (MSS _p_ ) are also expected to show a reduced rate of infection. Based on their resistance ratings, Tomahawk CL Plus (S), RGT Marsh (S _p_ ) and Thumper (S _p_ ) are expected to show a similar susceptibility as Scepter. 

The majority of high yielding varieties released in recent years have a yellow spot rating of MRMS. RGT Marsh is rated MSS which is similar to LRPB Anvil CL Plus and Cutlass as a reference (Table 17). There is a large range of ratings for leaf rust with LRPB Sonic an MRMS _p_ , while LRPB Vortex, Vixen and Brumby are rated SVS and Tomahawk CL Plus is rated S. 

Table 2 shows that for flag smut, the majority of leading wheat varieties in the quick to mid maturity group are susceptible. The other benefits of these susceptible varieties mean growers should manage flag smut through seed dressings rather than compromising yield potential through alternative, lower-yielding variety choices. RGT 

Marsh is the notable exception as a high yielding variety with a flag smut rating of MR _p_ , although it has an MSS yellow spot rating and is pending quality classification at the time of publication. 

Falling no. index ratings are not available for recent releases. Generally, the risk of low falling number is greater when early germination and variety maturity leads to maturation occurring earlier in spring and under wetter and cooler conditions. Varieties targeting April and early May germination are at a higher risk of pre-harvest sprouting and black point and growers should be particularly mindful of their varietal resistance for these scenarios. The current high yielding varieties are rated mostly S for black point, though LRPB Vortex and Shotgun did have higher black point incidence in two DPIRD time of sowing trials (similar to Thumper – SVS _p_ ). It would be recommended to avoid early sowing of these varieties in black point prone environments. 

Screenings comparison of 2024 NVT data show that Thumper had the highest risk of excess small grains in the leading quick to mid maturity varieties. Scepter, LRPB Sonic, Vixen and RGT Marsh were all better than average for screenings. Hectolitre weights indicate higher risks for Shotgun, Calibre, Thumper and Brumby.

**14 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

## **wheat Clearfield[®]** 

In the past, CL Plus varieties were not free to trade due to the Clearfield[®] licence agreements. This has recently changed allowing growerto-grower sales of seed. Various breeding companies may have specific conditions for grower-to-grower and it is recommended to check the conditions for your variety. 

The yield gap between Tomahawk CL Plus and all other CL Plus wheat varieties is around 10%. With the exception of Tomahawk CL Plus, most of the other leading CL Plus varieties have achieved around 99–102% of the NVT site mean, which is a considerable reduction compared to the leading non-Clearfield[®] wheats (Table 3). However, the differences in yield at environments with a yield potential below 2.5 t/ha have been less significant, representing scenarios where some of these alternative CL Plus wheats may have a fit. 

Powdery mildew resistance is low among CL Plus options, so proactive monitoring and management are advised. As with most current varieties, yellow spot is typically MRMS except for MSS ratings for both LRPB Anvil CL Plus and Razor CL Plus. Leaf rust ratings in this category are generally susceptible except Chief CL Plus (MR). Flag smut ratings show some resistant options in Razor CL Plus and Hammer CL Plus. 

Most CL Plus varieties were moderate risk for falling numbers with ratings of 4, representing a risk in high pressure environments. LRPB Anvil CL Plus and Valiant CL Plus have higher susceptibility for exhibiting low falling number. However, this is expected to be more significant for Valiant CL Plus which will be targeting earlier sowing which increases environmental risk, compared to LRPB Anvil CL Plus which will typically be used in later sowing and lower rainfall environments. Black point is weaker in Tomahawk CL Plus and LRPB Anvil CL Plus than Scepter, so avoiding early sowing in high-risk environments is recommended. 

Small grain screenings were better than average in all CL Plus varieties except Dozer CL Plus and Hammer CL Plus. Hectolitre weights were generally better than average except for Dozer CL Plus which was well below average. Tomahawk CL Plus at 73.8 kg/hl, is not statistically significant from the mean of 74.0 kg/hl or from Scepter at 74.1 kg/hl. 

_Note: There are NOW grower-to-grower sales permitted for Clearfield[®] varieties upon completion of a License Agreement but check with a variety’s seed distributor for clarification._

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 15** 

Wheat 

## **Australian Noodle Wheat (ANW)** 

Rottnest is the leading ANW variety for yield across the NVT datasets. In NVT yielding below 3.5 t/ha, AGT-Carnac and Rottnest yield similarly to Scepter and above other ANW varieties. At NVT sites yielding above 3.5 t/ha, Rottnest yields generally exceed AGT-Carnac and Scepter. Splendid yields competitively with AGT-Carnac in these environments (data not shown, refer to yield groups in NVT yield reporter). As Rottnest, AGT-Carnac and Splendid have only been tested in NVT for one season (2024), it is prudent to exercise some caution in interpreting their yield data and adopting these varieties until their performance has been proven in multiple seasons. 

There are limited options for powdery mildew resistance in the ANW varieties. Firefly with a provisional MSS rating, is marginally better that the other varieties. Generally, proactive monitoring and management of powdery mildew will be required for ANW varieties. Yellow spot ratings (provisional for Rottnest, AGT-Carnac and Splendid) are MRMS for all varieties except Kinsei (MS). Leaf rust susceptibility ranges from VS _p_ for Rottnest to MS for Kinsei, but AGT-Carnac, Splendid and Firefly are all equivalent to Scepter with an MSS rating. 

ANW wheats are generally resistant to flag smut with Zen rated as MS and all other varieties rated MR or better which provides useful resistance. AGT-Carnac has yet to be rated for flag smut resistance. 

The falling number index ratings for the ANW varieties were generally moderate. Zen was assessed to be a higher risk of low falling number, particularly with early sowing. There are no ratings for the newer ANW varieties. Firefly and Kinsei have weak ratings for black point compared to Ninja and Zen. As Rottnest, AGT-Carnac and Splendid were all included in the NVT for the first time in 2024, no black point ratings are available. 

Zen has been the gold standard for physical grain quality in NVT data since its release with very low screenings and high hectolitre weights. AGT-Carnac has the closest physical grain quality to Zen with low screenings and better than average test weight in 2024. Rottnest, Splendid and Firefly have screenings similar toor above Kinsei, while the hectolitre weight data show Firefly to have a much greater risk of low hectolitre weight. 

ANW growers have competitive yield options in Rottnest (and AGT-Carnac for environments yielding below 3.5 t/ha). The recent yield improvements mean that compared to Zen, the protein concentration of Rottnest was 0.6% lower and AGT-Carnac was 0.4% lower on average in main-season NVT data. Therefore, additional protein management may be required.

Wheat 

## **Mid–slow and slow maturity varieties in main and early season NVT** 

This section covers wheat varieties that exhibit a range of maturities from the mid maturity Brumby to the slower varieties such as Denison and Valiant CL Plus. Within the main season NVT, slower maturity wheats are often penalised in trials with later germination. However, some of the mid–slow maturity wheats remain competitive in the main season NVT and in these cases present useful options for reducing/managing frost risk without a predictable yield gap in unfrosted scenarios. Most of the varieties shown in this category have AH classification except for Brumby (APW), Denison (APW) and Kinsei (ANW). Brumby and Rockstar are the only varieties with APWN classification. 

Powdery mildew resistance varies with Brumby rated as R, AGT-Colt as MR _p_ and Rockstar and AGT-Hamelin as MSS or MSS _p_ . The remainder of the group have a rating of S or lower. Yellow spot ratings are MRMS (including provisional ratings) with Kinsei rated MS. Improved leaf rust resistance is available for early sowing options in AGTHamelin and AGT-Colt, both rated MR _p_ . Kinsei is rated MS for leaf rust while the remaining mid– slow or slow varieties are S, with Brumby SVS. Flag smut resistance ratings range considerably from VS in RockStar and SVS _p_ in Brumby and AGT-Hamelin, however Catapult, Kinsei, Denison, Genie and AGT-Colt are MR or better. 

Falling number and black point ratings are generally more important for early sowing/ germination scenarios as the crop is likely to flower and fill grain in wetter and cooler conditions, increasing the risk of sprouting and black point. Brumby, Catapult and Denison have higher falling number index ratings, while Rockstar and Valiant CL Plus are lower, representing a greater risk of downgrades due to low falling numbers. Black point ratings are not available for AGT-Hamelin and AGT-Colt. Catapult and Kinsei are rated S and care should be taken for early sowing in higher black point risk environments. 

Despite later flowering and grain development increasing exposure to terminal drought often causing smaller grains, AGT-Hamelin and Valiant CL Plus have well below average screenings across the main-season NVT data for 2024. Genie at the other extreme averaged 12% screenings, 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Wheat<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


well above all other varieties. Kinsei, Denison and AGT-Colt are above average for screenings but with earlier germination, this risk is typically lower. Hectolitre weights vary greatly among these varieties. AGT-Hamelin, Denison and Valiant CL Plus were all well above average, while Brumby and Rockstar were below average. 

Although only having one season of data, AGT-Hamelin has so far demonstrated strong yield performance, overall good disease risk profile and has excellent physical grain quality. AGT-Colt appears similar to Cutlass in maturity and yield patterns and physical grain quality but with much strong disease resistances. It will be important to investigate the falling number performance of these varieties given the suitability of these varieties for early sowing opportunities.

**18 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

## **Winter and slow season spring wheats in early season NVT** 

In WA since 2017 an early season wheat NVT series has evaluated the performance of slower maturing varieties when sown at an earlier sowing date (Table 6). 

Between 2020 and 2024 the early season series was generally sown in late April. A timing favourable to varieties with a mid–slow maturity but potentially not early enough for the very slow spring or winter varieties. Due to this reason and the smaller number of sites evaluated, hectolitre weight and screenings comparison have not been shown. 

Several new releases have generally improved the yield and agronomic traits in quick-winter types, with the latest of these being Wallaroo, an APW winter release in 2025 for WA. It is a quick winter with a robust disease package (Table 6). Wallaroo has been in the early season NVT since 2022 where overall it’s yields are similar to Mammoth (a very slow spring APW released in 2024) and competitive with the mid–slow and slow varieties. Table 13 shows the overall summary of the early season NVT’s from 2020 to 2024 where it can be seen that the longer season in 2022 benefitted Wallaroo compared to 2023 and 2024. 

Mammoth is a very slow spring variety, with a similar potential fit for very early sowing (late March to early April) to quick winter wheats, but it does not have the same vernalisation requirement (responding to an accumulation of cold temperatures). DPIRD data shows that flowering is quicker than Illabo at Mullewa and Merredin but can be similar at Katanning and Grass Patch. Despite its slower maturity, Mammoth has been competitive in yield with mid–slow varieties sown at the end of April in the early sown NVTs, outside Mammoth’s ideal sowing time. 

The suitability of winter wheats and slower spring varieties continues to be assessed; in general, they are more competitive when sown in early to mid-April in southern, longer season environments and/or at frost-prone locations. 

Winter wheats can provide options for farming system benefits from the extended vegetative phase which can allow more time for herbicide application and/or grazing which can provide an increase in profitability. Growers in higher rainfall areas have benefited from targeting paddocks at risk of poor trafficability in wetter seasons. The total impact on profit requires a farming system context, given other environmental considerations.

## **Grain yield Brenda Shackley and Dion Nicol (DPIRD)** 

The GRDC National Variety Trials (NVT) provide an independent assessment of crop variety performance in WA. NVT results can be viewed as individual site reports or as multi environment (MET) long-term summaries that provide insight into variety yield performance across environments and seasons. Tables 7 to 12 are outputs extracted from nvt.grdc.com.au and provide the MET data for the 6 Agzones in WA between 2020 and 2024. Table 12 represent a summary of the early season NVT. Where there is more than one year of data, or 4 or more observations, a 5-year weighted average has been calculated from the MET data, including the predicted yields for varieties that were absent at a site or in a season. Caution should be exercised when examining the weighted average as it can mask important variety-by-environment interactions (i.e. how a variety’s performance changes under different environmental conditions). 

Port-zone based summaries are now available online; they are not included in this guide. 

The overall performance of a variety within an Agzone/port zone does not necessarily capture the variation in relative yield performance of varieties in response to those environments. Major drivers in the relative performance of a variety include its maturity and germination timing, the amount and timing of rainfall and the occurrence of abiotic stressors such as drought, heat and frost damage. Growers are encouraged to consider the predominant environmental conditions experienced in any given season in their region when interpreting relative varietal performance in local NVTs. 

Visit app.nvt.grdc.com.au to access the NVT Yield Reporter.

## **Maturity** 

In WA, the flowering of spring wheat varieties is broadly classified into maturity categories of quick, quick–mid, mid, mid–slow and slow. While there are later maturing spring wheats and winter wheats, these are not commonly grown in WA. Most spring wheat varieties grown in WA have a minor vernalisation requirement (responding to an accumulation of cold temperatures) and photoperiod sensitivity (response to daylength), which means their development is mainly driven by temperature (warmer temperatures increase development rate). 

The quick–mid spring maturity type is the predominant maturity type in WA. Spring wheat varieties with a higher, albeit still limited, response to vernalisation (such as Magenta) or photoperiod (such as Cutlass) can be sown from late April as their maturity is delayed, and many of these 

varieties fall into the mid–slow and slow maturity class. In recent years, very slow spring (such as Mammoth) and quick winter wheats (such as Wallaroo, Mowhawk and Illabo) with greater adaptation to the WA environment have been released, offering unique maturity characteristics for very early sowing. 

Sowing spring wheats in April can result in an advanced rate of development (due to warmer temperatures and longer daylengths) and a quicker time to flowering. For this reason, winter wheats are seen as having more appropriate development times for an early April (or even March) sowing in WA, primarily due to their vernalisation requirement, which negates much of the warmer temperatures in April and May. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 27** 

Wheat

**Flowering (days before or after Scepter)** 

_**Figure 1: Flowering (days before or after Scepter) response of various maturity types sown on a range of sowing dates (mid-April to late May) average over 4 locations in 2023 and 2024**_ Source: DPIRD wheat agronomy research 

Flowering dates change with sowing date, location (Table 14) and from season to season (not shown), predominantly due to differences in temperatures, although stress can also have an impact (not shown). Figure 1 shows the large difference in flowering compared to Scepter between the quick to slow spring and winter wheats in WA (differences are greatest with an April sowing). Given that the genetic control of flowering is complex and it is driven by environmental conditions that differ from season to season, it is important to consider data from multiple sites and seasons to better understand the maturity type of a variety. 

Earlier sowing of mid–slow maturity types still result in an earlier flowering time than mid-May sowing of quick–mid maturity wheats. Risks associated with early flowering wheat, such as frost damage, low falling numbers or pre-harvest sprouting and black point, should be considered with a greater scrutiny if early sowing is planned with these maturity types. 

**FlowerPower:** is an online tool that predicts cereal flowering dates across the WA wheatbelt. FlowerPower enables the user to compare flowering date predictions over a range of sowing dates. Refer to the DPIRD website or https://fp.dpird.app 

Scepter is classified as quick–mid maturity and Table 14 outlines how other varieties compared to Scepter when sown mid to late May at the northern and south coast NVT sites and in DPIRD trials in 2024. A more detailed flowering comparison between Scepter and other varieties can be found in the variety snapshots (pages 43–56). 

Brumby and LRPB Vortex have the maturity category of provisional mid-season in this bulletin, as the duration of days to flowering has varied between Scepter and RockStar depending on the environment and season (Table 14). AGT-Carnac, Rottnest and Splendid are also mid _p_ or AGT-Colt and AGT-Hamelin are slow _p_ with only information from 2024.

## **Sowing time response of wheat varieties in WA** 

Matching varieties to their appropriate sowing date is the key to maximising potential wheat yield in WA. However, as the environmental constraints of each season differ in significance, prevalence and timing, the perfect match of sowing date and variety development is difficult to achieve. For example, many wheat growing areas in WA have had both tight, dry finishes and cool, long finishes over the past few years. These differing conditions alter the developmental timings of wheat crops and result in one maturity type being favoured over another for any given sowing date. Despite this, there are some consistencies that occur over several seasons that can guide appropriate variety choice for any sowing opportunity. 

Most of the main season wheat NVTs are germinated from mid-May onwards, a time best suited to the quicker maturity varieties that currently dominate WA’s wheat area. DPIRD’s research since 2020 has assessed the optimal variety choices for any given sowing date and the best match of variety maturity types to sowing opportunities in specific environments. 

Even at the vastly different locations of Mullewa, Merredin, Katanning and Grass Patch/Mt Burdett, peak yields generally occurred from a late April to early May sowing (Figure 2). However, the variety combinations to maximise yield from each sowing date at each site varied and there are opportunities to maintain high yields across the sowing window by matching varieties to a given sowing date. Table 15 provides the suggested sowing times of main season wheat varieties in WA.

**30 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

**Atypical seasons (not included in Figure 2):** In 2021, Merredin experienced an extreme frost event in the optimum flowering times, penalising all varieties. The atypical finish to the season in 2022 and 2024 at Mullewa, showed an improved performance of the mid–slow and even the quick winter varieties at the later sowing dates, while the tight/dry finish and no minor frost events in 2023 at Katanning showed an improved performance of the quick or quick–mid maturing varieties to yield higher than normal in late April. 

In addition to frost damage, a key factor in reduced yields of April sown wheat greatly depends on the degree of drought stress and the interaction of higher temperatures in April-May. These trials are irrigated to achieve a germination; in some conditions this may reduce the degree of crops ‘running-up’ from early season stress which results in very poor yield potential with low biomass. Cooler environments are less likely to experience this but are typically much higher risk of frost. 

The suitability of winter and very slow spring varieties is continually being assessed by DPIRD; in general, they are more competitive when sown in early to mid-April in southern, longer season environments and/or frost-prone locations. 

## **Suggested sowing times** 

Suggested sowing times for varieties (Table 15) have been developed to support decisions around preferences and opportunities in sowing times. The proposals are based on knowledge of the varieties and their performance in NVT and DPIRD agronomy trials (see _Sowing time response of wheat varieties in WA_ on page 30). The suggested sowing times were developed in consultation with breeding companies and researchers. For varieties not listed in the table, refer to the maturity class of the variety. 

_Note: spring wheats generally have a lower yield potential if sown before late April in WA._ 

_The number of days to flowering relative to Scepter is provided in the variety snapshots at the end of this guide._ 

Knowing the maturity length of a variety helps with planting order planning and enables variety development to be aligned with typical seasonal conditions. 

Table 15 does not suggest the sowing time of wheat varieties where frost may be an issue. Frost risk is extremely variable within the landscape and across environments. Delaying sowing time and the choice of variety or crop are still the most significant ways of reducing yield losses in frostprone areas. Key management strategies for frost are available on the GRDC and DPIRD websites.

## **Disease and pest resistance Manisha Shankar, Geoff Thomas, Carla Wilkinson, Sarah Collins and Daniel Huberli (DPIRD)** 

## **Key points** 

- Be aware of a variety’s disease package so that pre- and in-season disease management can be planned. 

- Do not plant a susceptible variety into a high disease risk paddock. 

- Use a diversity of wheat varieties and crop types. 

When selecting a wheat variety, it is important to consider its yield and potential quality grade along with the disease resistance (Table 17). Higher resistance ratings reduce disease severity and potential yield loss. Avoiding susceptible (S) or very susceptible (VS) varieties significantly reduces the chance of disease outbreaks and the need for in-season management. 

For a disease to become damaging in season, there needs to be: 

- the presence of inoculum, which is usually carried over from the previous season 

- favourable weather conditions for disease proliferation 

- a susceptible host crop that becomes infected. 

## **Depending on the disease in question, inoculum can be carried on infested stubble or trash, a green bridge, in seed or in the soil** (Table 16). 

Choose varieties for each paddock based on disease resistance/susceptibility and the disease risk of the paddock. Disease risk is related to the potential presence of disease inoculum and the favourability of the environment for the disease. For example, it is not advisable to sow Yitpi, which is rated SVS for yellow spot, onto wheat stubble. 

Knowing the disease strengths and weaknesses of a variety enables more effective disease management during the season. Varieties that are VS to S to powdery mildew are more likely to get the disease, and the rate of epidemic development will be faster and the impact will be greater in these types. When S varieties such as Vixen make up a high proportion of the crop grown, the disease will be more common and damaging across the grainbelt. Therefore, the need to respond with in-crop fungicide can be greater and the time to respond shorter with these varieties. Use of proactive protection, such as fertiliser applied fungicides, may be more useful for these varieties to delay or slow disease onset in favourable environments. 

Using a diverse range of varieties with different disease resistance traits reduces the risk that the whole farm will require disease management at the same time. Diversification also reduces the risk of new pathotypes emerging, which could render a significant proportion of a farm or region susceptible and require region-wide management responses. 

**Table 16: Examples of wheat diseases carried over from different inoculum sources** 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Inoculum carryover source Disease<br>Infested stubble or trash Yellow spot, septoria nodorum blotch, powdery mildew and crown rot<br>Green bridge Rusts, powdery mildew and viruses<br>Seed Loose smut and flag smut<br>Soilborne Root lesion nematode, cereal cyst nematodes, rhizoctonia root rot, take-all,<br>flag smut and common bunt<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**32 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

Disease ratings provided in this guide reflect the expected response to the most common or dominant pathotype or strain of a disease in WA. For most diseases, very little variability in response is evident between seasons or regions, but occasionally mutations or incursions of rusts can significantly change variety ratings. For example, leaf rust ratings in Table 17 are for pathotypes that entered WA in 2015 (104–1,3,4,6,7,8,10,12 +Lr37) and 2017 (104–1,3,4,5,7,9,10,12 +Lr37). 

Ratings for **powdery mildew** reflect expected resistance to the general mildew population; however, varietal response can differ on rare occasions when a more virulent isolate occurs. 

**Nodorum blotch** causes characteristic necrotic lesions on leaves and can also cause glume blotch, dark brown to black lesions or staining on the heads associated with infection. Varieties can differ in disease expression on foliage and heads, so in this guide variety rankings have been included for both these plant parts (Table 17). Susceptible varieties are more likely to suffer glume blotch in seasons where disease is present in the foliage and when weather favourable to disease occurs after head emergence. 

**Flag smut** is commonly seen in the medium and lower rainfall regions of the WA grainbelt. The incidence within paddocks and severity of infection seen in the 2024 season was significantly greater than normal. In the worst affected paddocks, some yield losses have occurred. The 2024 season will have contributed to increased inoculum for coming years. Varietal resistance and seed dressing application are the main management options for this disease with no in-season management options, however resistance ratings for flag smut in wheat varieties are no longer routinely assessed. In 2025 a DPIRD project with GRDC investment (DAW2506-007RTX _ NGN: Improving Management of Flag Smut in Wheat and Loose Smut in Barley for Western Australia) commenced with trials to update flag smut ratings on newer varieties. Ratings range from Catapult at RMR to RockStar the most susceptible at VS (Table 17). AGT-Hamelin, Brumby, Dale, Devil, LRPB Vortex, Shotgun, Sting, Tomahawk CL Plus and Vixen are SVS _p_ for flag smut, while AGT-Colt, Denison and RGT Marsh are MR _p_ . 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Wheat<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


_An example of severe flag smut observed at Merredin in 2024_ 

ANW varieties are generally MS or better for flag smut. Please refer to Table 17 for the full list now available. For 2026: 

- apply a fungicide seed dressing registered for smut and bunt control in wheat in all paddocks, particularly to susceptible varieties and those sown over wheat stubbles from 2024. Historical trial results suggest that registered products should effectively reduce infection risk, refer to labels. 

- Note that self-sown wheat of susceptible varieties will likely carry the infection and while unlikely to significantly affect yield, will prolong the soil-inoculum. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 33** 

Wheat 

## **Soilborne diseases and pests** 

Soilborne pathogens and nematode pests infect plant roots and impair their ability to take up water and nutrients. Environmental factors such as soil moisture, temperature and nutrient availability will determine the severity of disease development. The most prevalent root diseases or pests of wheat in WA are rhizoctonia bare patch, root lesion nematodes (RLN), fusarium crown rot and take-all. Less widespread are cereal cyst nematodes (CCN), common root rot and pythium root rot. 

Soilborne diseases and nematode pests are best managed by (i) identifying the pathogens or pests causing plant decline, (ii) use of crop and variety rotation, and (iii) applying chemical management where available. 

**Rhizoctonia bare patch** is probably the most prevalent and damaging soilborne disease in WA wheat, and there is no varietal resistance. For **fusarium crown rot** , there is limited resistance in varieties grown in WA. Reducing the build-up of infected cereal stubble and weeds is the best approach, through inclusion of broadleaf crops in the rotation and controlling grasses and cereal volunteers. 

_**Pratylenchus neglectus**_ is the dominant RLN species in broadacre growing areas of WA, followed by _**P. quasitereoides**_ (formerly _P. teres_ ). _**P. thornei**_ and _**P. penetrans**_ are detected less frequently but can also impact yields. The key to managing RLN is identifying paddocks with yieldlimiting levels and incorporating more resistant crops and varieties to reduce their populations. Wheat, barley and canola are all susceptible crops. These crops can suffer significant yield loss and can increase _P. neglectus_ and _P. quasitereoides_ levels in a paddock over a growing season. 

In this guide, _P. quasitereoides_ nematode resistance scores are from WA glasshouse and field trials. Ratings for _P. neglectus_ should be treated as indicative only, many varieties have not been tested in WA. Varieties with fewer than 5 observations, or where there has been no field trial verification of the glasshouse rating, receive provisional ratings. 

**CCN (** _**Heterodera australis**_ **)** is among the most damaging pests in WA soils and can cause significant yield losses in wheat, oats and barley. Canola and other broadleaf crops are not hosts and their yields are not affected by CCN. When the season breaks, CCN eggs hatch and juveniles enter plant roots where they set up a fixed feeding 



_Plant pathology group (from left to right): Karan Jaura, Manisha Shankar, Dorthe Jorgensen, Donna Foster and Ethan Ryan_ 

**34 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

site. They damage root tissues as they grow and impair plant function by extracting nutrients and water. These nematodes form into hardened cysts which protect hundreds of eggs over the summer period. CCN is most commonly found around Geraldton, Esperance and the Avon Valley but can occur elsewhere across the WA grainbelt. CCN can be effectively managed by including resistant cereal varieties and ensuring rotations include weed free, non-cereal crops. If levels of CCN are high, then 2 consecutive years of a non-host may be required. No chemical treatments are currently registered to control CCN. 

In-crop diagnosis of the disease or pest affecting plant roots is best achieved by sending affected plants to DPIRD Diagnostic and Laboratory Services (DDLS) in South Perth. For more information on sample submission contact 08 9368 3351 or DDLS@dpird.wa.gov.au . Alternatively, a DNA-based soil-testing service (PREDICTA[®] B) is also available and growers can contact their agronomist or SARDI for advice on how to submit samples for molecular testing. 

## **Fungicides** 

Fungicides can help control disease and limit yield impacts but a rapidly developing issue for the Australian grains industry is the development of fungicide resistance in a range of wheat and barley pathogens. Using integrated disease management (IDM), including varietal disease resistance, can help reduce reliance on fungicides for disease management. More information on fungicide resistance is available from the Australian Fungicide Resistance Extension Network ( https://afren.com.au/ )

## **Variety traits** 

## **Brenda Shackley, Dion Nicol and Jeremy Curry (DPIRD)** 

## **Coleoptile length and seeding depth** 

Seeding into moisture at a seeding depth of 2–4 cm is the preferred option in WA to ensure quick establishment and maintain yield potential. However, with expanding seeding programs and increased variability in the timing and amount of autumn rainfall, dry seeding has dramatically increased in WA. 

Avoid sowing deeper than 5 cm as this has the potential to delay and reduce emergence, causing weaker seedlings and an overall reduction in yield, even in varieties with a longer coleoptile (Table 18). 

Longer coleoptiles can increase establishment rate if seeding depth increases (Table 18). The ability to establish wheat crops from seed placed deeper in the soil can be useful in situations where the soil surface is dry, but the subsurface is moist. Coleoptile length is influenced by factors other than variety, including seed size and source, temperature, soil water, certain seed dressings, and the type of coleoptile length assessment, as shown in Table 18. Generally, there is more scope to adjust seeding equipment for deep sowing that can impact establishment to a greater degree than the often-minor differences in coleoptile length in the mainstream varieties. 

Varieties have inherently different coleoptile lengths. Table 21 provides a guide to the coleoptile group. Coleoptile groups are collectively identified as short (S), medium (M), long (L) or very long (VL). 

- Very long coleoptile wheats similar to Halberd are now in various breeding programs. 

- Varieties with long coleoptile lengths include Calibre, Cutlass, Magenta and Yitpi. Denison and Sting have a slightly shorter coleoptile, hence they are rated as M/L. 

- The impact of deep sowing on grain yield depends on growing season conditions and whether lower plant density and vigour can be compensated for through increases in other yield components such as tiller number, grains per ear and grain weight. 

- Increasing seeding rates can help partially reduce the yield penalty caused by reduced establishment with deeper sowing. 

- Not all seeding systems are equal for deep sowing, so ensure depth is monitored as conditions change. 

## **Physical grain quality** 

While low hectolitre weights (kg/hl) and high small grain screenings are largely environmentally driven and are typically within industry limits for WA, there are considerable differences in the risk profiles of individual varieties. In 2024 a higher proportion of trials and varieties in the WA NVT had hectolitre weights below 74kg/hl or screenings above 5 or 10% and summaries are presented in Tables 19 and 20.

## **Black point** 

Black point ratings in Table 21 are sourced from NVT disease ratings. Black point assessments are also routinely conducted in the DPIRD wheat agronomy trials where it has been observed that Shotgun (S _p_ ) and LRPB Vortex (S) had higher incidence of black point in 2024 than Thumper which is rated as SVS _p_ . Incidence of black point is more prevalent with earlier sowing; hence it is recommended to avoid sowing these varieties in April to early May, particularly in area of higher black point incidence. 

## **Falling number index** 

The falling number index (FNI) is a rating system that reflects the risk of a wheat variety exhibiting a low falling number at harvest (Table 21). The predominant cause of low falling number in wheat in Australia is pre-harvest sprouting, where mature grain begins to germinate in the paddock in response to rainfall. Enzymes produced during the germination process reduce the quality of the wheat for a range of end-uses, and the falling number test measures the activity of these enzymes. The falling number of a variety upon receival will be influenced by the wheat variety grown, stage of maturity, timing and intensity of seasonal rainfall. 

DPIRD has carried out research since 2013 to better understand the susceptibility of wheat varieties to low falling number, in response to both growing conditions and rainfall in the pre- and post-maturation period. 

**The FNI determines the risk of a variety exhibiting low falling number. On a 1–9 scale, the higher the rating the more likely a variety is to maintain falling number and the lower the risk of downgrade at grain delivery.** 

The pre-harvest sprouting (PHS) tolerance of Mace and Scepter (FNI of 5) has underpinned their widespread adoption across WA, even into areas of high PHS risk. With an FNI of 7, DS Pascal is considered the variety of lowest risk of PHS. Lower FNI ratings indicate varieties of higher risk of low falling number should harvest rainfall occur, with these varieties recommended for lower risk environments. The ratings were last updated with data from the 2022 season, for varieties including Calibre and Brumby (both rated 6 _p_ ), Mowhawk (5), Sting (4 _p_ ), and LRPB Anvil and Valiant CL Plus (both 2/3 _p_ ). 

**DPIRD research found crops that mature earlier (such as spring wheats sown in April) have a higher risk of low falling number and black point.** 

DPIRD’s current research is investigating the interactions between genetic resistance, environmental conditions, and maturity stage, to inform pre-harvest sprouting management. 

_Below: Jeremy Curry checking wheat heads to be tested for falling number_

## **Variety snapshots** 

## **Brenda Shackley, Jeremy Curry and Dion Nicol (DPIRD)** 

Variety snapshots are presented for 26 varieties in order of quality classification. 

Each snapshot includes a summary of the essential characteristics of a variety and its key strengths and weaknesses. Grain yields relative to Scepter for each year between 2020 and 2024 for each Agzone are presented (extracted from nvtonline.com.au ) and disease ratings are from Table 17. Comments on grain quality are directed to Tables 19 and 20. 

Flowering information is sourced from DPIRD experiments in 2020, 2021, 2022, 2023 and 2024, and from NVT sites when other data is not available. All information is presented relative to Scepter. 

Variety information including pedigree, seed licensee, seed trading restrictions and end point royalty (EPR) payable is sourced from breeding companies and Variety Central ( varietycentral.com.au ). 

If you are seeking information for any varieties not included in the snapshots, please consult varietycentral.com.au , nvt.grdc.com.au or the respective breeding company.

Wheat 

## **AGT-Colt**[A] 

## **AGT-Hamelin**[A] 

## **AH** 

## **Comments** 

AGT-Colt, released in 2025, is a potentially slow maturity AH. AGT-Colt has only been included in the main and early season NVT since 2024 where it has yielded approximately 89% of Scepter or 97% of Denison respectively. It has potentially the most robust leaf disease package for the mid–slow and slow maturity groups with an R for stripe rust, MR _p_ for stem rust, leaf rust and powdery mildew. Yellow spot is MRMS _p_ . AGT-Colt may display the ‘Mace yellows’ which is a physiological symptom not a disease. The 2024 NVT grain quality data suggests that AGT-Colt may have a higher risk of screenings, similiar to Kinsei but less than Denison. 

## **AH** 

## **Comments** 

AGT-Hamelin, released in 2025, is a potentially slow AH. It has only been included in the main and early season NVT since 2024, where it has yielded similar to RockStar or slightly higher in the earlier sowing NVT respectively. AGT-Hamelin potentially has a maturity between Catapult and Denison. Provisionally, AGT-Hamelin has a more robust disease package than RockStar, Catapult and Denison, with a higher leaf rust rating of MR _p_ and MSS _p_ to powdery mildew. Other ratings are MRMS _p_ for stem rust and yellow spot, and MR for stripe rust. The 2024 NVT grain quality data suggests that AGT-Hamelin has one of the better grain packages similiar to Valiant CL Plus.

Wheat 

## **Calibre**[A] 

## **AH** 

## **Comments** 

Calibre was the first Scepter cross released by AGT in 2021. It is a quick–mid maturing AH variety with a longer coleoptile than its parent Scepter (similar to Cutlass). Calibre has been included in the NVT since 2020, yielding similarly to or slightly higher than Scepter, and competitive with Vixen, RockStar, Devil and the new releases such as Tomahawk CL Plus, Thumper, LRPB Vortex, RGT Marsh and Shotgun across the various Agzones. Calibre is MSS to powdery mildew, which is higher than Vixen and Devil, while LRPB Vortex is MS and Brumby is R, the highest rating among the quick–mid and mid maturity groups. Preliminary data indicates that Calibre has a slightly higher rating than Scepter for pre-harvest sprouting.

## **Cata ult**[A] **p AH** 

## **Comments** 

Catapult is a mid–slow maturity AH variety released by AGT in 2019. Catapult yieldes slightly higher than Denison, but lower than Scepter, RockStar and Brumby in the main season trials. In the early season NVT, Catapult is lower yielding than Denison, but similiar to Valiant CL Plus, Cutlass and Kinsei. Catapult is rated S to leaf rust and powdery mildew. With a falling number rating of 6, Catapult has a lower risk of pre-harvest sprouting. In good growing conditions, Catapult can exhibit a speckling on the leaves or what has previously been known as ‘Mace yellows’. This is not a disease but a physiological response, which typically has no effect on yield.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 45** 

Wheat 

**Devil**[A] **AH (N)** 

## **Comments** 

Devil is a quick–mid maturity AH (N) variety which was released in 2018. Devil yields similar to Scepter. Devil has been recently downgraded to S for stem rust and remains SVS to the latest leaf rust pathotype and powdery mildew. DPIRD trials suggest that Devil has different maturity triggers to Scepter, resulting in earlier flowering when sown in April or in the northern regions. Devil has a falling number rating of 3, so is not recommended for areas prone to pre-harvest sprouting. 

## **Hammer CL Plus**[A] 

## **AH (N)** 

## **Comments** 

Hammer CL Plus is an AH (N) variety that is tolerant to imidazolinone (IMI) and was released in 2020 by AGT. Hammer CL Plus has been included in the WA NVT since 2020, where it yielded 6–7% lower than Scepter depending on the Agzone. The recently released Tomahawk CL Plus is now the highest yielding IMI-tolerant variety, yielding on average 11% higher than Hammer CL Plus. Both are similiar in maturity but Tomahawk CL Plus is an APW. Hammer CL Plus is closely related to Mace, with a similar maturity. A disadvantage with Hammer is that it may be prone to lodging compared to other IMI-tolerant varieties. Hammer CL Plus is rated RMR for stripe rust, MR for stem rust, and S for leaf rust and powdery mildew. It is registered for label rate applications of Intervix[®] herbicide. 

_Note: There are now grower-to-grower sales permitted for CL Plus varieties._

Wheat 

## **LRPB Anvil**[A] **CL Plus** 

## **AH** 

## **Comments** 

LPRB Anvil CL Plus was released by LongReach in 2022, as a quick maturity AH IMI-tolerant variety. It has been included in the WA NVT since 2020, previously achieving the highest yields of the IMI-tolerant varieties until the latest release of Tomahawk CL Plus in 2023, which yields approximately 10% higher. It is lower yielding than Scepter, particularly in the soft finish of 2022. LRPB Anvil CL Plus appears more suited to the terminal drought conditions in the low to medium rainfall areas of WA. LRPB Anvil CL Plus has a similar stem and stripe rust profile to Tomahawk CL Plus and Hammer CL Plus, but is weaker for leaf rust and yellow spot. LRPB Anvil CL Plus is rated S for black point and has a falling number index rating of 2/3 _p_ , so is at risk of grain being downgraded if the conditions are conducive for black point or sprouting. The 2024 NVT grain quality data indicates that LRPB Anvil CL Plus has a low risk of screenings. Registered for label rate applications of Intervix[®] herbicide. _Note: Grower-to-grower sales are permitted for CL Plus varieties, please contact the seed distributor for further information_ .

## **LRPB Sonic**[A] 

## **AH** 

## **Comments** 

LRPB Sonic was released by LongReach in 2025 with 2024 the first year in NVT. In 2024 LRPB Sonic has performed similarly to the highest overall yielding varieties LRPB Vortex and Tomahawk CL Plus, but the limited results need to be reviewed with the addition of the 2025 results. LRPB Sonic is potential a quick–mid maturity, slightly quicker than Scepter. It has a slightly weaker resistance to stem rust (MS _p_ ) than other varieties in the maturity group however it provisionally has the highest leaf rust resistance (MRMS _p_ ) and an improved powdery mildew resistance (MSS _p_ ). The 2024 NVT grain quality data indicates that LRPB Sonic has potential better grain quality than LRPB Vortex and Tomahawk CL Plus.

## **RGT Marsh**[A] 

## **AH (pending classification)** 

## **Comments** 

RGT Marsh was released in 2025 by RAGT Australia with its classification as AH pending until 2026. RGT Marsh has been in NVT since 2023 where it has yielded similar to Scepter, Calibre, Devil, Thumper, Brumby and Vixen. RGT Marsh is a potential quick– mid maturity. RGT Marsh has a similar provisional disease package to Scepter, except for a slightly lower yellow spot rating of MSS. New data suggests a provisional rating of MR _p_ for flag smut. The 2024 NVT grain quality data indicates that RGT Marsh has potentially a similar grain quality profile to Scepter.

## **RockStar**[A] 

## **AH (N)** 

## **Comments** 

RockStar is a mid–slow AH (N) variety released in 2019 by InterGrain. It has yielded similar to Scepter and higher than other mid–slow or slow alternatives such as Catapult, Cutlass, Denison, Kinsei and Valiant CL Plus in main season NVT. RockStar yields approximately 4% lower than Denison in the early season NVT. RockStar has different maturity triggers from other mid–slow or slow varieties, such as Denison; caution is recommended if sown in April. RockStar is rated MRMS to _P. neglectus_ but S to leaf rust and VS to flag smut. RockStar has a falling number rating of 2, so a higher risk to pre-harvest sprouting, particularly with earlier sowing. 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

**48 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Wheat 

## **Sce ter**[A] **p AH** 

## **Comments** 

Scepter, released in 2015, remains the yield benchmark in WA NVT, although it is similar in yield to Calibre and Devil, and slightly lower in yield than Vixen in Agzones 4 and 5. LRPB Vortex and Tomahawk CL Plus have had 3 years in NVT, where they have averaged 4% higher than Scepter. Scepter is rated MSS to the latest strain of leaf rust, which is an advantage over Tomahawk CL Plus, Calibre, Devil, Vixen, LRPB Vortex and Sting, which are rated S to SVS. Scepter has a similar pre-harvest sprouting resistance to Calibre (5 vs 6 _p_ ), but its powdery mildew rating is S compared to MSS for Calibre and Shotgun, MS for LRPB Vortex and R for Brumby. Due to a consistent increase in yield, grain protein is on average lower for this variety and additional nitrogen may benefit yield and protein performance.

## **Shot un**[A] **g AH** 

## **Comments** 

Shotgun is a quick–mid AH variety released in 2024 by AGT. Shotgun was included in only half of the NVT sites for the first time in 2023 and then all NVT sites in 2024, where overall it is yielding 2% higher than Scepter but 2% lower than LRPB Vortex and Tomahawk CL Plus. Shotgun’s disease package is similar to Scepter, with MRMS for stem rust and yellow spot, RMR for stripe rust and MSS for leaf rust. Shotgun is an MSS _p_ for powdery mildew compared to S for Scepter and it has a better resistance to _P. neglectus_ and CCN than Scepter. Shotgun is S _p_ for black point with issues noted in the early sown DPIRD’s trials at Mullewa and Katanning. New flag smut rating are a provisional SVS _p_ . The 2024 NVT grain quality data indicates that Shotgun has similar grain quality profile to Calibre, where lower hectolitre weights may be an issue.

Wheat 

## **Thum er**[A] **p AH** 

## **Comments** 

Thumper is an AH wheat variety released by InterGrain in 2023. It is a quick–mid maturity which was included in the NVT for the first time in 2023 where it has yielded similarly to Scepter depending on the Agzone. Thumper has one of the highest leaf rust rating, MSS, in its maturity group, but only MS for stem rust and MRMS for yellow spot, and S _p_ for powdery mildew. Stripe rust is RMR but it is rated SVS _p_ for black point so recommended to avoid sowing early and areas prone to black point. Thumper also has a new provsional rating of S _p_ for flag smut. The 2024 NVT grain quality data indicates that Thumper has a high risk of screenings and achieving lower hectolitre weights.

## **Vixen**[A] 

## **AH (N)** 

## **Comments** 

Vixen is a quick maturity AH (N) variety released in 2018 by InterGrain. Yields are higher than Scepter in Agzones 4 and 5 and slightly lower in Agzone 6. Despite its quick maturity,Vixen is a very competitive variety; however, it performs better with later sowing and terminal drought compared to the softer finish in 2022. Vixen is rated MRMS to stem and MR to stripe rust but SVS to the latest strain of leaf rust, powdery mildew and flag smut. Vixen has a falling number rating of 3, so it is not recommended for areas prone to pre-harvest sprouting.
