## **Brumb y**[A] 

**APW (N)** 

## **Comments** 

Brumby is an APW (N) variety released by InterGrain in 2022. Brumby was included in the WA NVT for the first time in 2021, where it yields similar to RockStar and Scepter. Brumby’s maturity has varied over the years tested, from a mid–slow maturity similar to RockStar to a mid in 2023. A key attribute of Brumby is its powdery mildew resistance of R*, with ratings of RMR for stripe rust, MR for stem rust but SVS for leaf rust. Brumby currently has a provisional rating of 6 _p_ for pre-harvest sprouting, similiar to Catapult, which has the highest rating for the more recently released wheat varieties grown in WA. Brumby has a new provisional rating of SVS _p_ for flag smut.

## **Denison**[A] 

## **APW** 

## **Comments** 

Denison is a slow maturing APW variety released by AGT in 2020. In the main season NVT, Denison yields about the same to 11% lower than Scepter, depending on the Agzone and season, and slightly lower than mid–slow varieties like Catapult. Overall Denison is the highest yielding varieties in the early season NVT, except in Agzone 4. Denison is rated S to leaf rust and powdery mildew. Denison has a good pre-harvest sprouting rating compared to Cutlass but may be at more risk to screenings so avoid sowing later in May.

Wheat 

## **Dozer CL Plus**[A] 

## **APW (N)** 

## **Comments** 

Dozer CL Plus was released by InterGrain in 2023 as a quick–mid APW (N) IMI-tolerant variety. It was included in selected NVTs in 2021 and later in the 2023 and 2024 NVT, where it has yielded approximately 5% lower than Scepter and 9% lower than Tomahawk CL Plus. Dozer has a similar leaf rust rating to Tomahawk CL Plus (S) but it has lower ratings for stem rust (MS), stripe rust (MRMS) and powery mildew (S) than many of the recent IMI-tolerant wheat releases. Dozer is registered for label rate applications of Intervix[®] herbicide. 

_Note: There are now grower-to-grower sales permitted for CL Plus varieties._

## **LRPB Vortex**[A] 

## **APW** 

## **Comments** 

LRPB Vortex was released by LongReach in 2024. It is an APW with a different genetic background from Scepter. DPIRD data suggests that LRPB Vortex is mid maturity, slightly longer than Scepter, however yield performance does not necessarily support early sowing with best NVT results in later May with high yield potential. LRPB Vortex has been included in the WA NVT since 2022, where it is now the highest yielding alongside Tomahawk CL Plus. LRPB Vortex tends to do better in higher yielding environments than Tomahawk CL Plus. Disease ratings are SVS for leaf rust and flag smut, RMR for stripe rust and MRMS for stem rust and yellow spot and MS for powdery mildew.

Wheat 

## **Tomahawk CL Plus**[A] 

## **APW** 

## **Comments** 

Tomahawk CL Plus is an APW variety released by AGT in 2023. It is closely related to Scepter with a similar maturity, but with the additional benefit of the option to use Clearfield[®] Intervix[®] technology in crop or to manage the Imidazolinone herbicide soil residues from previous crops. Tomahawk CL Plus was included in the WA NVT for the first time in 2022 where overall it is the highest yielding variety along side LRPB Vortex. Yield trends suggest that Tomahawk CL Plus out yields LRPB Vortex in environments with the potential of less than 3.5t/ha. Tomahawk CL Plus is rated S for leaf rust, compared to Scepter at MSS, is similar for powdery mildew (S), stripe rust (RMR) and yellow spot (MRMS) but higher for stem rust (MR) and a lower provisional flag smut rating of SVS _p_ . _Note: There are now grower-to-grower sales permitted for CL Plus varieties._

## **AGT-Carnac**[A] 

## **ANW** 

## **Comments** 

AGT-Carnac was released by AGT in 2025, AGT’s second ANW variety. AGT-Carnac has only been included in the NVT since 2024, yielding similar to Scepter and slightly less than Rottnest which AGT released in 2024. Flowering scores from NVT indicate that AGT-Carnac and Rottnest may have similar maturity in between Ninja and Zen. AGT-Carnac has a provisionally more robust disease package than Rottnest with MRMS _p_ for stem rust compared to S _p_ , and MSS _p_ for leaf rust compared to VS _p_ and S _p_ compared to SVS _p_ for powdery mildew. AGT-Carnac was also found to have less issues with screenings and hectolitre weights in 2024 compared to other ANW varieties (except Zen).

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 53** 

Wheat 

## **Firefly**[A] 

## **ANW** 

## **Comments** 

Firefly was released in 2023, one of InterGrain’s latest ANW release since Kinsei in 2018. Firefly was included in the 2021, 2023 and 2024 WA NVT where it has yielded overall similar to Ninja. Firefly has mid–slow maturity, between Zen and Kinsei. Physical grain quality parameters in NVT have shown much greater risk of low hectolitre weights and higher screenings than Zen which has a strong grain quality package.

## **Kinsei**[A] 

## **ANW** 

## **Comments** 

Kinsei is a mid–slow maturity noodle wheat released by InterGrain in 2018. It is well suited to early sowing opportunities and has also performed well in the NVT main season plantings, particularly in Agzone 3. Overall Kinsei yields slightly less than Ninja, and slightly higher than Zen. Kinsei is now outyielded by most of the new ANW releases, particularly Rottnest. Kinsei is rated S for black point, which may be an issue if sowing the variety in April. Kinsei’s disease ratings are marginally better than Ninja and Zen.

## **Ninja**[A] **ANW** 

## **Comments** 

Ninja is a noodle wheat variety released by InterGrain in 2016 that has a Calingiri and Wyalkatchem background. Ninja has been the highest yielding ANW variety, but is likely to be replaced in the future with the release of varieties such as Rottnest and AGT-Carnac. Ninja is rated S to stem rust, powdery mildew and the new incursion of leaf rust. It is rated MRMS to black point.

## **Rottnest**[A] 

## **ANW** 

## **Comments** 

Rottnest was released in 2024 and it is AGT’s first ANW variety. Rottnest has only been included in NVT since 2024 so the preliminary results need to be treated with caution. In 2024 Rottnest did become the highest yielding ANW variety, outyielding Ninja by 4%. DPIRD data suggests that Rottnest has a maturity similar to Ninja. Rottnest is S _p_ for stem rust, VS _p_ for leaf rust and SVS _p_ for powdery mildew so will require a more active leaf disease monitoring program than the other ANW varieties. The 2024 NVT grain quality data suggests that Rottnest may have more screenings issues than the other ANW varieties.

**56 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Barley** 

## **Introduction** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

This guide supports barley variety selection for Western Australian (WA) growers by providing market insights, grain yield and quality comparisons, disease resistance ratings, and agronomic profiles. It covers malt barley varieties currently segregated in WA, those in Stage 2 of malt accreditation with Grains Australia, and selected feed varieties deliverable to the WA bulk handling system (Tables 1–16 and Figures 1–8). 

Australia remains a key player in the global malt barley trade, supplying 30–40% of total exports, with China as its largest customer. However, declining beer consumption in mature markets and rising competition from Chinese malt exports, often made from WA Fair Average Quality (FAQ) barley, are compressing malt premiums and challenging profitability. 

Demand for premium, single-variety malt barley is growing in Africa and Latin America. Countries such as Mexico, Peru, and Ecuador exclusively purchase MALT1-grade barley, avoiding FAQand feed-grade barley. Chile and Brazil have expressed interest in WA malt barley. However, trade constraints currently limit grain exports, prompting Brazilian brewers to import processed Australian malt instead. These markets help sustain the malt–feed price spread, supporting the profitability of WA growers. 

Despite beer premiumisation trends, malt premiums remain low due to declining beer consumption in mature markets. In China, overall beer production is declining, while demand for premium beer is increasing. Chinese malt exports are increasing, driven by lower domestic consumption and reduced production costs, challenging Australian malt competitiveness. Much of this malt is produced from WA-sourced FAQ barley, including out-of-spec Maximus CL (barley that failed to meet MALT1 specifications). 

In 2024, WA growers delivered 32 barley varieties, however, only Maximus CL was planted across all 4 port zones, representing two-thirds of total plantings and 80% of MALT1 stack allocations. In 2025, its dominance has slightly declined as growers explore alternatives. However, limited varietal diversity and low availability of barley suitable for processing-aid-free malting continue to constrain global demand. 

The 2026–27 season is pivotal for WA’s malting barley industry. Persistently low malt premiums are influencing growers, particularly in Esperance, to favour feed varieties or prioritise volume over quality. In favourable seasons, some feed barley may meet MALT1 specs and be delivered to costeffective stacks, reflecting a pragmatic response to market pressures. 

Premium beer producers in Southeast Asia and Japan are seeking barley suitable for malting without the use of processing aids. This demand presents opportunities for WA malt processors, though varietal limitations, particularly the dominance of Maximus CL, restrict market access. Expanding varietal options is essential to meet quality requirements in these regions. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 57** 

Barley 

Deciding whether to grow barley for malt or feed classification still depends on 5 main factors: 

1. Premiums paid for segregated varieties. 

2. Relative grain yield of malt and feed-grade barley varieties. 

3. Differences in input cost based on agronomic and disease traits. 

4. Likelihood of meeting malt barley receival specifications with a malt variety. 

5. Location of receival segregations for malt barley varieties. 

The decision to sow wheat or even oats instead of barley depends on: 

1. The price spread between wheat, oats and barley. 

2. Relative grain yield of malt barley, feed barley, milling oats and wheat. 

3. Availability of premiums for malt-grade barley. 



Western Australia’s BFED1 receival standards prioritise hectolitre weight, with a minimum requirement of 56 kilograms per hectolitre (kg/hL). This contrasts with eastern Australia, where GTA Barley1 (feed barley) standards require both a minimum hectolitre weight of 62.5 kg/hL and screenings below 15% (2.2 mm sieve). As a result, WA feed barley systems can focus on maximising yield, allowing growers to select the highest-yielding variety regardless of malt accreditation or segregation. 

High-yield feed barley production typically involves sowing between mid-April and midMay at a density of 180–220 plants per square metre (plants/m²), supported by tailored nutrition, herbicide, and fungicide programs based on site conditions and varietal characteristics. 

The Australian barley industry is committed to maintaining the integrity of malt barley delivered to end users. Co-binning of segregated malt varieties is not permitted, even when varieties share similar agronomic traits. Intentional contamination of malt stacks is prohibited, and accurate variety declaration is legally required under the _Plant Breeders Rights Act 1994_ and the _Bulk Handling Act 1967_ . Growers should avoid mixing visually similar varieties (for example, La Trobe, Maximus CL, Spartacus CL). To support varietal integrity, machine learning-based identification is being progressively introduced at bulk-handling sites. 

Growers targeting MALT1 should consult with domestic processors and traders before sowing to confirm the availability of segregations and potential premiums. Recommended grain specifications include: 

- **Protein:** 10.3–10.8% (domestic), 10.5–11.0% (export) 

- **Retention:** at least 80% on a 2.5 mm sieve 

- **Hectolitre weight:** greater than 64 kg/hL 

- **Contaminants:** Ryegrass ergot less than 3 cm, no whole snails 

- **Harvest management:** Glyphosate must not be used as a desiccant 

Barley varieties vary in agronomic suitability across WA, and market demand differs by port zone due to the distinct domestic and international markets each zone serves. Selecting the right variety requires balancing farm business objectives with customer requirements, making strategic planning essential. 

**58 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Barley variety choice in 2026 – what should I grow?** 

Variety choice in WA is influenced by multiple agronomic and market factors, often in combination: 

- **Soil pH:** in soils with pHCa below 4.5, varieties with improved tolerance to acidity and aluminium toxicity, such as Buff, Kirwan CL, and Litmus, perform best. Kirwan CL is a higher-yielding option than Buff and Litmus with the added advantage of tolerance to imidazolinone residues and herbicides. 

- **Yield potential:** 

   - in low-yielding (less than 3 t/ha) non-acidic environments, Beast sets the benchmark. 

   - in high-yielding (greater than 5 t/ha) environments, Combat, Cyclops, and Neo CL are leading performers. 

- **Herbicide tolerance:** since the release of Scope CL in 2011, herbicide-tolerant barley varieties have grown in popularity, accounting for 84% of barley hectares in the 2025 season. Barley varieties now offer 3 herbicide tolerance groups, enabling flexible weed control strategies: 

   - imidazolinone: Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, Granite CL, Kirwan CL, Maximus CL, Neo CL, Rocket CL, Scope CL, Soldier CL, Spartacus CL, Zena CL. 

   - quizalofop-p-ethyl (as Aggressor[®] AX): PegasusAX, Titan AX. 

   - dual tolerance (imidazolinone and quizalofop-p-ethyl): AGT-Bunyip IA. 

- **Disease risk:** disease pressure varies by region and is subject to change over time. Resistance ratings for scald (Sc), net-form net blotch (NFNB), spot-form net blotch (SFNB), and powdery mildew (PM) may vary regionally due to local pathogen populations and the development of fungicide resistance. Growers observing unexpected disease levels in varieties rated moderately resistant to moderately susceptible (MRMS), moderately resistant (MR), or resistant (R) should report cases to DPIRD pathologists and, where appropriate, submit samples for pathotype and resistance testing. 

Refer to Tables 1–2 and 14–16 for current WA resistance data. Note that field observations for Sc and PM may differ from the listed ratings due to the emergence of new pathotypes. Varieties with variable field reactions are marked with an asterisk (#) following their resistance score in Tables 1–2 and 14–15. 

- **Malt premiums and availability of segregations:** with subdued malt premiums and limited segregations for varieties other than Maximus CL, many growers are opting for feed-only varieties or prioritising yield. However, in favourable seasons, feedmanaged crops of malt-accredited varieties may meet MALT1 specifications and be delivered if there is a receival point close enough. Although malt-approved varieties still dominate WA barley hectares, the release of high-performing feed-only options is shifting grower preferences, especially in the Esperance Port Zone. 

In 2025, Maximus CL dominated WA barley plantings, accounting for two-thirds of sown hectares. Its dominance has slightly declined in 2025, with growers exploring alternatives such as Combat and Neo CL, as well as Bigfoot CL, Cyclops, Granite CL, Laperouse, and PegasusAX to a lesser extent. By 2026, 5 new varieties, AGTBunyip IA, Ember, Kirwan CL, Rocket CL, and Soldier CL, will be available, and the varietal mix is expected to evolve rapidly. 

This sowing guide outlines the agronomic and market characteristics of current malt barley varieties, emerging options, and selected legacy lines. Detailed varietal profiles are available in the Variety Snapshot section, with commentary on new releases provided in the ‘New for planting in 2026’ section. Market insights from the Grain Industry Association of Western Australia (GIWA) are summarised in the ‘Market feedback’ section. Ultimately, variety choice should align with your agronomic conditions, market access, and business goals. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 59** 

Barley 

Figure 1 presents changes in the popularity of barley varieties across WA from 2024 to 2025, based on available data. Tables 1 and 2 highlight key attributes of widely grown varieties (Table 1) and those likely to gain traction in WA (Table 2), supporting informed variety selection for both agronomic fit and market access. More varietyspecific information can be found in Tables 4–16 and Figures 2–8. 

## **Geraldton Port Zone** 

The Geraldton Port Zone is primarily focused on exporting barley as grain, with no malt segregations planned for this region. According to GIWA’s July estimates (2015–25), the zone accounts for a median of 90,000 hectares, representing approximately 6% of Western Australia’s total barley area. 

In 2025, barley production in Geraldton was concentrated among 5 key varieties: Maximus CL, Litmus, Spartacus CL, Neo CL, and Yagan. These varieties accounted for 83% of the total barley area. Maximus CL dominated plantings, with an area nearly 7 times larger than that of Spartacus CL. Together, Maximus CL and Spartacus CL made up over half of the zone’s barley plantings. Imidazolinone-tolerant varieties — including Maximus CL, Spartacus CL, Neo CL, Commodus CL, and Scope CL, occupied 69% of the total area, underscoring the continued importance of imidazolinone-tolerant genetics in the region. 

In contrast, acid-tolerant varieties such as Litmus and Buff accounted for 16%, while the CoAXium[®] variety Titan AX maintained a modest presence with just over 2% of the area planted to it. Maximus CL and Neo CL both gained ground significantly in 2025. Meanwhile, Scope CL and Spartacus CL saw the most notable declines in area as grower preferences shift. 

Due to the region’s relatively low production base, the barley industry has opted not to recommend a malt barley segregation for the 2026–27 harvest. Maximus CL is the only malt-accredited variety grown in sufficient volume to justify segregation. Growers interested in pursuing malt premiums are advised to either establish relationships with domestic malt processors or deliver grain meeting MALT1 specifications to upcountry receival sites in the Kwinana-North (Midlands) Port Zone. 

Maximus CL is expected to remain the leading variety in Geraldton in 2026, while Spartacus CL is expected to continue its decline. Scope CL and Yagan remain viable options. In response to local agronomic conditions, alternative strategies are being trialled, including early sowing of Neo CL under a feed-barley management approach, despite its malt accreditation. Neo CL may be suitable for areas where Scope CL has performed well previously. Beast, a feed-only variety, offers a reliable option for low-rainfall environments. Kirwan CL, a new feed barley with both imidazolinone and acid tolerance, is likely

to be valuable on acid sands. Until its adoption increases, Buff and Litmus will continue to play a key role in managing acidic soils across the port zone. 

## **Kwinana Port Zone** 

The Kwinana Port Zone plays a central role in WA’s value-added barley supply chain. Most of the grain delivered in this zone is either processed in Perth and exported as malt or shipped as grain for shochu production in Japan. Only a small proportion is exported as grain to Africa, Southeast Asia, and Latin America. This export pattern reflects the zone’s strong alignment with specialised market channels and domestic processing infrastructure. 

According to GIWA’s July estimates (2015–25), the Kwinana Port Zone accounted for a median of 555,000 hectares, representing approximately 35% of the state’s total barley area. In 2025, the top 5 barley varieties, Maximus CL, Neo CL, Commodus CL, Titan AX, and Buff, accounted for 89% of total barley plantings in the zone. Maximus CL was the dominant variety, planted on nearly 70% of barley hectares, making it 7 times more prevalent than Neo CL. Several varieties saw significant declines in 2025. RGT Planet experienced an 80% decline in planted area compared to the previous year, reflecting a waning interest among growers. Buff and Spartacus CL also showed substantial declines in area planted. Neo CL was the standout, gaining significant traction among growers in 2025. 

In terms of trait groups, imidazolinone-tolerant varieties, including Maximus CL, Neo CL, Commodus CL, Spartacus CL, Scope CL, Bigfoot CL, and Zena CL, accounted for 85% of the barley area. CoAXium[®] -tolerant varieties, Titan AX and PegasusAX, made up 3%. Acid-tolerant varieties, Buff and Litmus, accounted for 4%. 

Looking ahead to 2026, Maximus CL is expected to remain the most widely planted variety, although its dominance may slightly decline. Neo CL is projected to gain significant ground as Spartacus CL and RGT Planet are phased out, with 2025 likely marking their final season for most growers. The varietal mix will remain diverse, incorporating Buff, Combat, Commodus CL, Laperouse, Litmus, and Titan AX, alongside the adoption of new feed lines such as Bigfoot CL, Granite CL, Kirwan CL, and PegasusAX. 

AGT-Bunyip IA is the first barley variety with dual tolerance to imidazolinone and Aggressor[®] AX (quizalofop-p-ethyl), enabling broader herbicide rotation. Its ability to control barley and brome grass while enabling herbicide group rotation is a key driver behind its development and adoption. These new lines offer growers greater flexibility in managing weed resistance and optimising crop performance. 

## **Albany Port Zone** 

The Albany Port Zone presents a strong market opportunity for barley, both as grain and as malt through domestic maltsters. While most grain delivered in this zone is exported directly, a portion is transported to Perth for malting before being shipped overseas. This dual pathway highlights Albany’s strategic role in supporting both bulk grain exports and domestic value-added processing. 

According to GIWA’s July estimates (2015–25), the Albany Port Zone accounts for a median of 600,000 hectares, representing 38% of Western Australia’s total barley area. In 2025, production was dominated by 5 varieties, Maximus CL, Neo CL, Laperouse, Combat, and Titan AX, which accounted for 89% of plantings. Maximus CL led with nearly two-thirds of the area, 3 times more than Neo CL. However, growers reduced their plantings of Maximus CL, RGT Planet, and Spartacus CL, while significantly increasing their plantings of Neo CL. Imidazolinone-tolerant varieties, including Maximus CL, Neo CL, Spartacus CL, Commodus CL, Zena CL, and Scope CL, comprised 84% of the total area, highlighting their regional importance. CoAXium[®] varieties, Titan AX and PegasusAX, made up 3%. Acid-tolerant types, Buff and Litmus, were scarcely planted. 

Looking ahead to 2026, Maximus CL is expected to remain the most widely grown variety, although its dominance is projected to decline. Combat, Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL are anticipated to rise in popularity. Neo CL will replace RGT Planet entirely. Changing pathogen virulence may influence some planting decisions, especially where Sc and PM are increasing in their prevalence. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 61** 

Barley 

In the Albany Port Zone, specific subregions favour barley varieties tolerant to Aggressor[®] AX herbicide, creating strong opportunities for herbicide-tolerant options such as Titan AX, PegasusAX, and AGT-Bunyip IA. These varieties support herbicide group rotation and enhance in-crop weed control, contributing to more sustainable agronomic practices. AGT-Bunyip IA is particularly well-positioned to challenge Maximus CL in rotations where Tomahawk CL Plus wheat is grown. This rotation strategy, alternating between AGT-Bunyip IA barley and Tomahawk CL Plus wheat or other Clearfield[®] wheat varieties, enables growers to maintain both genetic and species diversity in their cropping systems. Such diversity is a cornerstone of sustainable weed management, helping to reduce pressure from herbicide resistance and improve long-term system resilience. 

Additionally, niche varietal preferences will continue to shape planting decisions. RGT Atlantis is expected to be used in waterlogged paddocks, while Kirwan CL will be targeted for bulk-up on acidic soils. Overall, the varietal mix in Albany is evolving to meet agronomic challenges and market demands, with Maximus CL and Neo CL remaining key targets for both grain and malt export opportunities. Limited segregations for Cyclops and Laperouse also provide scope for growers to access malt premiums where supported. 

## **Esperance Port Zone** 

The Esperance Port Zone is focused on exporting barley as grain. According to GIWA’s July estimates (2015–25), this zone accounts for a median of 290,000 hectares, representing 18% of Western Australia’s total barley area. 

In 2025, barley production in Esperance was concentrated among 5 key varieties: Maximus CL, Neo CL, Combat, Beast, and Spartacus CL. Together, these accounted for 87% of the total area planted, with Maximus CL alone representing more than half of all barley hectares. It was 7 times more prevalent than Neo CL and Combat. Imidazolinone-tolerant varieties, including Maximus CL, Neo CL, Spartacus CL, Commodus CL, and Zena CL, occupied 72% of the barley area. This level of adoption was comparable to that in Geraldton, although lower than in the Kwinana and Albany Port Zones. CoAXium[®] varieties, Titan AX and PegasusAX, and acid-tolerant types, Buff and Litmus, were scarcely planted. Several older varieties, including RGT Planet, Rosalind, and Spartacus CL, saw sharp declines in planted area compared to 2024. Neo CL was the standout mover, gaining significant ground. Consistent with previous years, Esperance maintained the strongest focus on feed-only barley production among all WA port zones.

Looking ahead to 2026, Maximus CL and Neo CL are expected to remain the leading varieties in the Esperance Port Zone. Their continued popularity is driven by strong agronomic performance, compatibility with imidazolinone herbicide rotations, and eligibility for MALT1 delivery. Established feed varieties, such as Beast and Combat, will continue to be widely grown, while growers are showing interest in new feed options, including Bigfoot CL, Ember, and Granite CL. Among these, Ember, a new long-season feed barley, is gaining attention for its robust disease resistance and suitability for early sowing in highrisk environments. It offers growers a resilient option for managing challenging conditions. 

Although interest in herbicide-tolerant systems is growing, CoAXium[®] -tolerant barley varieties have seen slower uptake in Esperance compared to the Kwinana and Albany zones. However, PegasusAX, a CoAXium[®] variety with a Rosalindlike plant type, may appeal to growers targeting brome and barley grass in non-imidazolinone rotations. Cyclops is also expected to compete with Maximus CL in these systems, offering higher yield potential. AGT-Bunyip IA is likely to attract interest due to its dual tolerance to imidazolinone and Aggressor[®] AX herbicides, along with its plant type similarity to Maximus CL, which has performed strongly in the region. The adoption of Tomahawk CL Plus wheat will further support rotations involving AGT-Bunyip IA, enabling growers to maintain herbicide group diversity and improve long-term weed management outcomes.

## **Advice by rainfall zone** 

Barley varieties suited to low-rainfall environments include: 

- Beast (tall plant, yield benchmark, weed competitive plant type) 

- Bigfoot CL (shorter straw, weed-competitive, Compass background, imidazolinone-tolerant) 

- Buff (acid soil tolerant) 

- Commodus CL (imidazolinone-tolerant, weed-competitive plant type) 

- Granite CL (imidazolinone-tolerant, Rosalind background) 

- Kirwan CL (combines acid soil and imidazolinone tolerance) 

- Litmus (acid tolerant) 

- Maximus CL (suitable for MALT1 or imidazolinone herbicide systems) 

- PegasusAX (quizalofop-p-ethyl tolerant, Rosalind background) 

- Rocket CL (imidazolinone-tolerant) 

- Rosalind (reliable yield performance) 

- Titan AX (quizalofop-p-ethyl tolerant). 

Barley varieties suited to medium-rainfall environments include: 

- AGT-Bunyip IA (imidazolinone and quizalofopp-ethyl tolerant barley, Maximus CL type) 

- Bigfoot CL (shorter straw, weed-competitive, Compass background, imidazolinone-tolerant) 

- Combat (yield benchmark, strong SFNB resistance) 

- Commodus CL (imidazolinone-tolerant, weedcompetitive plant type) 

**64 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

- Cyclops (Rosalind replacement, potential for MALT1 delivery) 

- Granite CL (imidazolinone-tolerant, Rosalind background) 

- Laperouse (suitable for delivery as MALT1) 

- Maximus CL (suitable for MALT1 or imidazolinone herbicide systems) 

- Neo CL (early planting, imidazolinone-tolerant) 

- PegasusAX (quizalofop-p-ethyl tolerant, Rosalind background) 

- Titan AX (quizalofop-p-ethyl tolerant). 

Barley varieties suited to high-rainfall environments include: 

   - Combat (yield benchmark, strong SFNB resistance) 

   - Cyclops (Rosalind replacement, potential for MALT1 delivery) 

   - Ember (strong disease resistance, reduced lodging risk, suited to April planting) 

   - Laperouse (suitable for delivery as MALT1) 

   - Maximus CL (suitable for MALT1 or imidazolinone herbicide systems) 

   - Neo CL (yield benchmark, imidazolinonetolerant) 

   - RGT Atlantis (ideal for waterlogged paddocks) 

   - Soldier CL (reduced lodging risk). 

- AGT-Bunyip IA (imidazolinone and quizalofopp-ethyl tolerant barley, Maximus CL type)

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **New varieties for 2026** 

Two new feed barley varieties: Ember and Kirwan CL, plus 3 new varieties undergoing malting and brewing accreditation with Grains Australia: AGTBunyip IA, Rocket CL, and Soldier CL, will be available for planting in 2026. 

When deciding which barley variety to sow, the grain yield potential must be balanced against trade-offs in agronomic factors, disease resistance, grain quality, segregation opportunities, and market demand. Commonly grown varieties differ in their agronomic traits and the pathways to building yield (that is, trade-offs between tiller number, grains per ear and grain weight). These phenotypic differences may favour one variety over another in some seasons but not in others. Therefore, examining trends across seasons and sites is crucial when determining which variety best suits each farming business. 

The following are notes on these recently released barley varieties: AGT-Bunyip IA, Ember, Kirwan, Rocket CL, and Soldier CL. 

## **AGT-Bunyip IA** 

## **Key points:** 

- Currently in Stage 1 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program (2025), with the earliest possible accreditation in March 2027. 

- Targeted as an alternative to Maximus CL in a similar plant type with a similar grain yield. 

- Tolerant to both imidazolinone chemistry and quizalofop-p-ethyl herbicide (as Aggressor[®] AX). 

- Third year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025. 

- Rated as MS or lower to all leaf diseases as an adult plant, except SFNB (MRMS). 

AGT-Bunyip IA (tested as AGTB0530) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row barley variety bred by AGT and officially registered in June 2025. AGT-Bunyip IA is the first barley variety globally to offer dual herbicide tolerance, resistant to both imidazolinone and Aggressor[®] AX (quizalofop-p-ethyl). This enables sowing into imidazolinone residues and in-crop spraying with 

either herbicide, improving flexibility for managing carry-over and controlling key weeds such as brome grass, barley grass, annual ryegrass, wild oats, and volunteer wheat. When partnered in rotations with Tomahawk CL Plus wheat or other Clearfield® tolerant wheat varieties, growers will be able to maintain herbicide group diversity, manage wheat volunteers and improve long-term weed management outcomes. 

AGT-Bunyip IA has been tested in 55 WA barley NVT trials since 2023, with multi-environment trial (MET) data from 2020–24 showing a state wide yield of 106% of the site mean, comparable to Maximus CL at 105% (Table 10). In individual trials, AGT-Bunyip IA yielded lower than Maximus CL in 13%, equal in 58%, and higher in 29% of cases (p=0.05) (Table 12). Yield response was consistent across environments, indicating stable performance (Figure 4). Comparable yields of AGT-Bunyip IA and Maximus CL were also observed across 5 DPIRD nitrogen (N) management in response to increasing N supply (up to 150 kg N/ha) (based on unpublished DPIRD internal observations). 

In terms of disease resistance, AGT-Bunyip IA shows moderate resistance (MRMS) to SFNB but is susceptible (MS or lower) to Sc, NFNB, PM, and barley leaf rust (BLR) as an adult plant (Table 15). A broad-spectrum fungicide program is likely to maintain crop health while observing best-practice integrated disease management practices. 

In 5 DPIRD N management trials conducted in 2024, AGT-Bunyip IA exhibited more straw leaning than Maximus CL at a comparable plant height (data not presented), consistent with breeder observations (AGT publication #25-062, published August 2025). In those same trials, the level of leaning in AGT-Bunyip IA was comparable to Cyclops and RGT Planet. 

According to the breeder, AGT-Bunyip IA is a quick-maturing variety, typically reaching awn emergence at a similar time to Maximus CL. However, in 2024, DPIRD phenology trials at Northam and Katanning showed that it reached awn emergence 3–4 days later than Maximus CL (data not presented). DPIRD is collecting additional phenology data to confirm those observations. 

**66 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 through AGT Affiliates and authorised resellers. Growers may also trade seed farmer-to-farmer under the AGT Seed Sharing licence, which can be accessed at agtbreeding.com.au/sourcing-seed/ seed-sharing . Importantly, any grower intending to use Aggressor[®] AX herbicide must complete the CoAXium[®] stewardship program, available online at CoAXium.com.au/stewardship . The stewardship program and distribution of Aggressor[®] AX are administered by Sipcam, ensuring responsible use of the technology and compliance with best practice guidelines. 

## **Ember** 

## **Key points:** 

- Released as a feed-only variety and is not being evaluated for its malting and brewing potential. 

- Best suited to early sowing (April) in highrainfall environments, particularly where disease pressure is high. 

- Third year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025, but only in Agzones 3 and 6. 

- Adult disease resistance rating of MRMS or better to all leaf diseases except SFNB (MS). 

Ember (tested as IGB21130) is a medium-height, late-spring, 2-row feed variety bred by InterGrain and registered as a variety in July 2025. In DPIRD phenology trials (2023–24), Ember reached awn emergence at a similar time to Baudin and Gairdner, but was 7–14 days slower than Maximus CL and Neo CL under April and May sowing conditions. These findings are consistent with the observations of InterGrain regarding Maximus CL and Neo CL. 

Ember has not been widely tested in WA, having been included only in NVT trials in Agzones 3 and 6 (Tables 4–10). MET data (2020–24) show Ember achieving 97% of the site mean yield across WA, compared to 105% for Maximus CL and 109% for Neo CL (Table 10). In 17 individual trials, Ember yielded lower than Maximus CL in 59% of cases, equal in 29%, and higher in 12% (Table 12). Against Neo CL, it yielded lower in 88% of trials and never higher (Table 13). 



InterGrain’s product profile (released August 2025) suggests Ember performs similarly to Neo CL when planted in April but yields less with May planting. DPIRD N management trials in 2023 showed Ember’s yield ranged between Maximus CL and Neo CL, depending on N rate (data not presented). Further data from April sowing is needed to confirm its fit, but its strong disease resistance makes it a promising option for southern regions with early sowing windows. 

Ember offers robust disease resistance, rated MRMS or better for Sc, NFNB, PM, and BLR, but is moderately susceptible (MS) to SFNB (Table 15). Strong seedling resistance to NFNB and PM helps reduce the risk of early-season disease (Table 14). To maintain crop health, Ember should be grown using non-chemical control methods to reduce disease pressure, planted in rotations that minimise disease carryover, and sprayed only, if necessary, in a strategic manner with different modes of action. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 from Seedclub members and resellers. Seed is also free to trade from farmer-to-farmer by complying with the InterGrain Industry Standard Seed Licence, which can be accessed at intergrain.com/ epr/variety-license/ . 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 67** 

Barley 

## **Kirwan CL** 

## **Key points:** 

- Released as a feed-only variety and is not being evaluated for its malting and brewing potential. 

- Best suited to low pH soils with high aluminium levels in low to medium rainfall environments. 

- First barley variety combining imidazolinone tolerance and enhanced tolerance to acid soils. 

- Second year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025, having been evaluated in 2023 but not in 2024. 

- Has been released based on its performance in breeder trials. 

- Provisional disease data suggest Kirwan CL has good resistance to NFNB as an adult plant, but is weak against Sc, SFNB and BLR. It may show variable reactions against different pathotypes of PM. 

Kirwan CL has a provisional adult plant resistance rating of MRMS to NFNB, but is susceptible (S) to Sc, SFNB and BLR (Table 15). Kirwan CL may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. Where necessary, a comprehensive strategic fungicide program is recommended to maintain crop health while adhering to best-practice integrated disease management practices. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 from Seedclub members and resellers. Seed is also free to trade from farmer-to-farmer by complying with the InterGrain Industry Standard Seed Licence, which can be accessed at intergrain.com/ epr/variety-license/ . 

## **Rocket CL** 

## **Key points:** 

- Currently in Stage 1 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program (2025), with the earliest possible accreditation in March 2028 

- Targeted for sowing in low- and mediumrainfall areas, and for later planting. 

Kirwan CL (tested as IGB22014T) is a mediumheight, early-spring, 2-row feed barley variety developed by InterGrain and registered in August herbicide tolerance 2025. It combines Clearfield[®] with acid soil tolerance, making it a strong candidate for low pH soils with high aluminium levels. While not yet independently field-tested by DPIRD, Kirwan CL is included in the 2025 DPIRD phenology series. 

According to InterGrain’s August 2025 product profile, Kirwan CL is positioned as an alternative to Buff and Litmus, offering improved adaptability to challenging soil conditions along with Clearfield[®] tolerance. Although not tested in WA barley NVT in 2024, Kirwan CL was released based on breeder trial data and its inclusion in 16 WA NVT trials in 2023. MET data (2020–24) show that Kirwan CL achieved 106% of the site mean yield across WA in 2023 (excluding Agzones 3 and 6), outperforming Buff (99%) and Litmus (97%) (Table 10). In individual trials, Kirwan CL yielded higher than Buff in 63% of cases, equal in 31%, and lower in only 6% (data not presented). Against Litmus, it performed equally in 55% of trials and better in 45% (data not presented). 

- Tolerant to imidazolinone chemistry. 

- First entered the WA barley NVT in 2024. 

- Has been released based on its performance in breeder trials. 

- Provisional adult disease resistance to PM of MRMS# but MS or lower for Sc, NFNB, SFNB, and BLR. 

Rocket CL (tested as IGB22023T) is a mediumheight, very early-spring, 2-row barley variety developed by InterGrain and registered in July herbicide tolerance and 2025. It offers Clearfield[®] appears well-suited to late May–early June sowing in WA’s northern and eastern wheatbelt, where its early maturity helps avoid terminal drought and heat stress. 

In DPIRD 2024 phenology trials (Northam and Katanning), Rocket CL reached awn emergence at a similar thermal time to Mundah and was approximately 7–10 days earlier than Maximus CL (2024 only, data not presented). While Beast and Commodus CL were not included, breeder data suggest Rocket CL matures more rapidly. 

**68 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

InterGrain’s August 2025 product profile positions Rocket CL as an alternative to Beast and Commodus CL, with improved lodging tolerance and head retention. It was included in 22 WA barley NVT trials in 2024 (but not in Agzones 3 and 6) and released based on breeder trial performance. Across WA MET trials (2020–24), Rocket CL yielded 102% of the site mean, below Beast (108%) and comparable to Commodus CL (101%) (Table 10). In individual trials, Rocket CL outperformed Commodus CL in 55% of cases, matched it in 32%, and yielded a lower result in 14% (data not presented). Against Beast, it performed equally in 59% of trials and lower in 41% (data not presented). 

Rocket CL has a provisional adult plant resistance rating of MRMS# to PM but is susceptible (MS or lower) to Sc, NFNB, SFNB and BLR (Table 15). Rocket CL may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. A strategic fungicide program and integrated disease management practices are recommended to maintain crop health. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 from Seedclub members and resellers. Seed is also free to trade from farmer-to-farmer by complying with the InterGrain Industry Standard Seed Licence, which can be accessed at intergrain.com/ epr/variety-license/ . 

## **Soldier CL** 

## **Key points:** 

- Currently in Stage 1 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program (2025), with the earliest possible accreditation in March 2028. 

- Targeted for sowing in high rainfall zones. 

- Tolerant to imidazolinone chemistry. 

- Under evaluation in WA barley NVT for the first time in 2025, but only in Agzones 3 and 6. 

- Limited public data on its long-term performance is available. 

- Has been released based on its performance in breeder trials. 

- Provisional adult disease tolerance rating data is unavailable from NVT consensus trials. 

Soldier CL (tested as IGB22117T) is a shortheight, early-spring, 2-row barley variety bred by InterGrain and registered in July 2025. It offers herbicide tolerance and is well-suited Clearfield[®] to high-rainfall environments where lodging is a common concern. 

In DPIRD phenology trials in 2024 at Northam and Katanning, Soldier CL reached awn emergence at a similar time to Spartacus CL with April and May sowing and was up to a week quicker to awn emergence than Maximus CL and Neo CL (based on unpublished DPIRD internal observations). 

In 5 DPIRD N management trials conducted in 2024, Soldier CL showed reduced lodging and stronger straw integrity compared to Compass, Maximus CL, and Neo CL (data not presented). This aligns with InterGrain’s breeder observations of robust straw strength (InterGrain, published August 2025). 

Soldier CL has been released based on InterGrain’s internal data, as it had not been sown in WA NVT before 2025. InterGrain’s August 2025 product profile positions Soldier CL as an alternative to Neo CL, where lodging is a frequent problem. InterGrain internal trials (2019–24) suggest that Soldier CL yields consistently between Neo CL and RGT Planet in environments yielding up to 7 t/ha (InterGrain product profile, August 2025). DPIRD 2024 season N trials support this trend (data not presented). Growers are encouraged to seek independent data before making decisions on whether or not to adopt any new variety, including Soldier CL. 

As Soldier CL was not included in NVT pathology trials before 2025, consensus disease ratings are unavailable. However, InterGrain’s August 2025 product profile lists Soldier CL as having good adult plant resistance (MRMS or better) to Sc, NFNB and PM in WA, but susceptible (MSS or lower) to SFNB and BLR. Its disease resistance profile is broadly similar to Neo CL, although it is much weaker for SFNB. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 from Seedclub members and resellers. Seed is also free to trade from farmer-to-farmer by complying with the InterGrain Industry Standard Seed Licence, which can be accessed at intergrain.com/ epr/variety-license/ . 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 69** 

Barley 

## **Other barley varieties to consider** 

Three new feed barley varieties, Bigfoot CL, Granite CL, and PegasusAX, were released in 2024. All 3 demonstrate comparable grain yield performance to each other and to the benchmark herbicide-tolerant variety Maximus CL. They have an advantage over Commodus CL once the site yield exceeds 4 t/ha. However, they differ in plant architecture, lodging resistance, herbicide compatibility, and other agronomic traits. Each offers distinct advantages over Commodus CL, making them strong alternatives in environments where higher yield potential can be achieved. The following comments are relevant. 

## **Bigfoot CL** 

## **Key points:** 

- Released as a feed-only variety and is not being evaluated for its malting and brewing potential. 

- Targeted for sowing in low to medium rainfall environments. 

- Tolerant to imidazolinone chemistry. 

- Third year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025. 

- Adult disease resistance rating of MRMS to NFNB, MS or lower to other leaf diseases. It may show variable reactions against different pathotypes of PM. 

Bigfoot CL (tested as AGTB0669) is a mediumheight, early-spring, 2-row feed barley variety developed by AGT and first sown in WA NVT trials in 2023. It is targeted as an alternative to imidazolinone-tolerant varieties such as Commodus CL, Granite CL and Maximus CL. Bigfoot CL features a long coleoptile, supporting deeper sowing in dry or non-wetting soils (AGT publication #25-067, published August 2025). 

Bigfoot CL has a comparable grain yield to Maximus CL and a higher yield potential than Commodus CL above 4 t/ha (Tables 4–10, Figure 4). MET data (2020–24) shows Bigfoot CL 

achieved 106% of the site mean yield across WA, compared to 100% for Commodus CL and 105% for Maximus CL (Table 10). In 42 WA barley NVT (2023–24), Bigfoot CL outperformed Commodus CL in 69% of cases, and matched in 31% (p=0.05, data not presented). Against Maximus CL, Bigfoot CL yielded lower in 21% of trials, similar in 52%, and higher in 26% (Table 12). While Granite CL has only one year of NVT data, early results suggest similar yield performance to Bigfoot CL. In 24 WA NVT trials (2024–only), Bigfoot CL yielded similarly to Granite CL in 83% of cases and was lower in 17% (p=0.05, data not presented). Growers should not assume that Bigfoot CL’s relative performance in 2023 and 2024 will be indicative of its future performance. At least 3 seasons of data are generally required to indicate long-term performance. 

Bigfoot CL shares a Compass-type plant structure with Commodus CL, but has shorter straw and improved lodging tolerance. In contrast, Granite CL and Maximus CL have an erect plant type, which is generally considered less competitive with weeds than the semi-erect types of Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, and Compass. 

In terms of disease resistance, Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, Granite CL, and Maximus CL have comparable adult plant ratings (Table 15). However, Bigfoot CL offers improved tolerance to the Oxford virulent pathotype of NFNB and enhanced seedling resistance to the Beecher avirulent NFNB pathotype, helping to limit earlyseason disease spread (Tables 14 and 15). Granite CL also shares this enhanced seedling resistance trait with NFNB (Beecher avirulent), while also exhibiting improved seedling resistance to SFNB. Bigfoot CL may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 through AGT Affiliates and authorised resellers. Growers may also trade seed farmer-to-farmer under the AGT Seed Sharing licence, which can be accessed at agtbreeding.com.au/sourcing-seed/seed-sharing . 

**70 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Granite CL** 

## **Key points:** 

- Released as a feed-only variety and is not being evaluated for its malting and brewing potential. 

- Targeted for sowing in low to medium rainfall environments. 

- Tolerant to imidazolinone chemistry. 

- Second year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025. 

- Provisional adult disease resistance rating of MR to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), MS or lower to other leaf diseases. It may show variable reactions against different pathotypes of PM. 

Granite CL (tested as IGB21092T) is a mediumheight, medium-spring, 2-row feed barley bred by InterGrain and first sown in WA barley NVT in 2024. Granite CL is suited to low- and mediumrainfall zones, as an imidazolinone-tolerant alternative to varieties such as Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, and Maximus CL. Its erect growth habit, inherited from the Rosalind family, may reduce weed competitiveness compared to semi-erect types. 

In 24 WA barley NVT trials (2024-only and not tested in Agzone 4), Granite CL yielded similarly to Bigfoot CL in 83% of cases and higher in 17% (p=0.05, data not presented). It outperformed Commodus CL in two-thirds of trials, particularly at sites yielding above 4 t/ha. Compared to Maximus CL, Granite CL yielded lower in 17%, similar in 50%, and higher in 33% of trials. However, as Granite CL has only one year of NVT data, growers are advised to interpret these results cautiously; typically, 3 or more seasons of data are required to assess long-term performance. 

Granite CL has comparable adult plant disease resistance to Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, and Maximus CL. However, it is less tolerant than Bigfoot CL to the Oxford virulent pathotype of NFNB. It does offer enhanced seedling resistance to both the Beecher avirulent NFNB pathotype and SFNB, helping to reduce early-season disease pressure (Tables 14–15). Granite CL may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 from Seedclub members and resellers. Seed is also free to trade from farmer-to-farmer by complying with the InterGrain Industry Standard Seed Licence, which can be accessed at intergrain.com/ epr/variety-license/ . 

## **PegasusAX** 

## **Key points:** 

- Released as a feed-only variety and not being evaluated for its malting and brewing potential. 

- Targeted for sowing in all environments where Rosalind has been successful. 

- Tolerant to quizalofop-p-ethyl herbicide (as Aggressor[®] AX). 

- Third year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025. 

- Adult disease resistance rating of MRMS or better to NFNB, PM and BLR, MS or lower to Sc and SFNB. 

PegasusAX (tested as AGTB0667) is a mediumheight, early-spring, 2-row feed barley variety developed by AGT and first sown in WA NVT trials in 2023. PegasusAX is compatible with quizalofop-p-ethyl herbicide (Aggressor[®] AX) under the CoAXium[®] system. It is not tolerant to imidazolinone herbicides, offering an alternative mode of action for controlling grass weeds in barley. PegasusAX is suited to environments where Rosalind has performed well, particularly in medium to high-rainfall zones. Its erect growth habit (like Rosalind) and shorter straw reduce lodging risk (AGT publication #25-065, August 2025), making it suitable for high-biomass conditions. PegasusAX has a shorter coleoptile, so deep sowing should be avoided. 

PegasusAX has demonstrated comparable grain yield to Bigfoot CL, Granite CL, Maximus CL, and Titan AX, and performs better than Commodus CL at sites yielding above 4 t/ha (Tables 4-10, Figure 4). Across 42 WA barley NVT trials (2023–24), PegasusAX yielded similarly to Titan AX in 38% of trials, was higher in 45% and lower in 17% (p=0.05, data not shown). Against Maximus CL, it yielded lower in 12% of trials, similar in 55%, and higher in 33% (Table 12). 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 71** 

Barley 

PegasusAX offers improved adult plant tolerance to NFNB (Oxford virulent) and BLR compared to Commodus CL, Granite CL, Maximus CL, and Titan AX (Table 15). It also provides enhanced seedling resistance to the Beecher avirulent NFNB pathotype, helping to limit early-season disease spread (Table 14). The seedling and adult resistance of PegasusAX and Bigfoot CL is similar to the Oxford virulent pathotype of NFNB. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 through AGT Affiliates and authorised resellers. Growers may also trade seed farmer-to-farmer under the AGT Seed Sharing licence, which can be accessed at agtbreeding.com.au/sourcing-seed/ seed-sharing . Importantly, any grower intending to use Aggressor[®] AX herbicide must complete the CoAXium[®] stewardship program, available online at CoAXium.com.au/stewardship . The stewardship program and distribution of Aggressor[®] AX are administered by Sipcam, ensuring responsible use of the technology and compliance with best practice guidelines.

Barley 

## **Barley varieties in Stage 2 of malt accreditation** 

Spinnaker and Titan AX are in Stage 2 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program, with a decision expected in March 2026. Stage 2 indicates advanced evaluation of malt quality and brewing performance at a commercial scale. Each variety has a good fit in specific systems. 

## **Spinnaker** 

## **Key points:** 

- Undergoing Stage 2 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation program in 2025, with the earliest accreditation date being March 2026. 

- Targeted as an alternative to RGT Planet in low- to medium-rainfall zones. 

- Fifth year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025. 

- Rated as MRMS or better to Sc, NFNB (Beecher avirulent) and PM, and MS or lower to NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB and BLR. 

Spinnaker (tested as SCA21-Y003) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row barley bred by SECOBRA Recherches. While not yet commercially available, a production crop was established in the south-west in 2025. Spinnaker is positioned as an alternative to RGT Planet in low- to medium-rainfall zones. It combines good straw strength with excellent head retention or and does not carry tolerance to Clearfield[®] CoAXium[®] herbicides (SECOBRA Recherches Spinnaker factsheet, October 2024). 

Spinnaker’s malt profile may allow processing without gibberellic acid, making it attractive to domestic maltsters supplying Heineken and Japanese brewers seeking malt produced without processing aids. It is also under evaluation for the Japanese shochu market. 

In the WA barley NVT series (2023–24), Spinnaker demonstrated grain yield and hectolitre weight equivalent to RGT Planet, with moderately improved grain retention (Figures 5–7). It also showed slightly better seedling and adult resistance to NFNB and SFNB (Tables 14–15), 2 diseases that contributed to the decline 

of RGT Planet but also pose risks to Spinnaker. Integrated disease management, including crop rotation and strategic fungicide use with varied modes of action, is necessary to manage disease risk effectively, especially where the RGT Planet virulent NFNB exists. Compared to Maximus CL, Spinnaker had lower grain yield, hectolitre weight, and retention in NVT trials. However, in 10 DPIRD N management trials (2023–24) in the Kwinana Port Zone, Spinnaker yielded similarly to Maximus CL, with its hectolitre weight slightly better than RGT Planet and retention between Maximus CL and RGT Planet across N fertiliser rates from 10 to 150 kg N/ha (data not presented). If received, the probability of Spinnaker achieving MALT1 specifications is expected to be lower than that of Maximus CL and slightly higher than that of RGT Planet. These results suggest Spinnaker may be a viable option for domestic malt supply, particularly in western regions of the Kwinana Port Zone, if its malt quality is accredited and a premium is offered to support its production. 

Subject to Grains Australia accreditation and market demand, limited seed may be available for planting in 2026 through Seednet Partners and authorised resellers. Farmer-to-farmer trade of Spinnaker seed is prohibited. 

## **Titan AX** 

## **Key points:** 

- Undergoing Stage 2 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program in 2025, with the earliest accreditation date being March 2026. 

- Targeted as an alternative weed control option to the Clearfield[®] herbicide system in low- to medium-rainfall zones. 

- Tolerant to quizalofop-p-ethyl herbicide (as Aggressor[®] AX). 

- Fifth year of public testing in WA barley NVT in 2025. 

- Rated as MRMS to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), and MS or lower to Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB and BLR. It may show variable reactions against different pathotypes of PM. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 73** 

BARLEY 

Titan AX (tested as AGTB0325) is a tall-height, medium-spring, 2-row variety bred by AGT. Titan AX is compatible with quizalofop-p-ethyl herbicide under the CoAXium[®] system (Aggressor[®] AX), offering an alternative to Clearfield[®] chemistry for grass weed control. It shares a genetic background with Compass and is suited to low- to medium-rainfall environments, particularly where early vigour and longer straw are preferred and lodging risk is minimal. Its tall Compass-type structure may increase head loss risk in longerseason or high yielding environments, requiring careful management of lodging and harvest timing (AGT publication #25-077, August 2025). 

Titan AX is currently in commercial production in WA, contributing to the region’s interest in herbicide-tolerant barley systems. Despite undergoing malt accreditation, Titan AX’s medium fermentability profile, similar to Commodus CL, may limit its appeal in WA’s export malt markets. Commodus CL was removed from segregation plans for the 2026–27 harvest due to weak demand, and Titan AX may face similar market constraints. 

As a feed barley, Titan AX competes with other CoAXium[®] varieties such as AGT-Bunyip IA and PegasusAX, all of which show similar yield performance across site potentials (Table 10, Figure 4). Titan AX has a semi-erect early growth habit, whereas AGT-Bunyip IA and PegasusAX 

are more erect, with shorter straw and improved lodging tolerance (AGT publications #25-062, #25-065 and #25-077, published August 2025). AGT-Bunyip IA also offers imidazolinone tolerance, providing greater flexibility for managing herbicide residues and controlling problem weeds. Across 89 WA barley NVT (2021–24), Titan AX yielded less than Maximus CL in 40% of trials, similarly in 17% and higher in 43% (Table 12). As site potential changes, Titan AX and Maximus CL yield similarly (Figures 4–5). 

Disease strengths for Titan AX are NFNB (Beecher avirulent). However, it requires management for Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR. Titan AX may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 through AGT Affiliates and authorised resellers. Growers may also trade seed farmer-to-farmer under the AGT Seed Sharing licence, which can be accessed at agtbreeding.com.au/sourcing-seed/ seed-sharing . Importantly, any grower intending to use Aggressor[®] AX herbicide must complete the CoAXium[®] stewardship program, available online at CoAXium.com.au/stewardship . The stewardship program and distribution of Aggressor[®] AX are administered by Sipcam, ensuring responsible use of the technology and compliance with best practice guidelines. 



**74 2024 WESTERN AUSTRALIAN CROP SOWING GUIDE** 

Barley 

## **Newly accredited malt varieties** 

Three newly accredited varieties: Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL, have been added to the GIWA list of malt varieties for which segregations may be offered at the 2026-27 harvest following the removal of Buff, Commodus CL, and Spartacus CL. Segregation opportunities for these 3 varieties will depend on production volumes in specific regions and market demand. Cyclops, Laperouse and Neo CL are competitive with the entrenched malting variety Maximus CL, while offering growers differing agronomic packages and end-users different malt quality opportunities. Market-specific information can be found in the ‘Market feedback’ section. The following section describes the agronomic attributes of each variety. 

## **Cyclops** 

## **Key points:** 

- Cyclops was accredited as a malt barley by Grains Australia in March 2025 and is eligible for MALT1 receival and malt contracts, subject to market demand. 

- Targeted for sowing in all rainfall zones but better suited to medium to high rainfall areas. 

- Cyclops has been tested in WA barley NVT since 2020. 

- Grain yield advantage over Maximus CL at sites that yield more than 4 t/ha. 

- Requires management of Sc, NFNB (Oxford avirulent), SFNB and BLR. 

Cyclops (tested as AGTB0200) is a mediumheight, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley variety developed by AGT. It does not carry tolerance to Clearfield[®] or CoAXium[®] herbicides. Cyclops is broadly adapted to all rainfall zones but performs best in medium to high rainfall environments, particularly where yield potential exceeds 4 t/ha. Its erect growth habit and short coleoptile may limit competitiveness in situations where weeds are a problem at emergence or under deepsowing conditions. Cyclops grain has short rachilla hairs, classifying it as a low-itch variety (AGT publication #25-073, August 2025). Its early vigour 

and plant height are comparable to Maximus CL, though it is more prone to lodging under high N input (Figure 8). 

Cyclops has been extensively tested in WA, with 134 NVT trials since 2020. MET data (2020–24) show Cyclops achieved 108% of the site mean yield state wide, slightly outperforming Maximus CL at 105% (Table 10). In individual trials, Cyclops yielded lower than Maximus CL in 18% of cases, equal in 33%, and higher in 49% (Table 12). It shows a yield advantage over Maximus CL at sites yielding above 4 t/ha (Figure 5), and both varieties respond similarly to increasing N rates up to 150 kg N/ha (Figure 8). 

Cyclops offers a competitive grain quality package. In WA NVT trials (2023–24), its hectolitre weight was 1.5–2.5 kg/hL lighter than Maximus CL near the MALT1 lower limit of 64 kg/hL (Figure 6), while retention was similar (Figure 7). DPIRD N management trials (2022–24) confirmed these trends (Figure 8). The probability of meeting MALT1 receival standards may be slightly lower than Maximus CL. Cyclops and Maximus CL have a similar risk of germ-end staining (black point) (Table 16). 

Like Maximus CL, Cyclops exhibits useful resistance to NFNB (Beecher virulent), but may require management for Sc, NFNB (Oxford avirulent), and BLR (Tables 14–15). It is rated MS as a seedling and MSS as an adult to SFNB, limiting disease expression compared to more susceptible varieties like RGT Planet, Rosalind and Spartacus CL. It exhibits good resistance to the Medina pathotype of Sc; however, field observations may vary due to the evolving virulence of the pathogen. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 through AGT Affiliates and authorised resellers. Growers may also trade seed farmer-to-farmer under the AGT Seed Sharing licence, which can be accessed at agtbreeding.com.au/sourcing-seed/seed-sharing .
