## **Laperouse** 

## **Key points:** 

- Laperouse was accredited as a malt barley by Grains Australia in May 2025 and is eligible for MALT1 receival and malt contracts, subject to market demand. 

- Targeted for sowing in medium- to high-rainfall areas. 

- Laperouse has been tested in WA barley NVT since 2016. 

- Comparable grain yield and quality to Maximus CL. 

- MSS or lower to most leaf diseases in adult plants, except NFNB (Beecher avirulent). It may show variable reactions against different pathotypes of PM. 

of meeting MALT1 receival standards as Maximus CL. Laperouse and Maximus CL have a similar risk of germ-end staining (black point) (Table 16). 

In terms of disease resistance, Laperouse exhibits excellent resistance to the South Perth pathotype of PM and useful resistance to NFNB (Beecher virulent) at both the seedling and adult stages (Tables 14–15). Laperouse may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. It is less suited to regions where Sc is a recurring issue and is rated as MSS or lower for NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 through Seednet Partners and authorised resellers. Growers may also trade seed with other farmers by complying with the Seednet Grower Agreement (contact Seednet directly for further information). 

## **Neo CL** 

Laperouse (tested as WI4592) is a mediumheight, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley variety developed by the University of Adelaide Barley Breeding Program and licensed to SECOBRA Recherches. Laperouse is best suited to mediumand high-rainfall environments, where its strong straw and head retention traits help reduce harvest losses. It is not compatible with Clearfield[®] or CoAXium[®] herbicide systems (Figure 8, SECOBRA Recherches Laperouse factsheet, May 2025). 

The variety has been extensively evaluated in WA, with 191 NVT trials conducted since 2016. In 134 WA barley NVT trials (2020–24), Laperouse yielded less than Maximus CL in 32% of trials, matched it in 43%, and outperformed it in 25% (Table 12). Its state wide MET yield was similar to Maximus CL at 104% of the site mean but slightly lower than Cyclops at 108% (Table 10). Yield comparisons indicate that Laperouse and Maximus CL perform similarly across different site yields. However, Laperouse tends to underperform relative to Cyclops at high-yielding sites (above 4 t/ha) (Figures 2 and 5). Laperouse shows a similar response to fertiliser N as Maximus CL, and yield is limited without adequate fertiliser N (Figure 8). 

Grain quality assessments show that Laperouse generally achieves a hectolitre weight comparable to Maximus CL (Figure 6), with slightly improved grain retention (Figure 7) and lower grain protein concentration at equivalent yields (data not presented). These trends were confirmed in DPIRD N management trials (2022–24) (Figure 8), suggesting that Laperouse has a similar likelihood 

## **Key points:** 

- Neo CL was accredited as a malt barley by Grains Australia in March 2025 and is eligible for MALT1 receival and malt contracts, subject to market demand. 

- Targeted for sowing in medium- and high-rainfall areas. 

- First entered the WA barley NVT in 2023. 

- Improved grain yield and grain retention compared to RGT Planet. 

- Rated as MRMS or higher to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), SFNB, and PM, and is MS or lower to NFNB (Oxford virulent) and BLR. Neo CL is susceptible to emerging pathotypes of Sc. 

Neo CL (tested as IGB22102T) is a mediumheight, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley variety developed by InterGrain. It is a cross between RGT Planet and Australian genetics and carries tolerance to imidazolinone herbicides. Neo CL is best suited to medium and high rainfall zones, notably where improved lodging resistance and herbicide flexibility are required. Its semi-prostrate growth habit and shorter maturity height reduce lodging risk compared to RGT Planet (Figure 8, InterGrain Variety Guide 2025, July 2025). 

Unlike Cyclops and Laperouse, Neo CL has only been included in NVT trials since 2023. However, MET data from 2020–24 predicts a state wide yield of 109% of the site mean, outperforming 

**76 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

Maximus CL (105%) and RGT Planet (98%) (Table 10). In 55 WA barley NVT trials (2023–24), Neo CL yielded lower than Maximus CL in 45% of cases, equal in 16%, and higher in 38% (Tables 12–13). Compared with RGT Planet, Neo CL yielded higher results in 93% of trials and similar results in 7% (Table 13). Neo CL exhibits a yield advantage over Maximus CL at high-yielding sites (greater than 5 t/ha), but tends to underperform when site yields are below 3 t/ha. Relative to RGT Planet, Neo CL performs better when site yields exceed 2.5 t/ha. 

Across 14 DPIRD N management trials, Neo CL achieved high yields across all N supply levels (Figure 8). Neo CL responds well to fertiliser N but requires lower N input to reach 95% of maximum yield compared to Maximus CL. It is less responsive than Maximus CL but more responsive than RGT Planet, offering flexibility in N management strategies. 

Grain quality assessments indicate that Neo CL has a hectolitre weight similar to RGT Planet but lower than Maximus CL (Figures 6 and 8). Its grain plumpness is comparable to Maximus CL and superior to RGT Planet (Figures 7 and 8). The lower grain protein observed in DPIRD N management trials (2022–24) was primarily due to protein dilution associated with higher yield 

potential, as protein yield was similar across all N rates. NVT data (2023–24) suggests Neo CL may have a lower grain protein potential than Maximus CL. The probability of Neo CL meeting MALT1 receival standards is expected to be lower than Maximus CL, mainly due to its lower hectolitre weight, but higher than RGT Planet because of improved grain retention. Neo CL also has a lower provisional risk of germ-end staining (black point) than Maximus CL and a similar risk to RGT Planet (Table 16). 

Neo CL offers improved disease resistance compared to Maximus CL and RGT Planet. It is rated MRMS or better for NFNB (Beecher avirulent), SFNB, and PM at both the seedling and adult stages, which helps limit canopy infection (Tables 14–15). It is rated MRMS as an adult plant to the Medina Sc pathotype; however, field observations indicate a susceptible response due to the evolving virulence of the pathogen. Neo CL is rated MS or lower for NFNB (Oxford virulent) and BLR at both growth stages. 

Seed is available for planting in 2026 from Seedclub members and resellers. Seed is also free to trade from farmer-to-farmer by complying with the InterGrain Industry Standard Seed Licence, which can be accessed at intergrain.com/ epr/variety-license/ . 



**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 77** 

Barley 

## **Other considerations for barley growers** 

## **Changes in disease pathogens** 

Recent surveys confirm the widespread presence of the Oxford virulent NFNB pathotype in WA, along with increasing PM virulence in varieties previously rated as resistant. New Sc pathotypes have also emerged, requiring updated variety selection and fungicide strategies. More information can be found in the ‘Disease and pest resistance’ section, and in the ‘Barley variety choice in 2026 – what should I grow’ section. 

Growers should monitor for unexpected disease severity in varieties rated MRMS or better and report findings to local agronomists or plant pathologists. The rising incidence of fungicide resistance further underscores the need for an integrated approach to disease management. The Australian Fungicide Resistance Extension Network (AFREN) recommends the following bestpractice principles: 

- avoid susceptible crop varieties, 

- rotate crops and varieties, 

- use non-chemical control methods, 

- spray strategically and only if necessary, and 

- rotate and mix fungicide groups. 

## **Tips for managing grain protein in malt barley** 

Managing N is critical for achieving optimal grain protein levels in malting barley. Grain protein concentration is influenced by the balance between N supply and crop demand, which varies with seasonal conditions. 

Traditional N strategies focused on early application (seeding to 4 WAS) are less effective for balancing yield and protein. Delayed N application, particularly around stem elongation, can improve grain protein with minimal yield penalty. Applying approximately two-thirds of the total N around stem elongation can increase grain protein. In some seasons, additional N at flag leaf emergence may further boost protein levels. 

Delayed N strategies also enable growers to more accurately assess seasonal potential before committing to higher N inputs. 

Other strategies to increase grain protein include applying higher N rates, sowing into legume stubble to enhance soil N supply, and selecting higher-protein varieties such as Maximus CL. These varieties can deliver up to 1% higher grain protein at similar yield levels compared to lower protein types. 

## **Target plant density** 

Seeding rates should be calculated based on target plant density (plants/m²), accounting for seed size, germination, and establishment. Fixed machinery rates (kg/ha) can lead to under- or over-seeding, especially with variable seed lots. 

When aiming for high grain yield in medium to high rainfall zones, feed barley requires a higher target plant density than malt barley. Maintaining 180–220 plants/m² in feed barley improves weed suppression and yield potential. Even in clean paddocks, this density can provide a 1–3% yield advantage with minimal impact on grain quality, typically reducing hectolitre weight by less than 0.5 kg/hL. 

For malt barley, a target density of 150–180 plants/m² balances yield and grain quality. 

The target density in plants/m[2] determines the seeding rate in kg/ha and is calculated using the following formula: 

**Seed rate 1000 kernel weight (g) x target density (plants/m[2] ) = (kg⁄ha) germination % x establishment % x 100** 

For example, if sowing RGT Planet barley with a kernel weight of 45 g per 1000 kernels at a target density of 180 plants/m[2] with a germination of 96% and an expected establishment of 80%, then the seed rate in kg/ha required to establish 180 plants/m[2] is: 

**45 x 180 seed rate in kg/ha = 105 kg/ha = 0.96 x 0.80 x 100** 

**78 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Market feedback** 

## **Grain Industry Association of Western Australia (GIWA)** 

For the 2026–27 harvest, the following observations are relevant. 

## **Global barley market overview** 

Global barley market dynamics remain essentially unchanged from the previous year. China continues to dominate as the world’s largest barley importer, accounting for nearly half of global imports in 2023–24. Australia plays a significant role in the malt barley trade, contributing to around 30–40% of the global supply. China is also Australia’s largest export destination, particularly for FAQ barley. 

Meanwhile, Saudi Arabia has significantly reduced its presence in the global barley trade and is no longer a major player. This shift highlights the increasing significance of emerging markets and underscores the need for Australia to diversify its export strategy. 

## **Emerging and niche markets** 

Africa and Latin America are showing strong demand for premium single-variety malt barley, with countries like Mexico, Peru, and Ecuador exclusively purchasing single-variety MALT1 grade barley, avoiding FAQ and feed grades entirely. Chile and Brazil have expressed interest in importing WA malt barley grain, but access constraints currently limit trade. As a result, Brazilian brewers are importing processed Australian malt instead. 

These emerging and niche markets play a crucial role in maintaining the price spread between malt and feed barley. Without their demand, the gap would narrow further, putting additional pressure on profitability for WA growers and exporters. 

## **Southeast Asia and Japan** 

There is sustained demand for barley that can be malted without the use of processing aids, particularly from premium beer producers like Heineken in Southeast Asia and brewers in Japan. These markets are strategically important for 

domestic malt processors in WA, as they offer opportunities for value-added exports and longterm partnerships. However, meeting this demand is challenging due to limited varietal diversity, especially since Maximus CL, the dominant variety, is not preferred for this brewing segment. Expanding the range of suitable varieties will be essential to better serve these premium markets. 

## **Market challenges** 

Despite emerging markets and trends toward premiumisation, malt premiums remain subdued. This is primarily due to declining beer consumption in mature markets traditionally served by Australian barley and malt. In China, beer production has peaked and is now in decline, although demand for premium beer is rising, which is increasing the need for high-quality malt barley in the brewing process. 

Chinese malt exports are expanding, driven by lower domestic consumption and significantly reduced labour and energy costs, making Chinese malt more competitively priced than Australian malt. This shift is placing pressure on Australian malt houses, leading to reduced production levels and tighter business conditions. A substantial portion of Chinese malt is produced using FAQ barley sourced from WA. This includes outof-spec Maximus CL barley that fails to meet MALT1 standards upon delivery, as well as other malt barley varieties that are binned into feed segregations (often due to the absence of a segregation option for the variety they planted near their growing location). 

## **Variety constraints and opportunities** 

In 2025, only 11 of the 36 varieties planted were planted on more than 10,000 hectares in at least one port zone. Of those, 2 varieties reached that scale in 2 port zones, 4 in 3 zones, and just one, Maximus CL, was planted across all 4 port zones. Maximus CL dominated barley plantings in 2025, as it did in 2024, accounting for two-thirds of the total area. This led to 80% of the MALT1 bulk handling stacks within the CBH network being allocated to this variety in 2024. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 79** 

Barley 

In 2025, the dominance of Maximus CL has slightly declined as growers begin exploring alternative varieties. Despite this shift, the limited diversity in segregated varieties and the low availability of malting-suitable barley that doesn’t require processing aids are constraining global demand for WA malt barley grain and malt. 

The 2026–27 season represents a critical juncture for WA’s malting barley industry, offering a chance to diversify the supply chain and better align with shifting market demands. However, persistently low malt premiums are influencing grower decisions, particularly in the Esperance Port Zone, where many are opting to plant feed-only barley varieties or prioritising volume over quality in malt barley production. In seasons with favourable growing conditions, some of this barley may meet MALT1 specifications and is then delivered to the nearest cost-effective MALT1 stack for the planted variety. This approach reflects a pragmatic response to market pressures but underscores the need for strategic adjustments to maintain WA’s competitiveness in premium malt markets. 

WA has traditionally focused on exporting medium to high fermentability barley varieties, such as Baudin, Bass, Flinders, Maximus CL, RGT Planet, and Spartacus CL, which align with the preferences of key international malt markets. Recently accredited varieties: Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL, also meet this fermentability profile and are slated for commercial evaluation for their market suitability following the 2025–26 harvest. These varieties will reach sufficient on-farm production levels in 2026 to support segregation within the Bunge and CBH bulk handling systems. However, only Cyclops has been trialled at an industrial malting scale in WA, marking a key area for future development and market testing from the 2025–26 harvest. The adoption of Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL could support market diversification, but segregation remains limited, and market acceptance is still in development. 

Commodus CL, a medium fermentability variety considered in 2025, will not be segregated in 2026–27 due to limited market interest, despite ongoing market development in eastern Australia. Similarly, Spartacus CL has been removed from the segregable list due to insufficient supply, and Buff has been excluded because of a lack of domestic malting and market demand, despite its potential for malt production without the use of processing aids. Buff’s future may be further constrained by the release of Kirwan CL, a new imidazolinone-tolerant feed barley suited to acid soils. 

For the domestic market, niche storage solutions such as on-farm and small-scale bulk handling facilities may still support the procurement of key varieties like RGT Planet. However, general segregation will not be offered through Bunge or CBH systems. 

Segregation opportunities for Cyclops, Laperouse, Maximus CL, and Neo CL will vary by port zone and within zones such as Kwinana and Albany, as outlined in Table 3. 

## **Why rationalise malt varieties?** 

The WA barley industry supports a long-term strategy of segregating up to 2 malt barley varieties per port zone, occasionally 3, while limiting segregation for minor, new, or niche malt varieties. This approach is designed to improve logistical efficiency by reducing storage and handling costs, simplifying bin-level segregation planning, and encouraging stronger trader demand by avoiding small, unsaleable parcels of grain. 

At the same time, maintaining a spread of malt varieties with differing agronomic and malting characteristics is essential. This diversity enables processors to blend malt according to specific customer requirements and helps growers manage agronomic risks across varying environments. It is essential to be aware that chemical treatments applied to malt barley crops can impact market access, as not all export markets have the same import tolerances as those in Australia. For instance, opportunistic markets, such as Europe, currently do not accept barley with imazapyr residue or detectable levels of diquat herbicide. Should these markets become regular buyers, specific segregation protocols may be required to meet their standards. 

These recommendations have been developed by GIWA, through its Barley Council, in consultation with stakeholders across the WA barley supply chain. They are intended to guide growers and consultants in planning for the 2026 barley cropping season. A review of the plan will take place in autumn 2026, with any changes in market demand communicated to growers. It’s important to note that WA’s recommendations may differ from those in eastern Australia, reflecting WA’s strong focus on international markets. 

**80 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Malt variety-specific recommendations** 

The release and adoption of new malt barley varieties is occurring more rapidly than the phase-out of older varieties. This high turnover presents challenges for end users, who often prefer long-term supply and familiarity to optimise their brewing processes. Additionally, each new malt segregation increases storage and handling complexity and cost for bulk handlers. 

To address these challenges, the GIWA Barley Variety Rationalisation Plan aims to balance 2 key priorities: enabling growers to access new, highperforming malt varieties, and ensuring customers have consistent access to large, uniform parcels of the same variety over a minimum 5-year period. This approach supports both agronomic innovation and market stability. 

Each malt barley variety grown in WA has distinct malting characteristics, and brewers base their purchasing decisions on a combination of factors, including variety availability, familiarity, consistency, price, beer style requirements, and the type and level of adjuncts used in brewing recipes. This document outlines proposed segregation opportunities by port zone (Table 3) and varietal-specific comments. 

Growers are encouraged to use the market signals provided in this document to inform their malt barley variety choices for the 2026 season. Key considerations include: 

- Market demand and pricing signals. 

- Segregation availability by location. 

- Agronomic suitability and management requirements. 

- Risk associated with achieving malt-grade specifications. 

Varieties classified as PREFERRED are more likely to attract higher premiums than those listed as ACCEPTABLE. However, it is important to note that these recommendations are indicative only. Final segregation arrangements for the 2026–27 harvest may differ from those proposed in this document. 

Growers should maintain regular communication with their bulk handlers to confirm segregation plans and ensure alignment with market opportunities. 

The malt barley recommendations for the 2026 season are as follows: 

## **Cyclops** 

- Cyclops is being assessed for multiple export opportunities, including grain, malt, and shochu production (in Japan). 

- Grains Australia has identified Cyclops as a variety with a high fermentability profile. 

- The Grains Australia Malt Performance Summary for Cyclops is available here: - 

- grainsaustralia.com.au/master lists/ malting-variety-list#barley-master-list/ malt-performance-summaries/ . It presents early commercial malt data and serves as a reference for industry stakeholders, highlighting Cyclops’s anticipated quality traits. 

- Breeder’s data suggest that Cyclops has comparable grain and end-use quality to Spartacus CL, with the added benefit of lower wort β-glucan, which may enhance brewing performance (for example, filtration efficiency and beer clarity). 

- Cyclops has been malted at an industrial scale in WA (2 batches in 2023). However, its commercial malting performance, both domestically and internationally, remains to be evaluated. 

- Target production zones in 2026 include limited segregation in the Albany and Esperance Port Zones. 

## **Laperouse** 

- Laperouse is being assessed for multiple export opportunities, including grain, malt, and shochu production (in Japan). 

- Grains Australia has identified Laperouse as a variety with a medium to high fermentability profile, making it suitable for a range of brewing applications. 

- The Grains Australia Malt Performance Summary for Laperouse is available here: grainsaustralia.com.au/master-lists/ malting-variety-list#barley-master-list/ malt-performance-summaries/ . It presents early commercial malt data and serves as a reference for industry stakeholders, highlighting Laperouse’s anticipated quality traits. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 81** 

Barley 

- Breeder’s data demonstrates that Laperouse has a grain quality comparable to Maximus CL, while offering a distinct malt profile that enhances the diversity of Australian barley and malt options. To optimise malting performance, Laperouse benefits from the application of gibberellic acid and its low wort β-glucan levels support enhanced filtration efficiency and beer clarity. 

- Laperouse has not yet been malted at an industrial scale in WA. 

- Heineken has recognised Laperouse by including it in their “Yellow” variety list, indicating potential suitability for some of their brewing specifications. Further engagement is underway to elevate Laperouse to Heineken’s “Green” list. If successful, this would represent full approval for use in their brewing operations, making it a preferred variety for Heineken’s brewing operations. 

- Target production zones in 2026 include limited segregations in the Kwinana-South and Albany Port Zones. 

## **Maximus CL** 

- Maximus CL is acceptable for export as both grain and malt and is preferred for shochu production in Japan. 

- Grains Australia has identified Maximus CL as a variety with a high fermentability profile, making it well-suited to starch-adjunct brewing. 

- Maximus CL malt delivers high extract and enzyme potential but requires the application of gibberellic acid to achieve suitable modification. 

- The variety is approved by brewing customers outside Japan, with strong market acceptance in Latin America. 

- Maximus CL is the dominant variety in all WA port zones. 

- Growers should use recommended imidazolinone herbicides and remain aware of market advice regarding grain delivery from paddocks treated with these herbicides. 

- Target production zones in 2026 include the Kwinana, Albany and Esperance Port Zones. 

## **Neo CL** 

- Neo CL is being assessed for multiple export opportunities, including grain, malt, and shochu production in Japan. 

- The Grains Australia Malt Performance Summary for Neo CL is available here: - - 

- grainsaustralia.com.au/master lists/malting variety-list#barley-master-list/malt-performancesummaries/ . It presents early commercial malt data and serves as a reference for industry stakeholders, highlighting Neo CL’s anticipated quality traits. 

- Breeder’s data indicates that Neo CL has improved grain retention relative to RGT Planet, along with an enhanced malt profile. While the need for gibberellic acid during its malting process remains unknown, micromalt trials indicate that Neo CL malt delivers improved proteolytic activity and lower wort turbidity compared to RGT Planet malt. 

- Neo CL has not yet been malted at an industrial scale in WA. 

- Heineken has recognised Neo CL by including it in their “Yellow” variety list, indicating potential suitability for some of their brewing specifications. 

- Growers should use recommended imidazolinone herbicides and remain aware of market advice regarding grain delivery from paddocks treated with these herbicides. 

- Target production zones in 2026 include the Albany and Esperance Port Zones, with limited segregation in the Kwinana-South area. 

## **RGT Planet** 

   - RGT Planet is preferred for export as malt, primarily due to its ability to be consistently malted without the use of gibberellic acid, offering processing efficiency for maltsters. 

   - Grains Australia has identified RGT Planet as a variety with a high fermentability profile. 

   - RGT Planet malt has excellent extract with moderate enzyme potential, making it wellsuited for both sugar and starch based adjunct brewing. 

   - There is currently an insufficient supply of MALT1 grade RGT Planet to meet demand. This may result in premiums above Maximus CL, should the trade wish to accumulate RGT Planet through niche segregations with bulk handlers or on-farm storage. 

   - Target production zones in 2026 are niche or on-farm segregations in the Kwinana-South Port Zone. 

- Grains Australia has identified Neo CL as a variety with a high fermentability profile, making it suitable for a range of brewing applications.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 83** 

Barley 

## **Grain yield** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

The National Variety Trials (NVT), coordinated by the Grains Research and Development Corporation (GRDC), offer a nationally consistent and independent framework for evaluating varietal performance across diverse agroecological zones. These trials help growers and agronomists select barley varieties best suited to their local conditions. 

Results are available as single-site reports, reflecting performance in a specific season and location, or multi-environment trials (METs), which compare varietal performance against the site mean across multiple seasons. Users can access and filter NVT data by state, region, location, port zone or Agzone through nvt.grdc.com.au, enabling informed and tailored decision-making. 

This sowing guide is based on MET data from 2020–24, with an accuracy of ≥0.8 and variance accounted of ≥25%. Performance summaries are presented by Agzone and state wide (Tables 4–10). While port zone-based summaries are now available online, they are not included in this guide. 

When 4 or more observations are available, a 5-year weighted average is calculated. However, growers should interpret these averages cautiously, as they may obscure seasonal variability and variety-by-environment (GxE) interactions. Tables 11–13 provide direct comparisons between varieties using least significant difference (LSD) analysis at a 95% confidence level, based on single-site MET data. These comparisons are valid but should not be relied upon in isolation. 

Figure 8 summarises grain yield responses to increasing N fertiliser rates (10–150 kg N/ha) across DPIRD-funded trials in the Kwinana Port Zone (including Yerecoin, Wongan Hills, Merredin, York, and Popanyinning) from 2022–24. These results help guide N management strategies for different varieties. 

## **Grain yield – comparisons** 

The benchmark barley varieties for grain yield on non-acidic soils in WA are Beast and Neo CL (Figures 2–5). Beast is the benchmark for low-yielding environments (less than 3 t/ha), while Neo CL leads in high-yielding zones (more than 5 t/ha). Between 3 and 5 t/ha, varieties such as AGT-Bunyip IA, Bigfoot CL, Combat, Cyclops, Granite CL, Laperouse, Maximus CL, Minotaur, PegasusAX, Rosalind, and Titan AX are competitive, with selection depending on herbicide system, disease risk, and agronomic fit. Kirwan CL is the new benchmark for soils with an acidic profile (pHCa below 4.8). Where early vigour and weed competitiveness are primary factors in the variety choice in sub-3 t/ha environments, Beast, Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, Compass, and Titan AX are considerations. Above 3.5 t/ha, Combat and Laperouse are higher yielding than Commodus CL and Compass, and just as effective as weed-suppressive options. While not included in this sowing guide, Banks is worth considering for early sowing opportunities in April due to its longer flowering duration than RGT Planet and Rosalind. Ember is the new alternative to Banks for these early planting scenarios. On soils prone to regular waterlogging, RGT Atlantis is the benchmark. 

To better understand varietal performance across yield environments, NVT presents data in halftonne intervals (“yield groups”) via the Long-Term MET Yield Reporter. Figures 2–4 use linear regression to show how each variety performs as the site mean yield increases. These graphs assess deviations from the site mean and fit either linear or quadratic trends depending on statistical significance. If no trend is significant, the variety’s performance is considered stable across yield levels.

## **Grain quality** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

For feed barley, grain yield remains the primary selection criterion, but agronomic traits such as straw strength and head retention are also important. In contrast, for malt barley, grain quality characteristics, particularly hectolitre weight and grain plumpness, are critical for achieving MALT1 receival standards. These traits become even more important in seasons with a dry finish, where varietal differences in grain quality are amplified. 

As with the grain yield data presented in Figures 2–5, the physical grain qualities (hectolitre weight and retention above a 2.5 mm slotted sieve) of a variety have been plotted relative to the site mean as the site mean increases (Figures 6–7). The deviation from the site mean was similarly assessed for quadratic and linear trends. If neither the quadratic nor the linear trend was significant, the grain quality response of a variety was deemed to run parallel to the site mean quality at the average deviation for that variety. The data used for this analysis has been extracted from the ‘NVT state wide tables of yield and grain quality’ available at nvt.grdc.com.au . 

## **Grain quality – hectolitre weight comparisons** 

Figure 6 compares the hectolitre weight of 3 recently accredited varieties (Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL), 2 varieties currently in Stage 2 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program (Spinnaker and Titan AX), and 2 established malt varieties (Maximus CL and RGT Planet) across WA barley NVT trials for the 2023–24 seasons. Figure 8 compares Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL against the established malt varieties Maximus CL and RGT Planet across 14 DPIRD-funded N management trials. 

In WA barley NVT trials (2023–24), Maximus CL consistently recorded the highest hectolitre weight, outperforming Cyclops, Neo CL, RGT Planet, Spinnaker, and Titan AX (Figure 6). Cyclops averaged ~1.5 kg/hL lower, and Neo CL ~5 kg/hL lower, placing it near the MALT1 threshold of 64 kg/hL. Laperouse was similar to Maximus CL above this threshold. These trends were confirmed in DPIRD N management trials (2022–24) (Figure 8). In summary, Laperouse has a similar likelihood of meeting MALT1 hectolitre weight standards as Maximus CL, Cyclops slightly lower, and Neo CL comparable to RGT Planet. 

**96 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

If Spinnaker and Titan AX achieve malt accreditation and are segregated in WA, hectolitre weight will likely be a key factor limiting their success for MALT1 delivery (Figure 6). Spinnaker averaged about 1 kg/hL heavier than Neo CL across environments but remained approximately 4 kg/hL lighter than Maximus CL. Titan AX was 3–4 kg/hL lighter than Maximus CL. As with Neo CL, environmental conditions during grain filling will strongly influence the probability of Spinnaker and Titan AX meeting MALT1 hectolitre weight standards. 

## **Grain quality – grain plumpness comparisons** 

Figure 7 compares grain retention (percentage of grain remaining on a 2.5 mm slotted sieve) for 3 recently accredited varieties (Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL), 2 varieties in Stage 2 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation Program (Spinnaker and Titan AX), and 2 established malt varieties (Maximus CL and RGT Planet) across WA barley NVT trials for the 2023–24 seasons. Figure 8 compares Cyclops, Laperouse, and Neo CL against the established malt varieties Maximus CL and RGT Planet across 14 DPIRD-funded N management trials. 

In WA barley NVT (2023–24), Laperouse produced slightly plumper grain than Maximus CL across a range of grain plumpness levels, with an average retention of about 2% higher (Figure 7). Neo CL showed retention comparable to Maximus CL, while Cyclops averaged around 3% lower retention than Maximus CL. These trends were confirmed in DPIRD N management trials (2022–24) (Figure 8), suggesting that Cyclops and Laperouse have a similar likelihood of meeting MALT1 retention standards as Maximus CL. 

If accredited and received in WA, retention is unlikely to limit Titan AX’s success for MALT1 delivery, as it is at least as plump, if not plumper, than Maximus CL (Figure 7). Spinnaker, while showing lower retention than Maximus CL, represents a significant improvement over RGT Planet. However, its lower grain plumpness relative to Maximus CL may restrict its ability to meet MALT1 standards in some seasons and environments.

## **Disease and pest resistance** 

**Sanjiv Gupta (Murdoch University), Geoff Thomas, Blakely Paynter, Carla Wilkinson, Sarah Collins, Daniel Huberli, Kithsiri Jayasena, and Andrea Hills (DPIRD)** 

## **Foliar disease abbreviations:** 

- **BLR** = barley leaf rust 

- **BP** = black point 

- **BYD/CYD** = barley yellow dwarf/cereal yellow dwarf 

- **CCN** = cereal cyst nematode 

## **Fungicide abbreviations:** 

   - **DMI** = demethylation inhibitors (Group 3) 

   - **SDHI** = succinate dehydrogenase inhibitors (Group 7) 

   - **QoI** = quinone outside inhibitors (Group 11) 

- **CR** = crown rot 

- **NFNB** = net-form net blotch 

- **PM** = powdery mildew 

- **PLS** = physiological leaf spotting 

## **Seedling and adult resistance** 

- **RLS** = ramularia leaf spot 

- **RLN** = root lesion nematode 

- **Sc** = scald 

- **SFNB** = spot-form net blotch 

## **Disease resistance abbreviations:** 

- **VS** = very susceptible 

- **SVS** = susceptible to very susceptible 

- **S** = susceptible 

- **MSS** = moderately susceptible to susceptible 

- **MS** = moderately susceptible 

- **MRMS** = moderately resistant to moderately susceptible 

- **MR** = moderately resistant 

- **RMR** = resistant to moderately resistant 

- **R** = resistant 

- # = reaction may differ in the presence of alternative pathotypes 

- **APR** = adult plant resistance 

- _p_ = provisional rating 

_Refer to page 4 to interpret resistance classifications._ 

Resistance data for fungal, viral, and nematode diseases, as well as germ-end staining (black point), are presented in Tables 14–16 and the variety snapshots. Leaf disease ratings in this guide include seedling and adult plant stage resistance ratings for the fungal diseases NFNB, SFNB, PM and BLR. There is only adult plant stage resistance data for Sc, BYD/CYD and BP. 

Seedling resistance applies to early growth stages (typically 2–3 leaf stage) and is critical for decisions on seed or fertiliser applied fungicides. Adult plant resistance becomes relevant from late tillering to flag leaf emergence, depending on the disease and variety. Differences between seedling and adult ratings reflect the stage-specific expression of resistance genes. Varieties that are susceptible to stubble-borne diseases, such as Sc, NFNB, and SFNB, face a higher risk of infection when sown into barley stubble that is 1–2 years old. 

Variety disease ratings may change over time due to seasonal disease pressure, regional spread, climate variability, stubble retention, and the emergence of new pathotypes or races. While minor adjustments to resistance scores may occur between sowing guide editions, no significant changes have been made in response to new pathotypes. However, emerging pathotypes are influencing field observations for certain varieties. In Tables 1–2 and 14–15, varieties showing variable field reactions are marked with an asterisk (#) following their resistance score for the relevant pathotype. 

**100 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Changing pathotypes in scald (Sc)** 

A virulent Sc pathotype affecting Spartacus CL, Maximus CL, and Neo CL has become more widespread over the past 2 seasons. This new pathotype is also affecting Cyclops, La Trobe, RGT Atlantis, RGT Planet, and Zena CL. Growers should manage Sc based on observed disease levels, that is, treat what you see, regardless of the resistance rating of the variety. Growers are encouraged to report suspected cases of resistance failure to support ongoing monitoring and inform future breeding and management decisions. 

## **New pathotype of net-form net blotch (NFNB)** 

Recent surveys highlight significant variability in the NFNB pathogen, with the Oxford virulent pathotype—and its variants—posing a serious threat to varieties such as Neo CL, RGT Atlantis, RGT Planet, and Spinnaker. Many varieties remain susceptible at the seedling stage, increasing the risk of early-season infection. Adult plant ratings of MS or MSS should be interpreted cautiously in high-pressure regions. Resistance data is detailed in Tables 14–15 and should inform variety selection in areas with known Oxford virulent NFNB presence. 

## **New virulence to powdery mildew (PM)** 

A new PM pathotype has increased seedling and adult susceptibility on Beast, Combat, Commodus CL, and Maximus CL. This new pathotype may also affect Bigfoot CL, Granite CL, Kirwan CL, Laperouse, Rocket CL, and Titan AX. Growers should report unexpected PM outbreaks, especially in varieties rated MRMS or better. Early detection and reporting are crucial for supporting ongoing surveillance and informing future breeding and management strategies. 

## **Pathotype surveillance and fungicide resistance** 

Growers and consultants who observe higherthan-expected leaf disease levels in barley varieties rated as MRMS, MR, or R should collect infected leaf samples for pathotype identification and fungicide resistance testing. To ensure sample viability, collect samples before applying fungicides. 

Place infected Sc, NFNB, SFNB and BLR leaf material in paper envelopes marked with the location, variety, disease and date collected. Fold the leaf in half so the infected area is on the inside. Please do not wrap leaf material in plastic or send it in plastic-lined envelopes. Unlike other leaf diseases, PM-infected leaves should be placed into agar tubes to maintain a live culture for pathotyping. Sample collection kits for PM must be arranged before sampling and, therefore, before spraying. 

Send leaf material infected with NFNB and SFNB in paper envelopes to DPIRD, 1 Nash Street, Perth WA 6000, marked attention, Simon Rogers. For more information, contact Simon Rogers via email at simon.rogers@dpird.wa.gov.au or by phone at +61 429 151 665. 

DPIRD has no funding for assessing virulence changes in Sc or PM in WA. If there is an unexpected susceptible response to Sc or PM of a resistant variety, growers and consultants should contact DPIRD plant pathologists or barley scientists for advice before sending any samples. 

Send BLR samples in paper envelopes directly to the Australian Cereal Rust Survey, The University of Sydney, Reply Paid 88076, Narellan NSW 2567. For more information on sample collection and submission, contact Matthew Williams (Australian Cereal Rust Control Program (ACRCP) Operations matthew. and Technical Officer) via email at williams@sydney.edu.au or by phone at +61 409 552 349, or go to the Australian Cereal - Rust Survey website ( sydney.edu.au/science/our research/research-areas/life-and-environmentalsciences/cereal-rust-research/rust-reports ). 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 101** 

Barley 

To manage fungicide resistance and reduce future resistance development, growers should follow the disease management principles of the Australian Fungicide Resistance Extension Network (AFREN): 

- avoid susceptible crop varieties 

- rotate crops and varieties 

- use non-chemical control methods 

- spray strategically and only if necessary, and 

- rotate and mix fungicide groups. 

For more detailed information on fungicide resistance (incidence and management), see the AFREN website ( afren.com.au/ ). 

When disease response to fungicide control in barley crops is of concern, samples can be sent to Fran Lopez-Ruiz, Centre for Crop and Disease Management (CCDM), Curtin University, PO Box U1987, Perth WA 6845. Contact the Fungicide Resistance Group at frg@curtin.edu.au for details on collecting and submitting a sample. 

## **Scald (Sc)** 

Scald (Sc) is a severe foliar disease of barley caused by _Rhynchosporium secalis_ . It typically begins as pale grey-green, water-soaked blotches on older leaves. These lesions elongate into diamond-shaped patterns with a distinct brown margin and bleached centre. As the disease progresses, lesions coalesce into large necrotic areas, leading to premature leaf death and reduced photosynthetic capacity. Sc can significantly reduce barley performance by killing leaves prematurely (reducing photosynthetic capacity), lowering kernel weight, reducing the number of tillers and grain number per ear (especially with early infections). Yield losses of up to 45% are possible in S to VS varieties, often accompanied by quality defects. The disease survives between seasons on infected stubble, barley grass, and infected seed, making it a persistent threat in barley-on-barley rotations. 

Sc hot spots are apparent in WA, with wide variability in pathotypes. Varieties at highest risk are Beast (S), Bigfoot CL (S), Combat (S), Kirwan CL (S _p_ ), Laperouse (S), Litmus (SVS), Minotaur (VS), and Titan AX (S). Sc has also been reported in varieties such as Spartacus CL (MR), Maximus CL (MR), Neo CL (MRMS), and Zena CL (MR). Where disease reaches damaging levels, management should be undertaken regardless of the variety ranking. 



_Net-form blotch net blotch_ 

Effective management includes using registered seed dressings, applying in-crop fungicides, and avoiding sowing barley into barley stubble. Although currently just under a fifth of the barley area is planted with varieties rated MS or lower, the increased adoption of S and VS varieties, along with the emergence of new pathotypes, could lead to a resurgence of Sc as a significant disease in WA. With infections now appearing in varieties rated MRMS or better (for example, Maximus CL and Neo CL), the risk is expected to rise in the coming seasons. 

## **Net-form net blotch (NFNB)** 

Net-form net blotch (NFNB), caused by _Pyrenophora teres f. teres_ , is a major foliar disease of barley. It begins as pinpoint brown lesions that elongate into fine, dark brown streaks, forming a distinctive net-like pattern across the leaf blade. As lesions mature, they continue to elongate along the veins, reducing green leaf area and photosynthetic capacity. This disease can cause grain yield losses of 20–30% and negatively impact grain quality. Risk is significantly increased in barley-on-barley rotations, making fungicide intervention essential in high-risk environments, particularly for susceptible varieties or when pathotype shifts occur. 

Alarmingly, NFNB isolates with resistance to 3 fungicide groups have been detected in the South Stirlings region. CCDM has detected NFNB with resistance or reduced sensitivity to Group 3 DMI (for example, prothioconazole), Group 7 SDHI (for example, fluxapyroxad, benzovindiflupyr, and bixafen), and Group 11 QoI (for example, azoxystrobin) fungicides. Within the Group 11 

**102 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

QoL fungicides, there is some evidence that the resistance detected doesn’t equally affect pyraclostrobin as severely as it affects azoxystrobin. These findings may compromise fungicide reliability. Integrated disease management is now essential to maintain control efficacy. 

NFNB virulence varies by region and over time, influenced by varietal resistance genes in use. Historically, the Beecher avirulent (97NB1) pathotype has dominated across WA. However, the highly aggressive Oxford virulent pathotype is now widely distributed. RGT Planet is particularly susceptible to the Oxford virulent pathotype, with outbreaks in high-rainfall areas proving difficult to manage with fungicides. Varieties with better adult resistance (MRMS or MRMS _p_ ) to the Oxford virulent pathotype include Bigfoot CL, Ember, Kirwan CL, and PegasusAX, while most other varieties range from MS to SVS. 

Most barley varieties remain susceptible at the seedling stage, thereby increasing the risk of earlyseason infection and canopy spread. Currently, under a fifth of the planted area is sown to barley varieties rated as MS or lower to the Beecher avirulent pathotype as an adult plant. However, nearly all the barley area is planted with varieties rated MS or lower to the Oxford virulent pathotype, with outbreaks which increases the risk of widespread infection. To reduce annual NFNB risk, growers should prioritise fungicide programs (with resistance management in mind), crop rotation, and reducing the area sown to highly susceptible varieties. Sowing barley-on-barley significantly increases the risk. 

## **Spot-form net blotch (SFNB)** 

Spot-form net blotch (SFNB), caused by _Pyrenophora teres f. maculata_ , is a common foliar disease of barley. It develops as small, circular or elliptical dark brown spots that become surrounded by a chlorotic zone of varying width. Unlike NFNB, these spots do not elongate into net-like patterns. Spots typically enlarge to 3–6 mm in diameter. SFNB can cause grain yield losses of 10–50% and reduce grain quality. Double-cropping of barley significantly increases the risk of infection. 

SFNB populations in WA’s Albany, Esperance, and Kwinana-West Port Zones have shown resistance or reduced sensitivity to key fungicide groups. The most affected compounds are Group 3 DMI fungicides, including epoxiconazole, 

propiconazole, and tebuconazole, as well as Group 7 SDHI fungicides, particularly fluxapyroxad. Where fungicide resistance is suspected, growers are encouraged to submit samples to the CCDM for assessment. 

To manage SFNB and slow further resistance development, growers should adopt fungicide mixtures and rotation strategies that incorporate effective DMIs alongside alternative modes of action, such as Group 7 SDHIs (for example, fluxapyroxad and bixafen) and Group 11 QoIs (for example, azoxystrobin and pyraclostrobin). Where fungicide resistance is suspected, samples should be submitted to CCDM for testing. 

Varieties with the most effective combined seedling and adult resistance include AGT-Bunyip IA (MRMS), Combat (MR/MRMS), and Neo CL (MRMS _p_ ) (Tables 14–15). Partial tolerance (MSS or better) at both stages reduces the likelihood of severe infection, but SFNB can still establish under high disease pressure, particularly in barley stubble situations. 

Just over 80% of the barley area is planted to varieties rated MS or lower for SFNB at the adult stage. To reduce annual disease risk, growers should continue to implement fungicide programs, crop rotation, and limit the acreage of susceptible varieties. Sowing barley-on-barley significantly increases the risk. 

## **Powdery mildew (PM)** 

Powdery mildew (PM), caused by _Blumeria graminis f. sp. hordei_ , appears as fluffy white fungal growth on the surface of barley leaves. As the fungus extracts nutrients from the leaf, the surrounding tissue turns a yellowish colour. Over time, older infections become grey and may develop small black fruiting bodies. Early infection can cause yield losses of up to 25%, while later infections (post-stem elongation) typically reduce yield by around 10%. 

The most effective long-term strategy for managing PM is genetic resistance, which is increasingly vital due to the emergence of _CYP51_ gene mutations in the PM pathogen. These mutations have reduced the efficacy of several Group 3 DMI fungicides (for example, tebuconazole, triadimefon, and flutriafol) at label rates. Newer Group 3 DMIs and alternative modes of action, such as Group 5 amines (for example, spiroxamine), Group 7 SDHIs (for example, fluxapyroxad), and Group 11 QoIs (for example, azoxystrobin and pyraclostrobin), remain effective. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 103** 

Barley 



_Powdery mildew_ 

Varieties grown in WA with intermediate resistance or better (MRMS, MR, R) to PM fall into nine broad groups based on postulated or known effective resistance genes. This genetic diversity reduces the likelihood that all varieties will become susceptible simultaneously if new virulence mutations emerge. However, only varieties carrying the _mlo_ gene, such as RGT Atlantis, RGT Planet, and Zena CL, have durable resistance to PM. Most other widely grown varieties remain vulnerable to evolving PM pathotypes. 

Field scouting indicates increasing variation in the PM pathogen, including confirmed virulence to the _MlLa_ gene in southern WA. The variety Rosalind is significantly affected by this new virulence, while other varieties such as Beast, Combat, Commodus CL, Compass, and Maximus CL have shown variable responses. Close monitoring of these varieties is recommended in seasons and environments that favour mildew. 

Currently, less than a tenth of the planted area is sown to varieties rated as MS or lower to PM as adult plants. Still, new pathotypes are emerging that are defeating resistance genes in varieties previously carrying tolerance to PM (e.g. Combat and Maximus CL). To reduce annual PM risk, growers should continue to use sustainable fungicide programs and limit the acreage of susceptible varieties. 

## **Barley leaf rust (BLR)** 

Barley leaf rust (BLR), caused by _Puccinia hordei_ , appears as small, circular to oval pustules containing light-brown powdery spores on the upper surface of leaves. In severe infections, pustules may also develop on leaf sheaths. As the crop matures, these pustules darken, producing black spores that are embedded in the leaf tissue. Severe BLR infections can lead to yield losses exceeding 30%. 

Among current varieties, Rosalind (MR) has the best rating, followed by Combat, Ember, PegasusAX, RGT Atlantis, RGT Planet, and Zena CL, all of which are rated MRMS. Several of these varieties, including Combat, RGT Atlantis, RGT Planet, and Zena CL, rely on adult plant resistance (APR), which often expresses late in the season. The effectiveness of APR can vary with temperature and the genetic background of the variety. 

APR genes are not pathotype-specific, making them less vulnerable to future pathotype changes. However, APR develops fully only at the adult plant stage, typically after flag leaf emergence, though earlier expression can occur in some seasons. Until APR is fully active, these varieties remain susceptible to early rust infection, and fungicide protection may be necessary during early growth stages. 

Currently, the majority of the barley area is planted to varieties rated MS or lower for BLR at the adult stage. To reduce BLR risk, growers should limit the planting of susceptible varieties, apply fungicides strategically, and monitor varietal performance, particularly in environments prone to rust. 

## **Ramularia leaf spot (RLS)** 

Ramularia leaf spot (RLS) is caused by the fungus _Ramularia collo-cygni_ . It was first detected in WA in 2018. RLS typically becomes visible after flowering, making early detection difficult. Lesions can resemble those caused by SFNB, PLS and boron toxicity. Abiotic symptoms, such as PLS, do not respond to fungicides, making an accurate diagnosis essential. Where established, RLS can cause yield losses of up to 25%. 

Infected seed is the primary source of long-distance spread. Localised spread occurs via airborne spores from infected barley and grasses. Spread is favoured by prolonged leaf wetness, making medium- and high-rainfall areas more vulnerable. 

**104 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 



_Ramularia leaf spot_ 

Varietal responses to RLS are only available from the NVT ( nvt.grdc.com.au ) and are based primarily on NSW outbreaks. 

There are no specific management recommendations for the disease in WA. However, the fungicides currently used to manage net blotches in barley are likely to be active on RLS when applied at the booting stage. Two products are registered for RLS management, Revystar[®] (mefentrifluconazole + fluxapyroxad) and Elatus[®] Ace (benzovindiflupyr + propiconazole). As RLS is at high risk of developing fungicide resistance, sustainable rotation of fungicides should always be practised. 

## **Crown rot (CR)** 

Crown rot (CR), caused by _Fusarium pseudograminearum_ , is a stubble-borne fungal disease most common in cereal-based rotations. It infects the sub-crown internode, crown, and lower stems, and is typically not visible until after heading, when whiteheads may appear. Unlike take-all, whiteheads caused by CR are usually scattered throughout the crop rather than appearing in patches. 

Diagnostic features include honey-brown discolouration at the base of infected tillers, often visible under leaf sheaths. Pink discolouration may also occur around the crown or beneath leaf sheaths. Browning at the tiller base is the most reliable indicator, particularly in seasons with good spring rainfall when whiteheads may not develop. Infected heads often contain shrivelled or no grain. 

Management options are limited. Seed dressings are registered to suppress CR, but no foliar fungicides are available once the crop is established. The most effective strategies include rotating with non-cereal crops (for example, pulses, oilseeds, lupins, or grass-free pastures) to reduce inoculum, inter-row seeding to minimise contact with infected stubble, and maintaining grass weed control in break crops and fallows. 

Varietal resistance and tolerance to CR are limited. DPIRD research in WA indicates that while varietal differences exist, most barley varieties are susceptible and suffer yield loss under high CR pressure. Of the varieties tested, Litmus has shown the lowest yield loss in the presence of severe CR infection. 

## **Black point (BP)** 

Black point (BP), also known as germ-end staining, refers to a dark discolouration at the embryo end of the grain, historically attributed to fungal colonisation. However, Australian research has not confirmed a link to fungi. Instead, the darkening is believed to result from biochemical reactions, particularly involving peroxidase enzymes, during the grain-filling period, especially between the milk and dough stages of kernel development. 

The BP varietal scores in Table 16 are sourced from SARDI and published on the GRDC NVT website ( nvt.grdc.com.au/nvt-disease-ratings ). The scoring methodology involves assessing the level of BP on 500 grains per replicate from selected GRDC barley NVT and then annualising varietal rankings against checks and historical data. Genetic variation exists. Minotaur, Neo CL, RGT Atlantis, RGT Planet, Spinnaker and Zena CL at a lower BP risk (rated as MRMS or better). Despite the availability of moderately resistant varieties, less than a fifth of the planted barley area is currently sown to varieties rated MRMS or better for BP. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 105** 

Barley 

## **Barley and cereal yellow dwarf (BYD/CYD)** 

Both barley yellow dwarf (BYD) and cereal yellow dwarf (CYD) viruses are present in WA, with BYD occurring approximately twice as frequently as CYD. Resistance screening is conducted under field conditions, so varietal resistance scores reflect the combined impact of both viruses. Early seedling infection by BYD can result in yield losses of up to 80%. Later infections (post-tillering) may reduce yield by up to 20%. Infected plants often exhibit stunted growth, leaf yellowing, and reduced tillering. Barley plants are primarily infected by aphids, including those of the species _Rhopalosiphum padi_ (oat aphids) or _Rhopalosiphum maidis_ (corn leaf aphids). 

Varietal resistance helps reduce the impact of the virus but does not prevent aphid feeding damage. Even in resistant varieties, aphid control is essential to prevent yield loss. Aphids must be sprayed once they reach threshold levels in the crop (50% of tillers with 15 or more aphids) to prevent yield loss from feeding damage. 

## **Root lesion nematode (RLN)** 

Root lesion nematodes (RLN) caused by _Pratylenchus species_ , are microscopic, wormlike organisms that feed on plant roots, reducing water and nutrient uptake and causing yield losses in susceptible crops such as barley, wheat, and canola. RLN populations increase when susceptible crops and varieties are repeatedly grown, thereby raising the risk of economic damage. In WA, RLN infestations occur across approximately 6.25 million hectares, covering nearly 74% of the winter cropping area. The most common species is _Pratylenchus neglectus_ (found in 70% of paddocks), followed by _Pratylenchus quasitereoides_ . Many paddocks contain multiple RLN species. These nematodes can limit barley and wheat yields in over 50% of infested paddocks, with yield losses of up to 18% in barley. 

Effective RLN management begins with identifying paddocks with yield-limiting nematode populations. Key strategies include rotating with resistant crops, using resistant varieties, or implementing fallow periods to reduce nematode numbers. Incorporating non-host crops such as 

pulses, oilseeds, lupins, or grass-free pastures can suppress RLN populations. Additionally, understanding the species-specific feeding preferences of nematode species allows for more effective and tailored crop rotations. 

The resistance scores in this guide are based on glasshouse and field trials conducted in WA. Varieties tested in fewer than 5 trials or lacking field verification of glasshouse ratings are assigned provisional ratings. 

## **Cereal cyst nematode (CCN)** 

Cereal cyst nematode (CCN), caused by _Heterodera australis_ , is present in cropping regions around Geraldton, Esperance, and the Avon Valley, but can occur sporadically across the WA grain belt. CCN feeds on cereals and grasses, causing significant yield losses in wheat and oats. Barley is more tolerant, and yield loss is generally limited, even when infection occurs. Planting CCN-resistant wheat and barley varieties helps suppress the development of nematodes. Rotating with grass-free legumes or pasture reduces the populations of CCN in the soil. These practices help retard nematode development and lower the risk of future crop damage. CCN resistance ratings in this guide are not based on WA trials. Ratings should be considered indicative only, and not definitive for WA conditions. 



_Cereal cyst nematode_

## **Variety snapshots** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

## **Variety snapshots are presented for:** 

- **Malt varieties to be segregated in WA at the 2026–27 harvest (5):** Cyclops, Laperouse, Maximus CL, Neo CL and RGT Planet. These are deliverable into malt barley segregations as per the GIWA Western Australian malt barley variety receival recommendations for the 2026–27 harvest. 

- **Stage 2 malt accreditation (2):** Spinnaker and Titan AX are undergoing Stage 2 malting and brewing assessment with Grains Australia. These are deliverable into feed barley segregations unless they become part of the GIWA malt barley receival recommendations. 

- **Deliverable as a feed variety (21):** AGT-Bunyip IA, Beast, Bigfoot CL, Buff, Combat, Commodus CL, Compass, Ember, Granite CL, Kirwan CL, La Trobe, Litmus, Minotaur, PegasusAX, RGT Atlantis, Rocket CL, Rosalind, Scope CL, Soldier CL, Spartacus CL and Zena CL. These are deliverable into feed barley segregations. 

Each snapshot includes key agronomic and quality traits, highlighting yield performance, strengths, and weaknesses. Yield comparisons are based on data from the Long Term MET Yield Reporter (available at NVT online, nvt.grdc.com.au ), presented relative to a control variety, typically Maximus CL, rather than the site mean yield shown in Tables 4–10. Additionally, single-site MET data presented in Tables 11–13 is used to indicate the likelihood of a variety yielding less, the same, or more than another when grown under identical agronomic conditions. 

Disease resistance ratings for grain varieties are primarily sourced from DPIRD under a service agreement with GRDC for the NVT system. Where data gaps exist, supplementary information may be obtained from AGT, InterGrain, RAGT and SECOBRA Recherches. Ratings for diseases and nematodes are presented for adult plant stages (where available) and are based on data from Tables 14–16. 

Phenology data is generated using DPIRD’s predictive model, FlowerPower barley ( fp.dpird. app/ ). This statistical tool estimates the date of the awn emergence (Z49) for barley in 2 WA environments, Northam and Katanning, based on historical temperature data (sourced from the SILO database: ( longpaddock.qld.gov.au/silo/ point-data/ ). 

Predictions are provided for 4 sowing dates: 15 April, 5 May, 25 May and 15 June. The phenology data shown in the snapshots reflect the median predicted date to Z49 (that is, the date expected in 50% of seasons), based on FlowerPower barley version v2025.02.24. Varieties have been graded on their phenology relative to Stirling, where very early = -15 to -4 days, early = -3 to +3 days, medium = +4 to +10 days and late = +11 to +17 days after Stirling when sown on 5 May at Northam. Maturity ranking with an early May sowing may differ from that when crops are planted in April or after mid-June. Where DPIRD collected data is not yet available in ‘FlowerPower’, unpublished or breeder information is used. 

The comment section in each snapshot describes essential varietal characteristics, including yield relative to other varieties, key weaknesses and strengths (including, where appropriate, disease resistance, straw strength, and head loss), and relevant market information for varieties segregated as malt. 

Agronomic characteristics are compiled from multiple sources, including DPIRD trials, GRDC co-funded projects (DAW00190 and DAW00224) and, in some cases, from the breeder. Traits covered include: 

- **Coleoptile length:** short = 40–60 mm, medium = 60–80 mm, long = 80–100 mm, very long = 100–120 mm and extremely long ≥120 mm. At DPIRD, the coleoptile lengths were measured after germinating seeds in rolled, moistened filter paper for 15 days at 15 °C in the dark. The data was sourced from DPIRD and breeder brochures. 

- **Plant height:** plant height to the base of the ear (cm) at maturity at sites where the straw of control varieties (for example, Compass, Scope CL, and Stirling) was 65–75 cm long. Very short ≤45 cm, short = 45–55 cm, medium = 55–65 cm and tall = 65–75 cm. Data from DPIRD-GRDC, DPIRD internal agronomy trials and breeder brochures. 

**110 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

- **Lodging resistance:** straw strength is based on lodging scores taken at maturity and ranked relative to control varieties. Data from DPIRD-GRDC, DPIRD internal agronomy trials and breeder brochures. 

- **Head loss:** head loss risk, assessed in small plot trials and ranked based on counting heads post-harvest at sites where high levels of head loss were recorded in high-risk varieties (for example, Scope CL). Data from DPIRD–GRDC, DPIRD internal agronomy trials and breeder brochures. 

- **Grain protein:** grain protein deviation (relative to the average) as calculated and ranked using NVT (2005–24) and DPIRD-GRDCfunded barley agronomy trials (2006–24). Lower = <-0.3%, slightly lower = -0.3 to -0.1%, average = -0.1 to +0.1%, slightly higher = +0.1 to +0.3% and higher = > +0.3%. Grain protein deviation analyses the relationship between 

grain yield and grain protein concentration in barley varieties grown under similar management and environmental conditions in WA. A typical relationship exists in which grain yield increases and grain protein concentration decreases (yield dilution). Deviations from the relationship between grain yield and grain protein were used to classify varieties based on their grain protein deviation and determine relative levels of inherent grain protein concentration. 

Details on seed licensees, trading restrictions, and End Point Royalties (EPRs) are sourced from breeding companies, Variety Central ( varietycentral.com.au/ ) and IP Australia’s Plant Breeders’ Rights database ( ipsearch.ipaustralia. gov.au/pbr/ ).

Barley<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>

## **C clo s**[A] **y p** 

**Deliverable as a malt variety** 

## **Comments** 

Cyclops (AGTB0200) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley. It is being assessed for multiple export opportunities, including grain, malt, and shochu production in Japan. Cyclops does not carry tolerance to Clearfield[®] or CoAXium[®] herbicides. It has a short coleoptile and should not be sown deep. Cyclops shares the erect growth habit of Maximus CL but has different genetics. Its early vigour and plant height are comparable to Maximus CL, although it is more prone to lodging under high nitrogen input. Across 134 WA barley NVT trials (2020–24), Cyclops yielded less than Maximus CL in 18% of trials, the same in 33%, and higher in 49%. It is a higher-yielding option in environments producing more than 4 t/ha. Cyclops provides valuable resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), and PM. However, management is required for NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR, as well as emerging pathotypes of Sc. In terms of grain quality, Cyclops has similar grain plumpness to Maximus CL but a lower hectolitre weight, suggesting a slightly lower probability of meeting MALT1 receival standards. Target production zones for 2026 include limited segregations in the Albany and Esperance port zones. 

## **La erouse**[A] **p** 

## **Deliverable as a malt variety** 

## **Comments** 

Laperouse (WI4592) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley. It is being assessed for multiple export opportunities, including grain, malt, and shochu production in Japan. Laperouse offers good straw strength and head retention but does not carry tolerance to Clearfield[®] or CoAXium[®] herbicide technologies. Across 134 WA barley NVT trials (2020–24), Laperouse yielded less than Maximus CL in 32% of trials, matched it in 43%, and outperformed it in 25%. Yield comparisons indicate that Laperouse and Maximus CL perform similarly across a range of site yields. Grain quality assessments show that Laperouse generally achieves a hectolitre weight comparable to Maximus CL, with slightly improved grain retention and a lower grain protein concentration at equivalent yields. These characteristics suggest that Laperouse has a similar likelihood of meeting MALT1 receival standards as Maximus CL. Laperouse provides good tolerance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent) but requires management for Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR and emerging pathotypes of PM. Target production zones for 2026 include limited segregations in the Kwinana-South and Albany port zones.

**112 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Maximus**[A ] **CL** 

## **Neo**[A ] **CL** 

## **Deliverable as a malt variety** 

## **Comments** 

Maximus CL (IGB1705T) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row malt barley. It is accepted for export as both grain and malt and is preferred for shochu production in Japan. Maximus CL has a short coleoptile and should not be sown deep. Across 55 WA barley NVT trials (2023–24), Maximus CL yielded less than Neo CL in 38% of trials, the same in 16%, and higher in 45%, performing better in environments yielding less than 3 t/ha. Maximus CL is considered the new benchmark for grain quality, except for BP. It produces plumper grain than Spartacus CL with a similar hectolitre weight, resulting in a higher probability of meeting MALT1 receival standards. Growers should use only recommended imidazolinone herbicides and remain aware of market advice regarding grain delivery from paddocks treated with these products. Maximus CL is most at risk from NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR, with increasing reports of changing Sc and PM virulence on this variety. Maximus CL should be grown using non-chemical control methods to reduce disease pressure, planted in rotations that minimise disease carryover, and sprayed only if necessary, in a strategic manner with different modes of action. It appears to have a low risk of head loss. Target production zones in 2026 include Kwinana, Albany and Esperance port zones. 

## **Deliverable as a malt variety** 

## **Comments** 

Neo CL (IGB22102T) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley. It is currently being evaluated for multiple export markets, including grain, malt, and shochu production in Japan. The variety carries tolerance to imidazolinone herbicides; however, growers should use only recommended products and remain informed about market requirements for grain delivery from treated paddocks. It demonstrates early vigour comparable to RGT Planet but is shorter in height at maturity and offers improved lodging resistance. In 55 WA NVT barley trials (2023–24), Neo CL yielded lower than Maximus CL in 45% of cases, equal in 16%, and higher in 38%. It exhibits a yield advantage over Maximus CL at high-yielding sites (greater than 5 t/ha), but tends to underperform when site yields fall below 3 t/ha. The likelihood of meeting MALT1 receival standards is expected to be lower than Maximus CL (due to lower hectolitre weight) but higher than RGT Planet (due to improved retention). Neo CL provides useful resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), SFNB, and PM. However, it may require management for Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), and BLR. Target production zones for 2026 include the Albany and Esperance port zones, with limited segregation available in the Kwinana-South region.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 113** 

Barley 

## **RGT Planet**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a malt variety** 

## **Comments** 

RGT Planet (SFR85-104) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley. It is preferred for export malt due to its consistent malting performance without the need for gibberellic acid, offering processing efficiencies for maltsters. However, its initial advantages in WA have diminished due to its phenotype not matching recent seasonal conditions, the release of newer varieties, and increasing disease susceptibility, leading to a decline in grower adoption. Across 134 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), RGT Planet yielded less than Maximus CL in 63% of cases, matched in 14%, and exceeded in 23%. On average, it yields approximately a third of a tonne less than Maximus CL across varying site potentials. Effective disease management is essential, particularly for NFNB and SFNB, to which it is susceptible, as well as emerging pathotypes of Sc. RGT Planet should be grown using integrated disease management strategies, including crop rotation and strategic fungicide use with varied modes of action. Currently, the supply of MALT1-grade RGT Planet is insufficient to meet end-user demand. Target production zones for 2026 are limited to niche or on-farm segregations within the Kwinana-South port zone. 

## **S innaker**[A] **p** 

## **Stage 2 malt accreditation** 

## **Comments** 

Spinnaker (SCA21-Y003) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row barley. It is currently undergoing Stage 2 Malt Accreditation with Grains Australia, with the earliest possible accreditation date in March 2026. End-user interest is emerging, as Spinnaker appears to offer a malt quality profile similar to RGT Planet and may be malted without the use of processing aids. Spinnaker combines good straw strength with excellent head retention but does not carry tolerance to Clearfield[®] or CoAXium[®] herbicides. In 97 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), it yielded less than Maximus CL in 56% of cases, matched in 14%, and exceeded in 30%. Across varying NVT site potentials, Spinnaker showed lower grain yield, hectolitre weight, and retention compared to Maximus CL. If received, its probability of meeting MALT1 specifications is expected to be lower than Maximus CL but slightly higher than RGT Planet. Like RGT Planet, Spinnaker is sensitive to NFNB and SFNB. Integrated disease management, including crop rotation and strategic fungicide use with varied modes of action, will be necessary to manage diseases effectively. If accredited, Spinnaker may be a viable option for domestic malt supply, particularly in western areas of the Kwinana port zone, if a production premium is offered.

**114 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Titan AX**[A] 

## **AGT-Bun i IA**[A] **y p** 

## **Stage 2 malt accreditation** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

## **Comments** 

Titan AX (AGTB0325) is a tall, medium-spring, 2-row barley, featuring CoAXium[®] herbicide tolerance. It is currently in Stage 2 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation process, with the earliest possible accreditation date in March 2026. Titan AX shares a genetic background with Compass and is best suited to low- to medium-rainfall environments where early vigour, longer straw, and minimal lodging risk are preferred. While it performs well in these conditions, straw strength may be a concern in longer-season or high-yielding environments, with head loss risk expected to be similar to Compass. In 89 WA NVT barley trials (2021–24), Titan AX yielded less than Maximus CL in 40% of cases, matched in 17%, and exceeded in 43%. Overall, the two varieties perform similarly across varying site potentials. Titan AX shows resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent) but will require management for Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR, as well as emerging pathotypes of PM. It has a medium fermentability profile similar to Commodus CL. However, with Commodus CL removed from segregation plans for the 2026–27 harvest due to weak market demand for that fermentability profile, Titan AX may face similar market limitations if accredited and is therefore unlikely to be segregated in WA. 

AGT-Bunyip IA (AGTB0530) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row barley. It is currently undergoing Stage 1 of the Grains Australia Malt Accreditation program, with the earliest possible accreditation date in March 2027. AGT-Bunyip IA is the first barley variety globally to offer dual herbicide tolerance, resistant to both imidazolinone herbicides and Aggressor[®] AX (quizalofop-p-ethyl). This enables sowing into imidazolinone soil residues and in-crop spraying with either herbicide, providing growers greater flexibility in managing residue carryover and controlling key weeds such as brome grass, barley grass, annual ryegrass, wild oats, and volunteer wheat. In 55 WA NVT barley trials (2023–24), AGT-Bunyip IA yielded lower than Maximus CL in 13% of cases, matched in 58%, and exceeded in 29%. Its yield response across environments was consistent with Maximus CL, indicating similar performance as site potential varied. The variety shows moderate resistance to SFNB but may need management for Sc, NFNB, PM, and BLR. A broad-spectrum fungicide program, aligned with integrated disease management practices, will be necessary to maintain crop health.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 115** 

Barley 

## **Beast**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Beast (AGTB0113) is a tall, early-spring, 2-row feed barley developed for low- to medium-rainfall environments. It combines the genetics of Compass and Hindmarsh and shares a similar plant type to Compass, including comparable lodging risk. Beast offers strong early vigour, aiding weed suppression, and is particularly well suited to sub-3 t/ha environments where its taller straw and longer coleoptile provide agronomic advantages. In 134 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), Beast yielded less than Maximus CL in 13% of cases, matched in 39%, and exceeded in 49%. Its performance is strongest in lower-yielding environments. Beast may be vulnerable to Sc, NFNB, SFNB, and BLR. It has good PM tolerance but can show variable responses across WA in the presence of other pathotypes. 

## **Bi foot CL**[A] **g** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Bigfoot CL (AGTB0669) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row feed barley developed as an alternative to imidazolinone-tolerant lines such as Commodus CL, Granite CL, and Maximus CL. It features a long coleoptile, supporting improved establishment under deeper sowing conditions. Bigfoot CL shares a Compass-type plant structure with Commodus CL but offers shorter straw and improved lodging tolerance. In 42 WA NVT barley trials (2023–24), Bigfoot CL outperformed Commodus CL in 69% of cases and matched in 31%. Compared to Maximus CL, it yielded lower in 21% of trials, similar in 52%, and higher in 26%. Bigfoot CL shows useful resistance to NFNB, but will likely require management for Sc, SFNB, and BLR. It may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM.
