**116 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Buff**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Buff (IGB1506) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. It carries aluminium tolerance genetics similar to Litmus but differs in having a white aleurone. Buff has a short coleoptile and may present a moderate risk of head loss. Buff performs competitively with feed and malt varieties on non-acidic soils, except where imidazolinone herbicides are used. In 93 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), Buff yielded higher than Litmus in 62% of cases, matched in 18%, and lower in 20%. Across 134 WA barley NVT (2020–24), Buff has yielded less than Maximus CL in 54%, the same in 27% and higher in 19%. Fungicides may be required to manage Sc, SFNB, PM and BLR. 

## **Combat**[A] **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Combat (IGB1944) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row feed barley suited to a wide range of environments, from low- to high-rainfall zones. It has a semi-erect growth habit, offering improved weed competitiveness compared to Rosalind. Combat is moderately susceptible to lodging and head loss. In 107 WA NVT barley trials (2021–24), Combat outperformed Rosalind in 61% of cases, matched in 24%, and yielded lower in 15%. Against Maximus CL, it yielded higher in 69% of trials, the same in 19%, and lower in 19%. Combat is a yield benchmark in environments exceeding 5 t/ha, second only to Neo CL. It has valuable resistance to SFNB, PM, and BLR (late APR), but requires management for NFNB. Increasing reports of PM infections highlight the need for a broadspectrum fungicide program and integrated disease management.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 117** 

Barley 

## **Commodus**[A] **CL** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Commodus CL (IGB1908T) is a tall-height, early-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. Modelled on Compass, it is suited to low- to medium-rainfall regions and lighter soils. Commodus CL has a medium coleoptile, good early canopy development, and strong ground coverage for weed suppression. However, it is susceptible to lodging and head loss. Across 134 WA barley NVT (2020–24), Commodus CL yielded less than Maximus CL in 58% of trials, the same in 31% and higher in 11%. It tends to underperform relative to Maximus CL when site yields exceed 4 t/ha. It has valuable resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), but may require management for Sc, STNB, and BLR. Emerging virulence shifts are increasing its susceptibility to PM. 

## **Com ass**[A] **p** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Compass (WI4593) is a tall-height, early-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. Best suited to low- and medium-rainfall environments with a yield potential below 4 t/ha and where weed-competitive barley is required. Compass is susceptible to lodging, particularly in high-yielding situations. Many newer varieties, including Beast, Bigfoot CL, Combat, Granite CL, Maximus CL, and PegasusAX, have surpassed Compass in performance. In 134 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), Compass yielded less than Beast in 63% of cases, matched in 36%, and exceeded in just 1%. Against Maximus CL, it yielded lower in 44%, equal in 25%, and higher in 31%. Compass underperforms both varieties as site yield increases, falling behind Beast at yields above 2.5 t/ha and Maximus CL at yields above 4 t/ha. Compass requires active integrated disease management for Sc, NFNB, SFNB, and BLR. Emerging virulence shifts are increasing its susceptibility to PM.

**118 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Ember**[A] 

## **Granite**[A] **CL** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Ember (IGB21130) is a medium-height, late-spring, 2-row feed barley. It reaches awn emergence one to two weeks later than Maximus CL and Neo CL, aligning more closely with Baudin and Gairdner under April–May sowing. Ember is best suited to early- to mid-April sowing opportunities and has underperformed in WA NVT trials at standard sowing dates. Further data from April sowing is needed to confirm its fit, but its strong disease resistance makes it a promising option for southern regions with early sowing windows. Ember offers robust resistance to Sc, NFNB, PM, and BLR, and shows strong seedling resistance to NFNB and PM, reducing early disease risk. It is moderately susceptible to SFNB. To maintain crop health, Ember should be grown using integrated disease management practices, including non-chemical controls, strategic fungicide use with varied modes of action, and crop rotations that minimise disease carryover. 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Granite CL (IGB21092T) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row feed barley developed as an imidazolinone-tolerant alternative to Bigfoot CL, Commodus CL, and Maximus CL. It shares the erect plant architecture of the Rosalind family and carries Clearfield[®] herbicide tolerance. In 24 WA NVT barley trials (2024 only, excluding Agzone 4), Granite CL demonstrated comparable grain yield to Bigfoot CL and Maximus CL, and outperformed Commodus CL at sites yielding above 4 t/ha. It yielded similarly to Bigfoot CL in 83% of trials and higher in 17%. Against Maximus CL, it yielded lower in 17%, equal in 50%, and higher in 33%. Granite CL exhibits useful resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent), but will require integrated disease management to manage Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and BLR. It may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 119** 

Barley 

## **Kirwan**[A] **CL** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Kirwan CL (IGB22014T) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row feed barley positioned as an alternative to Buff and Litmus. It offers adaptability to challenging low soil pH soil conditions and carries tolerance to Clearfield[®] herbicides. In 16 WA NVT barley trials (2023, excluding Agzones 3 and 6), Kirwan CL outperformed Buff in 63% of cases, matched in 31%, and yielded lower in only 6%. Against Litmus, it performed equally in 55% of trials and better in 45%. Kirwan CL has useful (provisional) resistance to NFNB, but will require management for Sc, SFNB and BLR. It may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. A strategic fungicide program, aligned with best-practice integrated disease management, is recommended to maintain crop health.

## **La Trobe**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

La Trobe (IGB1101) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. While once widely grown, La Trobe has been surpassed in performance by newer varieties such as Beast, Bigfoot CL, Combat, Granite CL, Maximus CL, and PegasusAX. In 134 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), La Trobe yielded less than Beast in 83% of cases, matched in 16%, and exceeded in just 1%. Against Maximus CL, it yielded lower in 54%, equal in 40%, and higher in 7%. La Trobe underperforms both varieties as site yield increases, falling behind Beast above 1 t/ha and Maximus CL above 2.5 t/ha. An integrated disease management package will be required to manage NFNB, SFNB, PM, and BLR. Emerging virulence shifts are increasing its susceptibility to Sc.

**120 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Litmus**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Litmus (WABAR2625) is a tall, very early-spring, 2-row feed barley with enhanced tolerance to low pH and high aluminium soils, making it well-suited to environments where the soil pHCa at 10–30 cm is below 4.8. Buff and Kirwan CL are higher-yielding alternatives on most acidic and non-acidic soils. On more acutely acidic soils, Litmus may be a better choice. In 93 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), Litmus yielded less than Buff in 62% of cases, matched in 18%, and exceeded in 20%. In 11 WA NVT trials (2023 only), Litmus yielded less than Kirwan CL in 45% of cases and matched in 55%. Litmus has fair straw strength and is susceptible to all major leaf diseases except PM. However, it demonstrates the lowest yield loss in the presence of crown rot. An integrated disease management program is required to manage Sc, NFNB, SFNB, and BLR. Due to its blue aleurone, Litmus is only deliverable to sites equipped to manage blue aleurone in feed barley stacks. 

## **Minotaur**[A] **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Minotaur (AGTB0213) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. It is best suited to medium- to high-rainfall environments. Minotaur features a semi-prostrate growth habit, a long coleoptile for deeper sowing, strong straw strength to reduce lodging risk, and good head retention. Across 134 WA barley NVT (2020–24), Minotaur yielded less than Maximus CL in 33% of trials, the same in 31% and higher in 38%. Relative to Neo CL, across 55 WA barley NVT (2023–24), Minotaur yielded less in 38% of trials, the same in 44% and higher in 18% of trials. Minotaur performs similarly to Maximus CL below 5 t/ha but is outperformed by Neo CL at higher-yielding sites. Minotaur has valuable resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent). It requires an integrated disease management plan for all other leaf diseases, especially Sc.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 121** 

Barley 

## **Pe asusAX**[A] **g** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

PegasusAX (AGTB0667) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row feed barley variety compatible with Aggressor[®] AX herbicide, offering an alternative weed control option to imidazolinone-tolerant varieties. It shares the erect plant architecture of the Rosalind family, with shorter straw, lower lodging risk, and a shorter coleoptile than Titan AX. In 42 WA NVT barley trials (2023–24), PegasusAX yielded similarly to Titan AX in 38% of cases, higher in 45%, and lower in 17%. Against Maximus CL, it yielded lower in 12%, equal in 55%, and higher in 33%. It has demonstrated comparable grain yields to Bigfoot CL, Granite CL, Maximus CL, and Titan AX, and outperforms Commodus CL at sites yielding above 4 t/ha. PegasusAX offers excellent resistance to NFNB, PM, and BLR, but will require integrated disease management for Sc and SFNB. 

## **RGT Atlantis**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

RGT Atlantis (RP22054) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row barley currently undergoing Stage 1 Malt Accreditation with Grains Australia, with the earliest possible accreditation date in March 2027. It is the first commercial Australian barley with enhanced tolerance to transient waterlogging, developed by backcrossing a wild barley gene into RGT Planet to promote aerenchyma formation, allowing oxygen retention in waterlogged roots. RGT Atlantis is targeted for sowing in medium- to high-rainfall environments prone to transient waterlogging. It shares the plant structure, height, disease resistance, and lodging risk of the RGT Planet family but has lower yield potential in the absence of waterlogging. In 37 WA NVT barley trials (2023–24), RGT Atlantis yielded lower than RGT Planet in 62% of cases and similarly in 38%. RGT Atlantis is susceptible to NFNB (Oxford virulent) and SFNB, similar to RGT Planet. Like RGT Planet, it is also showing increased susceptibility to Sc. To maintain crop health, it should be grown using integrated disease management practices, including non-chemical controls, strategic fungicide use with varied modes of action, and crop rotations that minimise disease carryover.

**122 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **Rocket**[A] **CL** 

## **Rosalind**[A] 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Rocket CL (IGB22023T) is a medium-height, very early-spring, 2-row barley currently undergoing Stage 1 Malt Accreditation with Grains Australia, with the earliest possible accreditation date in March 2028. It offers Clearfield[®] herbicide tolerance and is wellsuited to northern wheatbelt environments and planting windows from late May to early June. Rocket CL reaches awn emergence earlier than Beast and Commodus CL, aligning more closely with Mundah under April–May sowing. It is positioned as a potential alternative to Beast and Commodus CL, with improved lodging tolerance and head retention. In 22 WA NVT barley trials (2024, excluding Agzones 3 and 6), Rocket CL outperformed Commodus CL in 55% of cases, matched in 32%, and yielded lower in 14%. Against Beast, it performed equally in 59% of trials and lower in 41%. Rocket CL shows provisional resistance to PM, although it may be susceptible to emerging pathotypes of PM. It is susceptible to Sc, NFNB, SFNB, and BLR. A strategic fungicide program and integrated disease management practices are recommended to maintain crop health. 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Rosalind (IGB1302) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row feed barley variety suitable for all environments. Once the yield benchmark for WA barley, Rosalind has now been surpassed by newer varieties, including Beast, Combat, Cyclops, and Neo CL. Rosalind offers good straw strength and head retention. In 134 WA NVT barley trials (2020–24), it yielded less than Beast in 40% of cases, matched in 36%, and exceeded in 24%, with Beast showing a slight advantage below 3 t/ha. In 107 trials (2021–24), Rosalind yielded less than Combat in 61%, equal in 24%, and higher in 15%, with Combat outperforming Rosalind above 4.5 t/ha. Rosalind requires an integrated disease management program to control Sc, NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB, and PM (where _MlLa_ virulent strains are present). It has useful resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent) and BLR.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 123** 

Barley 

## **Sco e CL**[A] **p** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Scope CL (VBHT0805) is a tall-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. Better adapted than Commodus CL, Maximus CL, and Spartacus CL to April sowing opportunities, and an alternative to Neo CL. Across 93 WA barley NVT (2020–24), Scope CL yielded less than Maximus CL in 85% of trials, the same in 11% and higher in 4%. Across 38 WA barley NVT (2023–24), Scope CL yielded less than Neo CL in 76% of trials, the same in 21% and higher in 3%. Fungicides in an integrated disease management package will be required to manage Sc. NFNB (Oxford virulent), SFNB and BLR. It has useful resistance to NFNB (Beecher avirulent) and PM. It should be harvested when ripe due to a high head loss risk. 

## **Soldier**[A] **CL** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Soldier CL (IGB22117T) is a short-height, early-spring, 2-row barley variety currently undergoing Stage 1 Malt Accreditation with Grains Australia, with the earliest possible accreditation date in March 2028. It offers Clearfield[®] herbicide tolerance and is wellsuited to high-rainfall environments where lodging is a frequent concern. Soldier CL reaches awn emergence at a similar time to Spartacus CL under April–May sowing and is up to a week earlier than Maximus CL and Neo CL. There is no WA barley NVT data available to quote for this sowing guide. It has been released based on InterGrain’s internal trial data (2019–24). Internal results suggest it yields between Neo CL and RGT Planet at site yields up to 7 t/ ha. Growers are encouraged to seek independent trial data before making adoption decisions. NVT consensus disease ratings are not yet available, but InterGrain’s product profile indicates useful resistance to Sc, NFNB, and PM. Integrated disease management is recommended for SFNB and BLR.

**124 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Barley 

## **S artacus CL**[A] **p** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Spartacus CL (IGB1334T) is a medium-height, early-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. It is tolerant to Clearfield[®] herbicides but has a short coleoptile and should not be sown deep. Across 134 WA barley NVT (2020–24), Spartacus CL yielded less than Maximus CL in 74% of trials and the same in 26% of trials. An integrated disease management plan is warranted to manage NFNB, SFNB, PM, and BLR. Emerging virulence shifts are increasing its susceptibility to Sc. 

## **Zena**[A] **CL** 

## **Deliverable as a feed variety** 

## **Comments** 

Zena CL (IGB20125T) is a medium-height, medium-spring, 2-row malt barley that is not segregated in WA and is only deliverable into feed barley segregations. It is targeted for sowing in medium- to high-rainfall zones. It shares the plant structure, height, disease resistance, and lodging risk of the RGT Planet family with identical grain yield but is tolerant of Clearfield[®] herbicides. Across 107 WA barley NVT (2021–24), Zena CL yielded the same as RGT Planet in all 107 trials. Zena CL is susceptible to NFNB (Oxford virulent) and SFNB, similar to RGT Planet. Emerging virulence shifts are increasing its susceptibility to Sc. To maintain crop health, it should be grown using integrated disease management practices, including non-chemical controls, strategic fungicide use with varied modes of action, and crop rotations that minimise disease carryover.

**128 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Canola 

## **Canola** 

## **Introduction** 

## **Bronte Wackett (DPIRD)** 

## **The 2024 season** 

Across all canola series in the 2024 National Variety Trials (NVT), average yields were higher than in 2023 (Tables 2–6). Yields in the mediumhigh rainfall zone ranged from 2.30–3.09 t/ha between the herbicide tolerance groups (Tables 2–4). In the low-medium rainfall zone yields were lower, ranging from 2.16–2.63 t/ha for triazine tolerant (TT) and glyphosate tolerant (GT) varieties respectively (Tables 5–6). A total of 1.65 million hectares in WA was dedicated to 

canola production, a reduction of 220,000 hectares from 2023 (GIWA, 2025). Despite this, production was 280,000 tonnes higher in 2024, with estimates of statewide canola production totalling 2.8 million tonnes. The increase in production is likely due to more favourable climate conditions in the 2024 season. Despite the late break to the season, there were fewer reports of water logging and frost in high rainfall regions (GIWA, 2025). Canola prices at the start of 2024 were lower than at the beginning of 2023. However, the prices steadily rose over the 2024 season and were higher than 2023 at the conclusion of the season in December (ABARES, 2025). 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
Canola<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 129** 

Canola 

## **Canola types** 

The most widely grown herbicide tolerance groups in canola are glyphosate tolerant (GT), triazine tolerant (TT) and imidazolinone tolerant (IMI). There are many varieties with single herbicide tolerance available to WA growers. However, there are also many varieties offering dual or ‘stacked’ tolerance with combinations 

of TT + IMI or GT + IMI. Another option is LibertyLink[®] (LL), which is tolerant of glufosinateammonium and is usually offered in dual tolerance varieties in combinations such as GT + LL and TT + LL. The herbicide tolerance of current varieties can be found in Table 1. 

The GT group includes both Roundup Ready[®] and TruFlex[®] types, which share the same single gene genetic modification licensed from Bayer. TruFlex[®] canola types allow for higher glyphosate rates and a wider application window than Roundup Ready[®] canola. Pioneer also offers Optimum GLY[®] varieties. These varieties have a similar glyphosate application pattern to TruFlex[®] canola but are based on a different gene modification. For more detailed information on herbicide tolerance and weed control in canola see Dhammu et al. (2020). 

IMI varieties are usually developed through traditional breeding, using the Clearfield[®] (CL) production system. However, there are also some IMI varieties available that do not use CL branding. 

Canola is available as open pollinated (OP) or hybrid breeding types. OP seed is created through self-pollination and harvested OP seed is often retained on-farm for use in subsequent seasons. Only TT and conventional canola are currently available for purchase as OP varieties. Due to a lack of chemical options for weed control, very little conventional canola is grown in WA. Consequently, conventional varieties are no longer tested in any NVT trials and are therefore not included in this guide. 

Hybrid seed is produced from managed crosses between elite canola lines and harvested seed should not be retained for subsequent seasons. Hybrid TT varieties usually higher yielding than OP TT varieties and may be a good choice where there is higher yield potential. Despite possible reductions in yield, OP varieties may be an alternative option in high-risk scenarios due to lower seed costs, as harvested seed can be retained. 

Some longer maturity ‘winter types’ with CL herbicide tolerance are available for dual purpose use (Graze and Grain). These varieties need a long period of cold before entering the reproductive stage and are suitable only for cooler southern areas. Dual purpose types provide an opportunity to graze the vegetative crop before flowering begins. 

Graze and Grain varieties are not included in NVT trials and are not reported in yield tables in this guide. Information on the management and yields of winter canola types are available from the seed company websites listed at the end of this chapter. 

## **Choosing a canola variety** 

Considerations when choosing a canola variety include: 

- **Herbicide tolerance:** Herbicide tolerance is dependent on weed species present in the paddock and chemical residue from previous rotations. Herbicide spray window is another consideration, with TruFlex[®] and Optimum GLY[®] varieties able to be sprayed with glyphosate until first flower. It should be noted that continued use of a single herbicide group in subsequent years can result in development of herbicide resistance in weeds. To avoid this, it is recommended to rotate the use of herbicide groups. 

- **Hybrid vs OP:** Most hybrids have a higher yield potential but are associated with higher seed costs, as new seed must be bought every year. Despite the possibility of reduced yields, OP varieties may be more economically competitive in some scenarios due to the lower seeds costs as harvested seed can be retained for subsequent seasons. 

- **Yield:** Choose varieties with proven high yields and reliability across seasons and yield ranges. Tables 2–7, show yield averages of varieties from the NVT. Use Figure 1 to identify which rainfall zone your region falls into to ensure use of the appropriate yield table. 

- **Variety maturity:** Maturity of the variety should match the intended time of sowing. Late maturity varieties will generally perform best when sown early and earlier maturity varieties will perform best when sown later. Early-mid and mid-maturing varieties can be adaptable across both early and late sowing times. 

**130 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Canola 

- **Blackleg resistance:** Select a variety with the highest possible blackleg resistance rating and follow the 8 management steps to control blackleg, as listed in the GRDC _Blackleg management guide_ ( grdc.com.au/GRDC-FSBlacklegManagementGuide ). DPIRD has also developed a tool called BlacklegCM to assist growers with decisions relating to the control - 

- of blackleg ( https://www.dpird.wa.gov.au/online tools/blacklegcm/ ). 

- **Risk:** Yield potential of the season and variety need to be balanced with the cost of seed and other inputs such as herbicide, fungicide and fertiliser. 

- **Oil content:** Oil content should be considered as a part of the overall assessment of variety performance and is likely to be greatest with early sowing. 

- **Pod shattering:** Genetics can be manipulated to make varieties less prone to shattering using genes such as PodGuard[®] . Variety selection should consider varieties with lower shattering tendencies, especially in regions with climates that pose an increased shattering risk. Contact the seed distributor for information on the shattering tolerance of varieties. However, it should be noted that there is not a standardised method for testing shattering tolerance. Caution should be used when comparing varieties from different companies. 

Herbicide tolerance, blackleg resistance, maturity and oil content of current canola varieties can be found in Table 1. Limited pod shattering information is also presented. 

## **Establishment of canola** 

Larger seeding programs are causing the timing of sowing of canola crops to shift, trending towards dry seeding at earlier dates. While the yield potential of canola is higher with earlier establishment, uncertainty in climatic conditions, such as rainfall and temperature, pose a threat to successful crop establishment. Canola crops face greater challenges than cereals, as establishment is limited by small seed size and epigeal germination. It is not uncommon to need to re-seed canola due to poor establishment and small changes to seeding factors can affect crop establishment. 

Seeding considerations for improved crop establishment: 

- **Sowing rate:** Plant density is managed through seeding rates and is fundamental to achieving yield potential. Recommended target plant densities (plants/m[2] ) will differ depending on the rainfall zone and whether the variety used is a hybrid or OP. Higher plant density are often preferred for OP canola crops to account for the possibility of reduced vigour. Additionally, it is generally more financially viable to increase the sowing rate to mitigate possible establishment issues with OP canola crops as seed costs tend to be lower. Seeding rates can be calculated using the target plant density, expected field establishment, seed size and germination percentage. DPIRD provides an online Canola Seeding Rate Calculator to aid in determining seeding rate ( https://www.dpird.wa.gov.au/ online-tools/canola-seeding-rate-calculator/ ). 

- **Fertiliser placement:** Canola seedlings are vulnerable to fertiliser toxicity when it is in close proximity to the seed. Care should be taken during sowing to keep the fertiliser separated from the seed, and excessive fertiliser rates should be avoided. 

- **Seeding depth:** In other commodities, deeper seeding depths are often used as a tactic to access greater soil moisture, especially when dry seeding. However, canola seedlings have difficulty emerging from greater depths due to the small seed size. It is generally recommended to seed canola at a depth between 1.2 and 2.5 cm (GRDC, 2018) but other factors such as soil type and soil moisture can alter ideal depth. 

- **Seed size:** Larger seed size has been associated with better establishment due to increased seedling vigour.Some varieties have a larger seed size, but caution must be used when comparing varieties, as seed size can very a lot between individual seed lots within a variety. If retaining OP seed, consider seed sizing and using larger seed for subsequent year’s crop. 

- **Seed type:** Hybrid varieties tend to have greater seedling vigour than OP varieties. Seedling vigour helps with establishment, particularly in poor conditions, with hybrids commonly achieving 10–15% higher establishment. However, the gap in establishment with OP varieties can be alleviated through elevating the sowing rate. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 131** 

Canola 

- **Press wheels:** There is a range of press wheel types available on the market. The type of press wheel and the pressure it applies will alter the degree of soil compaction the canola seedling must break through to reach the surface. The type of press wheel chosen should be suitable for shallow seeding and the soil type of the paddock. 

- **Insect damage:** Canola in the seedling stage is extremely vulnerable to insect damage, which can be prevented through seed dressings and chemical sprays. Non-chemical factors, such as crop rotations, stubble management and site selection can also influence insect damage. 

## **Current research in this area** 

WA farmers have reported field establishment rates of 60–80% (Fletcher et al., 2024). However, field establishment rates as low as 40% are not uncommon. Additionally, two-thirds of farmers have reported establishment poor enough that re-seeding has occurred at least once in the last 5 years. As a result, a national research investment from GRDC and led by CSIRO, began in 2023 to investigate risk factors surrounding dry sown canola. As part of this project, DPIRD has conducted field trials in 2023, 2024 and 2025 seasons aiming to identify critical limits of soil temperature and moisture to better understand the effect these factors have on canola establishment. Additionally, other agronomic factors are being investigated to determine the effect of seeding depth and press wheel pressure on establishment. This research will be used to develop a decisionmaking tool for farmers manage the risks associated with early seeding of canola. 

Trials across 2023, 2024 and 2025 have shown that canola seed is able to sit in dry soil for a long period (up to 8 weeks) and still provide high plant numbers at establishment. However, very little moisture is needed to start degrading the seed underground and cause poor establishment. The critical threshold of soil moisture needed to degrade the seed underground will depend on environmental factors such as soil type and climate. It is important to note that in many situations the timing of canola establishment is more important than the number of plants established. Fewer plants established early can yield more than lots of plants established later in the season. 



Results from trials in 2023 and 2024 suggest that seeding deep (>3 cm) is not recommended with current varieties due to reductions in establishment (Fletcher et al., 2024). However, growers planning to increase seeding depth to chase soil moisture should consider removing press wheels or reducing press wheel pressure. The effect of press wheel pressure on establishment was investigated further in the 2025 season. These results indicated that the effect of press wheel pressure is heavily dependent on soil moisture at seeding and soil texture, with establishment decreasing under heavy press wheel pressure in a sandy soil with adequate moisture and the reverse effect in a heavier soil with marginal moisture. The difference in press wheel response between sites is likely due to the soil-to-seed contact and soil texture but additional research is needed to validate this. For more detailed information on results from the Canola Establishment project see Fletcher et al. (2024), ( https://www. agronomyaustraliaproceedings.org/images/ sampledata/2024/2024ASAfetcher_andrew-161892-Fletcher-Andrew.pdf ). 

CSIRO is also leading a GRDC supported project exploring the role of hypocotyl length in canola establishment. Longer hypocotyls in canola are associated with improved emergence from deeper sowing (Nelson et al., 2024) but current Australian varieties tend to have short hypocotyls and emerge poorly from deep sowing compared with international varieties. There is potential to reduce the risks with early sown canola through the development of canola varieties with longer hypocotyls that can be sown deeper to access moisture deeper in the soil. This project is developing Australian adapted germplasm with longer hypocotyls and better selection tools so that canola breeders can develop long hypocotyl varieties that are better able to emerge from deeper sowing. 

**132 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Canola 

## **New varieties for 2026** 

## **TT varieties:** 

- **AGT Insurgent TT** is an open-pollinated variety with early-mid maturity. This variety had a high yield in the 2024 NVT for an open pollinated TT variety and yielded higher than some hybrids. Additionally, the variety has a high oil content. 

- **Pioneer PY432T** is an early-mid maturity hybrid and had a high yield across both zones in the NVT in 2024. 

## **TT + LL varieties:** 

- **InVigor LT 4030P** is an early-mid maturity variety with PodGuard[®] to protect against shattering. The variety had high yields across both NVT zones in 2024. 

## **Withdrawn varieties** 

## **TT Varieties:** 

- ATR Bonito*, ATR Stingray, InVigor T 4510, InVigor T 6010 

## **GT Varieties:** 

- DG Bindo TF, DG Drummond TF, Pioneer 44Y30RR 

## **GT + IMI Varieties:** 

- Hyola Garrison XC 

## **IMI Varieties:** 

- Hyola Continuum CL, Pioneer 45Y93CL 

_*ATR Bonito still included in Guide as harvested OP seed can be retained for subsequent years and this variety was still in use in WA in 2025._ 

## **GT varieties:** 

- **DG Buller G** is an early-mid maturity variety that utilises Optimum GLY[®] for glyphosate tolerance. This variety had moderate yields in the 2024 NVT but has a high oil content. 

## **GT + IMI varieties:** 

- **Chronos TFI** is an early-mid maturity variety that uses TruFlex[®] technology for glyphosate tolerance. NVT data for this variety is not yet available. However, based on Nufarm internal data, this variety has a recommended target yield similar to Hunter TF. 

## **IMI varieties:** 

- **Vesta IMI** is an early-mid maturity variety with a high oil content and is resistant to blackleg. This variety was not present in 2024 NVT in the low-medium rainfall zone but had moderate yields in the medium-high rainfall zone.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 135** 

Canola 

## **NVT results** 

## **Data analysis** 

NVT results are available as single-site reports or as multi-environment trial long-term summaries (MET data). MET data establishes relationships between varieties, comparing results from similar environments and considers issues that affect a single site, such as poor establishment or variable soil types. Because of this, MET data is preferred over single-site reports. Additionally, MET data is used to generate predicted yields, even when a variety is not present in a trial. This is particularly useful for canola, as new varieties are often released with limited NVT testing. 

In this guide, data from the NVT Long-Term MET Yield Reporter (data accuracy ≥80% and Variant Allele Frequency ≥25%) is presented for each NVT series for the years 2020–24. During this period, there were 215 canola NVT in WA. The locations of the 2024 trials can be seen in Figure 1. 

The data is presented as a table of performance values for each variety compared to the mean of all NVT sites within that rainfall series (either low-medium or medium-high rainfall, see Figure 1). Shaded cells in Tables 2–7 represent predicted yield values from the MET analysis even though the variety was not present in the trials that year. Yield values for varieties that have been included in trials over multiple seasons should be considered more robust than yield values for varieties that have been included in a limited number of seasons. 

MET data from 2020–24 was used to calculate a weighted average for each herbicide group within the 2 rainfall zones. Weighted averages are not presented in cases where a variety has only been present in a single year of NVT testing. The weighted averages are based on a variety’s average performance over several years and across an entire rainfall zone. Caution should be used when interpreting the weighted averages as these factors can mask interactions between the variety and the environment. Growers should consider the relative performance of varieties within an individual season, and whether their location fits into the low-medium or medium-high rainfall zone, to help explain how a variety might perform on their farm. 

Results of individual NVT trials and MET analyses are available online at nvt.grdc.com.au . 

## **Medium-high rainfall NVT** 

The 2024 NVT in the medium-high rainfall zone were sown between 24 April and 11 May. Yields in 2024 were greater across all trial series than in 2023. In total, there were 21 trials across the 3 herbicide tolerance trial series. 

## **TT varieties** 

The hybrid TT varieties outperformed all TT OP varieties, with the exception of AGT Insurgent TT (Table 2). This new variety yielded similarly to moderately yielding hybrids such as RGT Capacity TT and InVigor T 4511 in 2024. AGT Insurgent TT also had a high oil content but is not well suited if there is a high risk of blackleg yield reductions (Table 1). DG Bidgee TT and Renegade TT had reduced yield and a lower oil content (Table 1) in 2024 NVT compared to AGT Insurgent TT but have remained reliable high yielders for OP varieties over several seasons (Table 2). Additionally, DG Bidgee TT and Renegade TT have stronger blackleg resistance compared to AGT Insurgent TT (Table 1). All 3 of these varieties have similar mid-early maturity, making the varieties adaptable to varied sowing dates. DG Avon TT has early maturity, making this variety suitable for later sowing dates. Alternatively, the mid-late maturity 

DG Murray TT is more suitable for earlier sowing dates. Both these varieties have lower yields than top performing OPs (Table 2), but both have moderate oil contents (Table 1) and should be considered when intended sowing dates are outside the range of early-mid or mid maturity varieties. 

Pioneer PY429T, Hyola Blazer TT and HyTTec Trophy are all high yielding (Table 2) options for an early-mid maturity variety (Table 1). Hyola Blazer TT has a higher oil content compared to the other 2 varieties, while 

HyTTec Trophy has a higher blackleg resistance rating (Table 1). The 5 year average yield for Pioneer PY429T was greater than the other varieties but it has only been in the last 2 years of NVT and therefore the reliability of the yield of this variety is not as robust compared to Hyola Blazer TT and HyTTec Trophy (Table 2). 

**136 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Canola 

Other varieties that have been reliably high yielders over multiple seasons include HyTTec Trifecta and HyTTec Trident. HyTTec Trifecta is an option for a mid-maturity variety with a good blackleg resistance rating (Table 1). HyTTec Trident has a similar yield reliability and blackleg resistance but also provides an option for an early maturity variety with improved oil content. 

Although slightly lower in yield compared to HyTTec Trifecta (Table 2), Pioneer PY520TC is a similar maturity type but has additional benefits with dual tolerance to TT and IMI, which widens the options for chemical weed control and protects against chemical residues in soils. Hyola Defender CT did not yield as high as Pioneer PY520TC but has the same dual tolerance benefits with a higher oil content and blackleg rating (Table 1). InVigor LT 4030P is a new variety with dual tolerance to TT and LL and has had a high yield in the last 2 years of NVT, similar to HyTTec Trophy (Table 2). Additionally, InVigor LT 4030P utilizes PodGuard[®] technology, making this variety suitable for areas more prone to shattering (Table 1). 

## **GT varieties** 

Hunter TF has consistently achieved high yields over multiple NVT seasons (Table 3). Pioneer PY428R has a similar 5-year average to Hunter TF, but has not got the same reliability over multiple seasons due to limited testing in the NVT.  Both are high yielding options for early mid maturity varieties, which provides a degree of flexibility in sowing dates (Table 1). However, Hunter TF has slightly improved oil content and blackleg resistance. Pioneer PY323G has had slightly reduced yields compared to Hunter TF (Table 3), but has a high oil content and is an early maturity variety, providing an option for later sowing dates (Table 1). Pioneer 45Y28RR is an option when targeting earlier sowing dates, as this variety is a mid-maturity variety. Although the yield of this variety is slightly lower compared to Hunter TF (Table 3), Pioneer 45Y28RR offers similar resistance to blackleg but has a vastly improved oil content (Table 1). Pioneer 44Y27RR is similar in yield to Pioneer 45Y28RR and has slightly earlier maturity. It is important to note that seed for Pioneer 44Y27RR will only be available for the 2026 season before being withdrawn, with Pioneer intending to replace this variety with Pioneer PY428R. The new variety, DG Buller G, 

has a high oil content and good blackleg resistance, but only moderate yields in the 2024 NVT (Table 3). 

Hyola Regiment XC is another variety that has had stable yields across multiple seasons in the NVT (Table 3). This variety has a reduced average yield compared to Hunter TF and Pioneer PY428R but has additional tolerance to IMI, which can be useful in alleviating concerns with soil chemical residues and allows a broader range of chemical control options for weeds. Hyola Regiment XC is similar to Hunter TF in oil content and maturity allowing for flexibility in sowing dates (Table 1). However, Hyola Regiment XC has an excellent blackleg package with a high resistance rating and utilises multiple gene groups. InVigor LR 4540P was on par with top yielding varieties, like Hunter TF and Pioneer PY428R (Table 3). The oil content and maturity of InVigor LR 4540P is also similar to Hunter TF but this variety has the extra advantage of dual tolerance for GT and LL (Table 1). 

## **IMI varieties** 

Pioneer PY421C, Pioneer 45Y95CL and Pioneer 44Y94CL all have high yield averages (Table 4). However, there is a greater variation in yield results across seasons for Pioneer 45Y95CL, indicating this variety is less stable under different seasonal conditions. Despite this, Pioneer 45Y95CL is still an option for a mid-maturity variety which provides opportunity for earlier sowing dates (Table 1). Pioneer PY421C and Pioneer 44Y94CL have improved oil content compared to Pioneer 45Y95CL and both are early-mid maturity types making them more suitable for slightly later seeding dates. Despite slightly reduced yields (Table 4), compared to other IMI varieties in this series, the new variety, Vesta IMI, has a high oil content and improved disease resistance (Table 1).

## **Low-medium rainfall NVT** 

Sowing dates for 2024 NVT in the low-medium rainfall zone fell between 25 April and 1 June. As in the medium-high rainfall zone, 2024 NVT yields were higher than the previous year in the TT series and the GT series. In total, there were 18 trials in these 2 herbicide trial series but there were no IMI trials in the low-medium rainfall zone in 2024. 

## **TT varieties** 

AGT Insurgent TT was higher yielding than other OP varieties in the 2024 NVT but was still only comparable to moderately yielding hybrids (Table 5). However, AGT Insurgent TT offers flexibility in sowing dates through early-mid maturity and has a high oil content (Table 1). The yield of the next best OP varieties, DG Avon TT and Renegade TT, were a long way behind AGT Insurgent TT in the 2024 NVT but have remained stable as high yielding varieties across several seasons (Table 5). The oil contents of DG Avon TT and Renegade TT were lower than AGT Insurgent TT, but the oil content of DG Avon TT was still above the herbicide group average (Table 1). DG Avon TT and Renegade TT had the additional benefit of stronger blackleg resistance, compared to AGT Insurgent TT. Renegade TT is an early-mid variety, like 

AGT Insurgent TT and therefore offers similar flexibility in sowing dates. However, DG Avon TT is more suitable for later sowing dates as this variety is an early maturity type. 

HyTTec Velocity and HyTTec Trident have had reliable high yields across the 5-year period (Table 5), but HyTTec Trident has a higher oil content and blackleg resistance rating (Table 1). Both varieties are early maturity types, making them suitable for later sowing dates. High yielding options for varieties suited to earlier sowing dates include Pioneer PY432T, Pioneer PY429T and HyTTec Trophy, all of which have early-mid maturity. Pioneer PY432T is a new variety that showed promise with a high yield in the 2024 NVT, but limited testing in the NVT across multiple seasons means the yield stability of this variety is uncertain (Table 5). Likewise, the yield stability across seasons is not clear for Pioneer PY429T, despite the high yield of this variety in the 2023 NVT. Pioneer PY429T has good resistance to blackleg but the oil content of Pioneer PY432T was higher (Table 1). Although slightly lower in yield, HyTTec Trophy has remained reliable across multiple seasons (Table 5). Additionally, HyTTec Trophy had similar blackleg resistance to Pioneer PY429T and also a higher oil content (Table 1). Dynatron TT was the highest yielding mid-maturity type, with yields slightly lower than 

**140 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Canola 

HyTTec Trophy (Table 5). However, the mid-maturity of this variety makes Dynatron TT well suited for earlier sowing dates (Table 1). Dynatron TT and Spark TT have the highest oil content of all TT hybrids. Be aware that Dynatron TT is not recommended if there is a high risk of blackleg reducing yield. 

Griffon TTI was the highest yielding TT + IMI dual tolerance variety, with yields equivalent to Dynatron TT (Table 5). Hyola Enforcer CT and Hyola Defender CT had lower yields, but both provide better protection against blackleg (Table 1). Despite a slightly reduced yield compared to Hyola Defender CT (Table 5), Hyola Enforcer CT had a higher oil content (Table 1). All 3 of these TT + IMI varieties are early-mid maturity types offering flexibility in sowing dates and provide a broader range of chemical weed control options and alleviate concerns of chemical residues in soils. InVigor LT 4030P is a new variety offering dual tolerance to TT and LL. The yield stability of this variety is uncertain due to limited NVT testing. However, this variety had promising results in the 2024 NVT, with a high yield equivalent to HyTTec Trophy (Table 5). InVigor LT 4530P is the other option for TT + LL dual tolerance and despite a reduction in yield, InVigor LT 4530P could still be advantageous in regions prone to blackleg (Table 1). 

and Pioneer PY323G had moderate resistance to blackleg, but Pioneer PY323G was superior in oil content compared to all GT varieties previously mentioned (Table 1). The only new variety entered in this trial series was DG Buller G. Despite moderate yields (Table 6), this mid maturity variety may be suitable if a higher oil content or earlier sowing date is required (Table 1). 

Pioneer PY424GC is similar in yield to Pioneer 44Y27RR but had the additional benefit of dual tolerance for GT and IMI, which allows for a broader spectrum of chemical weed control and protects against chemical residues in soils (Table 6). Hyola Regiment XC has slightly lower yields compared to Pioneer PY424GC but provides the same dual tolerance benefits with a much stronger blackleg resistance package and improved oil content (Table 1). InVigor LR 4540P has had high yields in this NVT series with only slightly reduced yield compared to Hunter TF (Table 6). The variety offers similar flexibility in sowing dates to Hunter TF, as they are both early-mid maturity types, but InVigor LR 4540P also widens options for chemical weed control through dual tolerance to GT + LL (Table 1). InVigor LR 4540P also utilises the PodGuard[®] gene making this variety ideal for regions prone to shattering. 

## **IMI varieties** 

## **GT varieties** 

Hunter TF and Pioneer 44Y27RR have both had reliably high yields over multiple NVT seasons (Table 6). These varieties have good blackleg resistance, but Hunter TF has a higher oil content (Table 1). It is important to note that seed for Pioneer 44Y27RR will only be available for the 2026 season before being withdrawn, with Pioneer intending to replace this variety with Pioneer PY428R. There is limited NVT data available for Pioneer PY428R (Table 6). However, in the 2024 NVT trial, Pioneer PY428R achieved a higher yield than the variety it will replace (Pioneer 44Y27RR) with the yield only slightly lower compared to Hunter TF. All these varieties provide flexibility in sowing dates due to the early-mid maturity (Table 1). Varieties better suited to later seeding dates include early maturity types such as Emu TF and Pioneer PY323G. Emu TF had a higher 5-year average yield compared to Pioneer PY323G (Table 6). However, the yield of Emu TF in 2020 was abnormally high. In the 2 years where both varieties were present in the NVT, Emu TF and Pioneer PY323G achieved similar yields, equivalent to Pioneer 44Y27RR. Both Emu TF 

Extreme caution should be used when comparing NVT yield results from IMI varieties in the low medium rainfall zone due to the limited number of years of NVT data available across all varieties (Table 7). Hyola Solstice CL had the highest 4-year average yield. However, there was a large discrepancy in the yield between the 2 years Hyola Solstice CL was present in the trials, suggesting a lack of yield stability across seasons. Hyola Solstice CL does have a strong blackleg resistance package and a high oil content (Table 1). The 4-year average yields for Pioneer 44Y94CL and Pioneer 43Y92CL were lower than Hyola Solstice CL but were more consistent across season variations (Table 7). Both varieties have good disease packages but were weaker in blackleg resistance compared to Hyola Solstice CL and also had reduced oil contents (Table 1). Considerations should be made for the varied maturity type of the 3 above varieties. Ceres IMI and Pioneer PY421C had high yields in 2023 season, equivalent to Hyola Solstice CL, but more NVT testing in this zone is required to make more robust comparisons (Table 7).

Canola 

## **Canola varieties grown in WA, 2020–25** 

The usage of TT varieties continued to decline in 2025, with 5% less land in 2025 dedicated to TT canola than in 2024 (Table 8). GT varieties still dominate in usage and account for half of the area sown to canola in 2025. However, the use of dual tolerance varieties has doubled since 2024 with area sown to dual tolerance varieties now 

overtaking TT varieties. GT + IMI and GT + LL varieties were the most common dual tolerance types, accounting for 50% and 40% of area sown to dual tolerance varieties respectively (Figure 2). TT + IMI was less common covering only 7% of dual tolerance area.

Canola<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 145** 

Canola 



Figure 3 shows the proportion of canola area sown to each herbicide tolerance type for each port zone. In Geraldton, use of GT varieties was highly dominant, accounting for 79% of canola land use in this region. TT and IMI varieties were uncommon in Geraldton, with only 6% of the area using these herbicide systems. Dual tolerance varieties utilised 15% of the canola area, with almost all combinations comprising of a GT component and only 1% using TT combinations (Figure 4). 

Usage of herbicide systems in Kwinana followed a similar pattern. However, GT systems were not quite as dominant, as more TT varieties were used (Figure 3). Despite greater use of TT systems, dual tolerance systems were still more prevalent and combinations using GT tolerance were preferred (Figure 4). However, compared to Geraldton, a greater proportion of the canola area utilised TT dual tolerance varieties in Kwinana, totalling 9%. 

While the split of herbicide systems within Albany was more even compared to Geraldton and Kwinana, the usage of these systems followed the same trend (Figure 3). GT systems were utilised over the greatest proportion of land, followed by dual tolerance, TT and then IMI. Albany had the highest proportion of IMI usage at 16%, when no other region dedicated more than 5% of the canola area to this system.  Combinations using GT were still the most prevalent type of dual tolerance system, with TT combinations accounting for 7% of dual tolerance area (Figure 4). 

Esperance was the only region where GT systems were not dominant (Figure 3). Despite TT systems utilising the largest proportion of canola area, there was less than 10% difference between area of GT, dual tolerance and TT systems. Dual tolerance combination including TT were the highest in this region, accounting for 13% of dual tolerance varieties used (Figure 4). However, GT combinations were still preferred and accounted for 87% of dual tolerance use in Esperance. 

The top 5 varieties used in 2025 were Hunter TF, Emu TF, Hyola Regiment XC, InVigor LR 4540P and InVigor R 4520P. These varieties combined accounted for just over half of the total area of canola in WA (Table 9). All these varieties utilise the GT (TF) system, which offers a broad-spectrum chemical control option for weeds and flexibility in the timing of applications. 

The most prevalent TT varieties, HyTTec Trident, HyTTec Trophy and Hyola Blazer TT, have all decreased in usage since 2023, but were still in the top 10 varieties used. The use of TT (OP) varieties has declined since 2020, likely due to the potential yield and vigour penalties of OP varieties compared to hybrid TT varieties. Only 3 OP varieties had more than 0.5% of area, ATR Bonito, Renegade TT and DG Avon TT. Renegade TT and DG Avon TT are relatively new varieties, but ATR Bonito has declined in area usage by 31.9% since 2020. 

IMI varieties were also uncommon, with Pioneer 44Y94CL, Pioneer 45Y95CL and Pioneer PY421C being the only varieties with areas greater than 0.5%. Hyola Regiment XC was the dual tolerance variety that covered the largest proportion of area sown to canola, with use of this variety increasing 4.2% since 2023. This variety is combines GT + IMI, which was the most used dual tolerance combination. Only a single variety with TT + IMI had an area greater than 0.5%, which was Pioneer PY520TC and used 0.7% of area in 2025. Likewise, only a single GT + LL variety, InVigor LR 4540P, had an area greater than 0.5% but this variety covered a much larger proportion of area sown to canola at 8.9%. 

Across all varieties used in WA, early-mid maturity was the most common type. Further, all new varieties for 2026 are also early-mid maturity (Table 1). The popularity of this maturity type is likely due to the flexibility of sowing dates that this maturity type provides.

**150 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

## **Oats** 

## **Introduction** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

This oat guide is designed to help growers determine which milling oat or export hay variety to grow. The guide provides characteristics, disease ratings and agronomic information for oat varieties that offer the best opportunity to meet market requirements (Tables 1–14 and Figures 1–11). 

Many oat grain varieties are available for delivery into the CBH Group system. Grain Industry of Western Australia (GIWA) oat delivery grades available at selected CBH receival bins are OAT1 and OAT2, while OWAN is an exclusive segregation for Wandering oats. All milling oat varieties, except Wandering, can be delivered as OAT1, provided they meet the required quality specifications. Each variety has its strengths and weaknesses across different growing regions. Most successful oat growers choose to grow more than one variety because no single oat variety is likely to provide optimum agronomic traits, disease resistance, yield and quality in any one year. Some milling oat varieties are suitable for baling as export hay, but hay-only varieties may provide a better option for dedicated export hay growers. This guide summarises the suitability of oat varieties for grain and hay (Table 1). It also outlines the characteristics of 3 new oat varieties relative to 4 established oat varieties (Table 2). The variety ‘snapshots’ at the end of this section summarise the strengths and weaknesses of all grain and hay varieties documented in this bulletin. 



The decision on whether to grow an OAT1, OAT2 or OWAN grain oat depends on 5 main factors: 

1. The premium paid for different varieties based on their receival grade. 

2. Relative grain yield of oat varieties. 

3. Differences in input costs due to their agronomic and disease characteristics. 

4. Likelihood of meeting oat receival 

   - specifications. 

5. Location of receival segregations for OAT1 and OAT2 varieties. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 151** 

Oats 

## **Grain oat variety choices in 2026 – what should I grow?** 

Responsibility for oat classification in WA has transitioned from GIWA (a regional classification) to Grains Australia (a national classification). Following consultation with Grains Australia, CBH, and domestic oat processors, it has been confirmed that the existing segregation terminology—OAT1, OAT2, and OWAN—will remain in place. From the 2024–25 harvest, all varieties classified by Grains Australia as milling oats are eligible for delivery into the highest GIWA segregation, OAT1, provided they meet the required quality specifications. The newly accredited milling varieties Goldie (tested as 13008-18), Koala (09143-35), and Minnie (15010-09) are eligible for delivery as OAT1. 

A further review of the WA classification systems has led to the acceptance of Durack into OAT1 stacks starting from the 2025–26 harvest. However, no changes were made for Wandering, which remains eligible only for OAT2 and OWAN delivery. 

Bannister was the dominant grain oat variety grown in WA, accounting for just under 60% of the area sown to oats in 2025 (Figure 1). Other widely planted grain oat varieties include, in decreasing popularity, Carrolup, Goldie, Yallara, Williams, and Wandering. The area sown to Goldie increased significantly from 2024 to 2025, with a slight increase for Koala and Kowari. The area sown to Bannister, Pallinup, Wandering, Williams, and Yallara decreased. Goldie, Koala, and Minnie are expected to be more widely planted in 2026 as seed availability increases. 

Goldie is emerging as a strong replacement for Bannister across most production areas in WA. It offers improved grain yield, particularly in environments that consistently yield below 5 tonnes per hectare and has a higher likelihood of meeting OAT1 receival standards due to its plumper grain shape. Koala is well-suited for very early sowing and performs best in high-yielding environments where yields consistently exceed 5 tonnes per hectare. Minnie, on the other hand, is ideal for medium to high rainfall zones, especially where lodging is a concern, most notably with April sowing.

**152 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 153** 

Oats 

## **Hay oat variety choices in 2026 – what should I grow?** 

The Australian Exporters Company (AEXCO) collects tonnage data for oat varieties with a hay End Point Royalty (EPR), excluding varieties whose EPR has expired (e.g. Brusher, Wintaroo) or non-EPR varieties (e.g. Carrolup). This makes it difficult to accurately determine what is being planted and cut for export hay. 

In 2024–25, Bannister was the dominant export hay variety in WA, accounting for nearly half of all deliveries with an EPR. Other key varieties (for which data is collected), in order of delivery volume, included Yallara, Kingbale, and Archer, with smaller contributions from Williams, Koorabup, Mulgara, and Wallaby. Although Brusher and Carrolup remain important, their impact is not reflected in AEXCO data. As a proportion of the total hay crop, the percentage delivered as Archer and Bannister increased from the 2023–24 to the 2024–25 financial year, while Koorabup and Mulgara decreased. 



Dual-purpose varieties, those suitable for both milling and export hay, made up the bulk of deliveries. New milling varieties Goldie and Koala are suitable for dual-purpose use. In contrast, Minnie is unlikely to be suitable for export hay due to its short stature. 

Of the hay-only varieties, the single-gene imidazolinone (IMI) tolerant varieties, Archer and Kingbale, together accounted for nearly one quarter of export hay deliveries among varieties with an EPR. Archer and Kingbale are fit for rotations where IMI residues exist or where incorporated before sowing (IBS) use of Sentry[®] (imazapic + imazapyr) is being considered to manage barley grass, brome grass, wild oats and other weeds on the label. Sentry[®] herbicide is approved for IBS application in Archer and Kingbale hay, forage, seed and grain crops (domestic feed market only). For more information, see nufarm.com/au/product/sentry/ . Excess grain, seed and screenings produced from the single-gene IMI-tolerant oaten hay varieties Archer and Kingbale can be used for domestic livestock feed. However, the grain of these two varieties cannot be delivered into the Bunge and CBH bulk handling systems. 

New hay-only varieties Kultarr and Wallaby show promise but have yet to be widely adopted. Both offer hay yield and quality comparable to Brusher, with reduced lodging risk and good leaf greenness, particularly in Wallaby. Wallaby is shorter and matures later than Brusher, offering flexibility in cutting schedules. Kultarr is slightly earlier in phenology, with similar hay length to Brusher. 

Traditional hay-only varieties, Koorabup, Mulgara, and Wintaroo, represented a small share of deliveries in 2025–26, indicating a shift in grower preferences. In 2024–25, Brusher was nearly a fifth of plantings, so it would be fair to assume that Brusher is still popular for export hay. 

**154 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

## **New for planting in 2026** 

No new milling or hay oat varieties have been released in 2025. However, growers now have access to three newly accredited milling oat varieties, Goldie, Koala, and Minnie, which were released in 2024. These varieties offer improved agronomic performance and grain quality attributes compared to existing varieties. 

## **Goldie** 

## **Key points:** 

- Accredited as a milling oat variety by Grains Australia in February 2024. 

- Targeted as a replacement for Bannister in all growing regions. 

- Dual-purpose variety. 

- Earlier to panicle emergence than Bannister, around one week earlier, with late April to early May sowing. 

- Taller than Bannister with a lodging risk similar to Williams. 

- Carries a similar disease-resistance package to Bannister. 

- Enhanced grain yields relative to Bannister in less than 5 t/ha environments. 

- Improved receival quality (lower screenings with equivalent or better hectolitre weight). 

- Goldie offers growers greater flexibility in nitrogen (N) fertiliser management compared to Bannister. 

The main risk, however, is a potential for Goldie to lodge under high N rates (Figure 2). 

Goldie has demonstrated suitability for export hay, reaching watery ripe earlier than Brusher and Bannister when sown in early May. It produces shorter hay than Brusher with moderate stem thickness and reduced lodging risk. While panicle emergence may be less consistent than hay-only varieties, Goldie has shown promising results in DPIRD and InterGrain trials (Tables 11–12). If Goldie hay meets export receival standards it will be received by export hay companies. 

Goldie seed will be available for planting in 2026 through resellers, Seedclub Members, and farmerto-farmer trade. 

## **Koala** 

## **Key points:** 

- Accredited as a milling variety by Grains Australia in August 2024. 

- Targeted as an alternative to Bannister in high-yielding environments (>5 t/ha). 

- Dual-purpose variety. 

- Later to panicle emergence than Bannister, with late April to early May planting. 

- Slightly taller than Bannister with a lodging risk similar to Williams. 

- Offers improved resistance to OSR over Bannister. 

- Enhanced grain yields relative to Bannister in greater than 5 t/ha environments. 

Goldie (tested as 13008-18) is a medium-spring, tall-grain oat variety derived from Bannister, with the pedigree 02095-9/Bannister. It exhibits enhanced grain yield (below 5 t/ha) and grain plumpness (Figures 3–11), with an improved likelihood of meeting OAT1 receival standards. 

In an economic analysis of 12 DPIRD N management trials (2022–24), using an OAT1 price of $360/t, urea at $835/t, and fixed costs (seeding, spraying and harvest) of $290/ha, the optimal N rate for Bannister was 45–55 kg/ha. For Goldie, optimal profit occurred at 70–80 kg N/ha, with higher returns than Bannister at 45–55 kg N/ha. Goldie, therefore, offers growers greater flexibility in their fertiliser N management strategies than Bannister. 

- Similar receival quality (equivalent screenings and hectolitre weight) to Bannister. 

- The suggested N strategy for this variety aligns closely with that used for Bannister. 

Koala (tested as 09143-35) is a very late-spring, tall-grain oat variety derived from Bannister, with the pedigree 02088-70/Bannister. Has a different grain yield response to environments than Bannister, with a similar grain quality package (Figures 3–11) and a similar probability of meeting OAT1 receival specifications. DPIRD research suggests that Koala responds differently to 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 155** 

Oats 

fertiliser N than Bannister. High N rates (above 100 kg N/ha) should generally be avoided, as Koala is less responsive to N (Figure 5). 

In an economic analysis of 12 DPIRD N management trials (2022–24), using an OAT1 price of $360/t, urea at $835/t, and fixed costs (seeding, spraying and harvest) of $290/ha, the optimal N rate for Bannister and Koala was 45–55 kg/ha. The suggested N strategy for Koala, therefore, aligns closely with that used for Bannister. Where high N rates are used, there is the potential for Koala to lodge (Figure 2). 

Koala shows potential for export hay, being ready to cut about a week later than Brusher and Mulgara when sown in early May. It produces shorter hay with moderate stem thickness and a reduced risk of lodging compared to Brusher. However, panicle emergence was lower than that of Brusher in the 2023 trials. If Koala hay meets export receival standards it will be received by export hay companies. 

Koala seed will be available for planting in 2026 through Seednet partners and farmer-to-farmer trade. 

## **Minnie** 

## **Key points:** 

- Accredited as a milling variety by Grains Australia in September 2024. 

- Targeted as an alternative to Bannister in medium to high rainfall environments. 

- Potential as a dual-purpose variety is likely limited by its short stature. 

- Earlier to panicle emergence than Bannister, closer to Carrolup when sown in late April to early May. 

- Minnie has a shorter height straw than Bannister, with a lower lodging risk. 

- Carries a similar disease-resistance package to Bannister. 

- Enhanced grain yields relative to Bannister in less than 4 t/ha environments. 

- Improved grain plumpness relative to Bannister, with a hectolitre weight equivalent to Williams. 

- Minnie offers growers greater flexibility in N fertiliser management compared to Bannister. 



Minnie (tested as 15010-09) is a medium spring, short-height grain oat variety derived from Bannister, with the pedigree Bannister/07251-9. It exhibits enhanced grain yield (below 4 t/ha) and grain plumpness (Figures 3–11), although its hectolitre weight may limit delivery as OAT1 in some seasons and environments. Among the three newly accredited milling oat varieties, Minnie has the lowest lodging risk (Figure 2), making it particularly suitable for environments prone to leaning. It also demonstrates high yield potential and a slightly stronger response to applied N than Bannister (Figures 4 and 5). 

In an economic analysis of 8 DPIRD N management trials (2023–24), using an OAT1 price of $360/t, urea at $835/t, and fixed costs (seeding, spraying and harvest) of $290/ha, the optimal N rate for Bannister was 60–70 kg/ha. For Minnie, optimal profit occurred at 80–90 kg N/ha (the same as Goldie), with higher returns than Bannister at 60–70 kg N/ha. Minnie, therefore, offers growers greater flexibility in their fertiliser N management strategies than Bannister, but without the lodging risk associated with growing Goldie (Figure 2). 

Due to its shorter height, Minnie may not be suitable for export hay production. Further feedback from export hay companies and growers is needed to determine its viability at an industrial scale. 

Minnie seed will be available for planting in 2026 through resellers, Seedclub Members, and farmer-to-farmer trade.

## **Grain – yield and quality** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

## **Grain yield** 

The National Variety Trials (NVT), coordinated by the Grains Research and Development Corporation (GRDC), offer a nationally consistent and independent framework for evaluating varietal performance across diverse agroecological zones. These trials help growers and agronomists select oat varieties best suited to their local conditions. 

Results are available as single-site reports, reflecting performance in a specific season and location, or multi-environment trials (METs), which compare varietal performance against the site mean across multiple seasons. Users can access and filter data by state, region, or location through nvt.grdc.com.au, enabling informed and tailored decision-making. 

This sowing guide is based on MET data from 2020–24 (excluding Agzone 1), with an accuracy of ≥0.8 and variance accounted of ≥25%. Performance summaries are presented by Agzone and state wide (Tables 3–8). While port zonebased summaries are now available online, they are not included in this guide. 

When 4 or more observations are available, a 5-year weighted average is calculated. However, growers should interpret these averages cautiously, as they may obscure seasonal variability and variety-by-environment (GxE) interactions. Table 9 provides direct comparisons between varieties using least significant difference (LSD) analysis at a 95% confidence level, based on single-site MET data. These comparisons are valid but should not be relied upon in isolation. 

To better understand varietal performance across yield environments, NVT presents data in halftonne intervals (“yield groups”) via the Long-Term MET Yield Reporter. Figures 3 and 4 use linear regression to show how each variety performs as the site mean yield increases. These graphs assess deviations from the site mean and fit either linear or quadratic trends depending on statistical significance. If no trend is significant, the variety’s performance is considered stable across yield levels. 

Figure 5 presents average grain yield responses to increasing N fertiliser rates across DPIRDfunded trials in the Kwinana port zone (Yerecoin, Wongan Hills, York, and Popanyinning) from 2022–24 and 2023–24. 

Based on NVT and DPIRD data (Tables 3–9; Figures 3–5), the highest-yielding oat varieties in WA include: 

- **Bilby:** Superior to Bannister in <3 t/ha environments since 2021; remains competitive in higher-yielding zones. 

- **Goldie:** Benchmark performer in environments yielding below 4 t/ha; consistently outperforms Bannister in sub-5 t/ha conditions. 

- **Koala:** Best suited to high-yielding environments (above 5 t/ha); shows improved yield over Bannister in these zones. 

- **Minnie:** Performs similarly to Goldie with slightly lower yield potential; consistently exceeds Bannister below 4 t/ha since 2022. 

- **Wandering and Williams:** Competitive performers depending on region and environmental yield potential. 

## In contrast: 

- **Carrolup and Yallara:** Generally, they underperform relative to Goldie and Minnie. 

- **Durack:** Competitive in very low-yielding environments (below 1.5 t/ha), particularly with June sowing.

## **Grain quality** 

Grain quality is a critical determinant of market suitability for milling oat varieties. Key physical traits include hectolitre weight and screenings (measured using a 2.0 mm slotted sieve). Figures 6–7 and 9–10 present these traits relative to the site mean across both established and newly accredited varieties. Where statistically significant, linear or quadratic trends were fitted; otherwise, varietal responses were considered stable across yield levels. Data were sourced from the NVT State wide Yield and Grain Quality tables available at nvt.grdc.com.au . 

Figures 5 and 8 illustrate the average grain quality responses to increasing N fertiliser rates across DPIRD-funded trials in the Kwinana port zone (Yerecoin, Wongan Hills, York, and Popanyinning) for the periods 2022–24 and 2023–24. 

Currently, no milling oat variety combines both high hectolitre weight and low screenings. Goldie is the closest candidate. Yallara previously held this position but has since underperformed in yield, falling below Bannister in 79% of WA NVT trials (Table 9). 

Carrolup remains the benchmark for hectolitre weight, followed by Durack (not shown) and Yallara (Figures 6–8). Bannister and Williams exhibit weaker hectolitre weights, with Bannister slightly outperforming Williams. Bilby and Goldie generally show improved hectolitre weight compared to Bannister, though both are inferior to Carrolup. Goldie’s hectolitre weight is comparable to Yallara’s. Koala performs slightly below Bannister but better than Williams, while Minnie’s hectolitre weight is similar to or better than Williams. 

Goldie and Yallara are the benchmark varieties for grain plumpness (Figures 9–11). Genetic differences in screenings are evident at the OAT1 receival limit (10% through a 2.0 mm slotted sieve), with even more pronounced variation at the OAT2 limit (15%). Williams has the poorest grain plumpness, posing the highest risk of failing milling receival standards. Bannister shows improvement over Williams but remains less plump than Goldie. Koala performs similarly to Bannister and is an improvement over Williams. Bilby offers a slight advantage over Bannister and a more apparent improvement over Williams. Minnie matches Goldie in grain plumpness and exceeds both Bannister and Koala.
