Oats 

## **Management advice for grain oat growers** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

## **Target April sowing when growing milling oats** 

Grain with more than 15% screenings through a 2.0 mm slotted sieve is not accepted into the bulk handling system. Research conducted by DPIRD, in collaboration with GRDC (project DAW1901002RTX), has demonstrated that sowing milling oats in early April significantly reduces the risk of screenings. Across two seasons (2019 and 2020), early planted oats exhibited: 

- Increased hectolitre weight (up to +3 kg/hL), 

- Reduced screenings (–9%), and 

- Higher grain yield (+0.65 t/ha) compared to oats sown after the first week of May. 

Grain staining was below reportable thresholds and did not affect quality. Importantly, early planting combined with appropriate variety selection proved more effective in reducing screenings than lowering fertiliser N rates. These practices are essential for achieving OAT1 quality standards and optimising gross margins. 

Oats are particularly well-suited to April sowing due to their elongated mesocotyl, which, together with the coleoptile, supports enhanced emergence from sowing depths beyond 5 cm, and up to 12 cm depending on soil type. This enables effective establishment in conditions with stored subsoil moisture and limits the effect of pre-emergence herbicides on establishment. Milling oats benefit from April emergence and demonstrate greater tolerance to flowering frost than wheat and barley. Their flexibility in sowing depth enhances productivity when sown in April, and being able to be sown early reduces their risk of failing to meet OAT1 grain receival standards. 

## **Tips for nitrogen fertiliser on milling oats** 

N strategies differ between grain and hay oats. For milling oats, the rate of N application has a greater influence on yield than the timing. The recommended approach is: 

- Apply one-third of the target N at seeding, 

- Apply the remaining two-thirds 6 weeks after sowing (WAS). 

Applying all N at seeding often results in reduced yield. Timing has minimal impact on grain quality. 

Varieties such as Carrolup and Williams are more sensitive to increasing N rates than Bannister, with higher risks of low hectolitre weight and elevated screenings (Figures 8 and 11). These risks can be mitigated by sowing in April and selecting varieties with superior grain plumpness and hectolitre weight. Goldie and Minnie outperform Bannister in grain plumpness, making them more resilient to high N rates. However, Minnie’s lower hectolitre weight may limit its yield potential under elevated N levels. 

Milling oats are generally less responsive to fertiliser N than barley. In 12 DPIRD trials (2022–24), oat yield peaked between 50 and 100 kg N/ha, compared to 100–150 kg N/ha in barley. Maximus CL barley increased yield by 1.7 t/ha with the addition of N (from 10 to 150 kg/ha), while Bannister and Goldie gained 0.8 and 0.9 t/ha, respectively. The rate of N applied to milling oats should be less than what is applied to Maximus CL, the dominant barley variety in WA. 

Hectolitre weight in oats declines more sharply with increasing N than in barley. For every 50 kg N applied, hectolitre weight dropped by 1.4 kg/hL in Bannister, 1.1 in Goldie, and 1.2 in Koala, compared to just 0.4 in Maximus CL (12 trials, 2022–24). Minnie’s response was similar to Goldie and Koala across 8 trials (2023–24). 

**168 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

Screenings also increase with higher N rates. Choosing a variety with higher grain plumpness (e.g. Goldie) can mitigate the risks associated with higher N fertiliser application. Across the 12 DPIRD trials, for every extra 50 kg of N applied, screenings rose by 2.6% in Bannister, 1.7% in Goldie, and 2.4% in Koala (2.0 mm sieve), compared to 3.6% in Maximus CL (2.5 mm sieve). Minnie’s screening response was comparable to Goldie across 8 trials (2023–24). 

## **Varietal difference in the target nitrogen rate for milling oats** 

In an economic analysis of 12 DPIRD N management trials (2022–24), using an OAT1 price of $360/t, urea at $835/t, and fixed costs (seeding, spraying and harvest) of $290/ha, the optimal N rate for Bannister and Koala was 45–55 kg/ha compared to Goldie at 70–80 kg N/ha. Goldie outperformed Bannister and Koala at their optimal N rate. 

In an economic analysis of 8 DPIRD N management trials (2023–24), using the same pricing, Bannister’s optimal N rate was 60–70 kg/ha, while profit peaked at 50–60 kg N/ha for Koala. For Goldie and Minnie, it was 80–90 kg/ha, with both outperforming Bannister and Koala in gross margin at their optimal N rates. 

Goldie and Minnie offer growers greater flexibility in N management than Bannister and Koala. However, Goldie and Koala carry a higher lodging risk under elevated N rates, whereas Minnie does not. 

## **Target plant density** 

Target plant density for oats should be calculated based on end-use (grain or hay), rainfall zone, kernel weight, viability and establishment conditions. Rather than using a fixed machinery seeding rate (kg/ha), growers should calculate seeding rates based on target plant density (plants/m²). 

For grain oats (based on a kernel weight of 30–45 mg): 

- **Low rainfall zone:** Target density = 160 plants/m² → Seeding rate = 55–85 kg/ha 

- **Medium rainfall zone:** Target density = 240 plants/m² → Seeding rate = 85–130 kg/ha 

- **High rainfall zone:** Target density = 240 plants/m² → Seeding rate = 85–130 kg/ha 

In 2025, DPIRD is investigating whether Goldie, when sown at hay production densities, can still meet OAT1 grain quality standards. This research addresses outdated density recommendations following the shift from mid-May to early April sowing. 

The target density in plants/m[2] determines the seeding rate in kg/ha and is calculated using the following formula: 

**Seed rate 1000 kernel weight (g) x target density (plants/m[2] ) = (kg⁄ha) germination % x establishment % x 100** 

For example, if sowing Bannister oats with a kernel weight of 35 g per 1000 kernels at a target density of 240 plants/m[2] with germination of 96% and an expected establishment of 80%, the seeding rate in kg/ha required to establish 240 plants/m[2] is: 

**35 x 240 seed rate in kg/ha = 109 kg/ha = 0.96 x 0.80 x 100** 

## **Management of grain staining in grain oats** 

Fungicide strategies can reduce, but not eliminate, the risk of grain staining. In highrisk environments, variety selection is the most effective tool for mitigating risk. DPIRD research indicates that untreated varieties with partial septoria resistance had lower levels of stained grain compared to highly susceptible varieties treated with multiple fungicide applications. 

Bannister is WA’s most widely sown oat variety for grain due to its yield advantage over Carrolup and better grain quality than Williams. However, despite sharing the same septoria resistance rating, Bannister is more prone to grain staining, especially in high rainfall zones, increasing the risk of receival downgrading. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 169** 

Oats 

Bannister is WA’s most widely sown oat variety for grain due to its yield advantage over Carrolup and better grain quality than Williams. However, despite sharing the same septoria resistance rating, Bannister is more prone to grain staining, especially in high rainfall zones, increasing the risk of receival downgrading. The relative risk of Goldie, Koala, and Minnie is unknown. 

In high disease pressure situations, such as oaton-oat rotations, high rainfall regions, or when growing susceptible varieties, a 2-spray fungicide regime is recommended: one at stem elongation (when more than 5% of the leaf area is affected) and another at flag leaf emergence. Under lower disease pressure or late-season infection, a single spray at flag leaf emergence is sufficient. Late-season rainfall can also cause staining, but fungicide applications at Z55–59 are generally ineffective. 

## **Harvest timing for grain oats** 

Timely harvest is essential to maximise yield and minimise losses due to shedding or lodging, particularly in non-dwarf varieties. Direct harvesting is preferred when grain moisture is below 12%. If moisture exceeds 12%, post-harvest aeration or drying is required. In uneven ripening or slow drying conditions, swathing should be considered. Desiccation is not permitted for oats in Australia. 

DPIRD research (2019) on 12 milling oat varieties found that delayed harvest significantly reduces yield. A 3-week delay resulted in a 10% reduction in grain yield, while a 6-week delay led to a 25% reduction. All varieties responded similarly to delayed harvest, reinforcing the importance of timely operations. 



**170 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

## **Hay – yield and quality** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

## **Quality standards for export hay** 

Establishing a contract with an exporter before sowing oats for export hay is essential to ensure alignment with market-specific quality requirements. Optimal hay yield and quality are typically achieved by cutting at or just before the watery ripe stage (Z71). Cutting earlier may improve quality, provided panicles are not retained within the boot. A harvest window of approximately 5–7 days post-Z71 allows flexibility before hay quality begins to decline. Rainfall events exceeding 10 mm after cutting can significantly compromise the quality of hay. 

Unlike grain, there is no universal standard for hay grading. In WA, processors assess the nutritional value of export hay using near-infrared spectroscopy (NIR) and wet chemistry, alongside physical traits like colour and stem thickness. Grading systems vary by processor; some use 4 or 5 grades, others a 100-point scale, depending on their target markets. 

Common grading parameters across exporters include: 

- **Crude protein (CP):** Reflects N concentration; essential for livestock maintenance and production. 

- **Metabolisable energy (ME):** Indicates usable energy after digestion losses. 

- **Dry matter digestibility (DMD):** Simulates rumen digestion; values greater than 65% indicate high-quality feed. Feed with a DMD below 55% is of poor quality and will not maintain live weight even if the stock has free access. 

- **Water soluble carbohydrates (WSC):** Measures total digestible sugars, including glucose and fructans. 

- **Neutral detergent fibre (NDF):** Quantifies structural fibre (hemicellulose, cellulose, lignin); a high NDF intake reduces feed intake. NDFDom30 assesses digestibility after 30 hours. 

- **Fibre digestibility (NDFDom):** Evaluates digestible fibre via rumen incubation; reported on an organic matter basis. 

- Measures less 

- **Acid detergent fibre (ADF):** digestible fibre (cellulose and lignin); lower ADF correlates with higher energy. 

- **Stem thickness:** Influences fibre content; 

- however, the stem-to-leaf ratio may be more critical. 

- **Greenness:** Assessed via near-infrared (NIR), Soil Plant Analysis Development (SPAD), photo analysis, or visual inspection. While not directly linked to nutrition, greenness reflects the impact of weathering and disease. 

While benchmarks for hay quality vary between exporters, DPIRD’s Table 10 outlines typical standards for WA export hay. Hay can also be downgraded for non-nutritional factors such as excessive moisture, weed contamination, and damage from disease or weather. 

To prevent spoilage, hay should be delivered with a maximum bale moisture of 14%, with some exporters requiring a moisture level as low as 12%. Hay with moisture above 18% risks spoilage from mould and self-combustion during storage. 

Most processors have a limit of 1% by weight of broadleaf plants and 5% of other cereals/ryegrass/ wild oats. Zero tolerance exists for toxic plants, double-gee, and foreign material, such as dirt, stones, sticks, insects, wool, wire, and carcasses. 

Livestock deaths from annual ryegrass toxicity (ARGT) in importing countries pose a serious threat to Australia’s hay export industry. All export hay must be tested for the bacterium _Rathayibacter toxicus_ , the causal agent of ARGT. If contamination is suspected, growers should implement a formal ARGT management plan. 

Most processors permit up to 10% diseaseaffected leaves. Growers must observe fungicide withholding periods and refrain from applying fungicides close to harvest time. Planting disease-resistant varieties is recommended. Export markets expect a clean, green product and monitor for breaches of maximum residue limits (MRLs) for herbicides, insecticides, and fungicides. Always follow label registrations for all chemical applications.

Oats

## **Fibre digestibility** 

Digestibility is increasingly recognised as a critical trait in export hay markets, particularly as highly digestible feeds enhance nutrient uptake in livestock and are associated with reduced methane emissions in the dairy industry. 

While digestibility is influenced by complex interactions between fibre, sugars, minerals, and rumen microflora, fibre content remains the most significant factor. Consequently, WA exporters are transitioning from using Dry Matter Digestibility (DMD) to Neutral Detergent Fibre Digestibility (NDFDom) as a more precise indicator of feed quality. 

NDFDom specifically evaluates the digestibility of the neutral detergent fibre (NDF) fraction, offering a more accurate assessment than whole-feed digestibility metrics. This trait is now widely adopted in hay grading systems across WA’s export markets and is considered a key benchmark for nutritional quality. 



## **Variety performance in hay variety trials** 

Hay variety performance in WA is informed by three primary sources: 

- InterGrain breeding trials (national MET analysis) (2018–24), 

- AgriFutures-supported National Hay Agronomy (NHA) trials (2019–21), and 

- DPIRD agronomy trials (2022–23). 

Table 11 presents MET data from 64 national trials, including 5 AgriFutures-funded and 2 DPIRDsupported trials. Of the 64 trials in the national MET, 5 were part of the AgriFutures-funded research and 2 were supported by DPIRD. Table 12 summarises DPIRD trial results from 4 WA sites over 2 years. Katanning (2022) and Wongan Hills (2023) were excluded due to poor conditions. Koala was trialled in 2023 but excluded from Table 12. DPIRD evaluated the hay performance of 3 dual-purpose oat varieties (Carrolup, Goldie, Yallara) and 6 hay-only varieties (Archer, Brusher, Koorabup, Kultarr, Mulgara, Wallaby). 

AgriFutures NHA trials (PRJ-011029) assessed 4 dual-purpose (Carrolup, Durack, Williams, Yallara) and four hay-only varieties (Brusher, Koorabup, Mulgara, Wintaroo) across WA, SA, Vic, and NSW over 2 sowing dates and 3 N rates (averaged due to the absence of variety × N interaction). Data from these trials is not included in this guide. 

The key findings across the InterGrain breeding (Table 11), NHA agronomy and DPIRD hay agronomy (Table 12) trials were: 

- **Hay yield:** 

   - InterGrain MET: hay-only varieties (except Koorabup) yielded 10.0–10.4 t/ha; dualpurpose varieties averaged 0.3 t/ha less. 

**172 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

   - DPIRD trials: most hay-only varieties (except Koorabup) yielded over 11 t/ha (ranging from 11.2 to 12.3 t/ha), while the dual-purpose varieties yielded about 1 t/ha less and ranged from 10.3 to 10.9 t/ha. 

   - NHA trials: yields ranged from 2–18 t/ha; 50% of cuts yielded 4.5–10.5 t/ha. Wintaroo had the highest average yield, Durack the lowest. Brusher was most affected by delayed sowing (–1.8 t/ha); Carrolup and Durack were least affected (–1.0 t/ha). 

- **CP:** Minimal genetic variation (1%). Minnie, Williams (dual-purpose), and Archer (hayonly) were the highest; Kultarr, Kingbale and Wintaroo were the lowest. 

- **DMD:** Genetic variation of over 3%, up to 6%. Of the varieties suitable for export hay, Bannister, Goldie, Mulgara, Wallaby, and Yallara generally had higher digestibility; Carrolup, Durack, and Kingbale had lower. 

- **WSC:** Goldie and Wallaby were the highest, followed by Mulgara, Kultarr, and Yallara. Archer, Durack and Koorabup were the lowest. 

- **NDF:** Genetic variation of 2 to 5%. Goldie had the lowest NDF among export-suitable varieties; Kingbale and Koorabup were among the highest. 

- **NDFDom30:** Genetic variation of 3 to 7%. Of the varieties suitable for export hay, Bannister, Goldie, Mulgara, and Wallaby had the most digestible NDF; Carrolup and Kingbale were among the lowest. 

- **ADF:** Genetic difference was 3 to 5%. Bannister, Goldie, Koala and Wallaby had the lowest ADF of the varieties suitable for export hay. Kingbale had the highest.

New variety highlights for export-hay: 

- **Archer:** Offers lower digestibility (DMD and NDFDom30), WSC, and higher fibre hay than Brusher, but enables export hay production in rotations with imidazolinone residues without yield loss. Registered for the IBS application of Sentry[®] to manage barley and brome grass incrop. Ready for cutting about a week later than Brusher from the same sowing date, with good early vigour, straw strength and hay colour retention. Archer is an ideal replacement for Yallara, which has proven to perform well with high WSC, low fibre (ADF, NDF), thin stems, competitive yield, low lodging risk, and good colour. It should be noted that Archer is later to cut than Yallara from the same sowing date and has thicker stems. 

- **Goldie:** Goldie consistently delivers high hay yield and quality, with superior digestibility (DMD, NDFDom30) and WSC compared to Brusher. Disease reactions are similar to Brusher, with cutting readiness a few days 

later. Produces shorter hay than Brusher with lower lodging risk, though panicle emergence may be less consistent. Goldie can be grown as a dual-purpose variety. Ongoing research is assessing whether Goldie, when sown at the hay target density (320 plants/m²), can meet OAT1 receival standards as reliably as at the grain target density (240 plants/m²). Success would confirm Goldie’s suitability as a true dual-purpose variety. 

- **Kingbale:** Lower hay quality than Archer and Brusher, but provides management flexibility by reaching watery ripe later at the same sowing date. Shows slightly better septoria tolerance than Archer and Brusher, but is susceptible to oat leaf (crown) rust (OLR). Yield and quality are comparable to Wintaroo, with the added benefit of imidazolinone residue tolerance and registered for use with an IBS application of Sentry[®] . Best suited to planting in April or sowing in environments where it rains through until October. Like Wintaroo, it is susceptible to lodging. 

**174 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

- **Koala:** Koala is a dual-purpose variety and is suitable for export hay. Comparable to Brusher in yield and quality. Reaches watery ripe later than Brusher and Goldie, allowing earlier sowing (including April) or later cutting. Produces shorter, stiffer straw than Brusher, and like many varieties targeted for grain, may have reduced panicle emergence compared to Brusher. Offers improved oat stem rust (OSR) resistance and comparable septoria and OLR resistance relative to Brusher. 

- **Koorabup:** Underperforms relative to other hay-only varieties, with lower yield, higher fibre, lower WSC, and reduced digestibility than Brusher. However, it offers the best septoria resistance among current hay-only and dual-purpose varieties. 

- **Kultarr:** Similar hay yield to Brusher but with slightly lower quality. Matures later and is more susceptible to OSR. Best suited to northern production areas, particularly north of the Great Eastern Highway. 

- **Minnie:** Performs well in research trials but is likely unsuitable for export hay due to short straw. Further feedback from industry and growers is required to confirm commercial suitability. 

- **Wallaby:** Exhibits the best overall hay quality among new oat lines, with high digestibility (DMD, NDFDom30), high WSC, and lower NDF and ADF than Brusher at equivalent yield. Offers improved OSR resistance and comparable septoria and OLR resistance. As it matures later, it supports alternate cutting dates when matched on-farm with other varieties, such as Brusher or Carrolup. The performance of Wallaby, however, is likely to be optimised when sown in April compared to those varieties. 

Varietal response to sowing date was inconsistent in small-plot trials and not easily predictable. While early sowing generally improves yield potential, further evaluation is needed for the new hay-only varieties, Archer, Kingbale, Kultarr, and Wallaby, as well as the dual-purpose varieties, Goldie and Koala.

Oats<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 175** 

Oats 

## **Management advice for hay oat growers** 

## **Blakely Paynter, Kylie Chambers, and Bronte Wackett (DPIRD)** 

Meeting export hay standards requires a combination of high-yielding varieties with favourable quality traits, optimal crop nutrition and disease management, and timely cutting and curing. While most varieties respond similarly to agronomic practices, selecting varieties with genetically superior quality traits increases the likelihood of meeting export specifications, especially when management inputs such as sowing date and N application vary. 

## **Time of sowing** 

Earlier sowing (late April to early May) increases the potential for higher hay yield but does not always improve hay quality compared to later sowing (late May to early June). Early sown hay typically has increased lodging risk, thicker stems, lower CP, higher lignin content, higher WSC, lower fibre digestibility and a greener appearance. 

Delaying sowing by 3–4 weeks may increase fibre content (NDF) but does not consistently improve fibre digestibility (NDFDom). This suggests that elevated lignin levels in early sown hay may have a stronger negative impact on digestibility than fibre content alone. Further research is required to validate the relationship between digestibility, yield, and fibre, as well as the response of these parameters to sowing date. 

## **Timing of cutting** 

Cutting oat hay at the watery-ripe stage (Z71), when the grain contains a clear, greenish liquid, is considered optimal for achieving a balance between yield and quality. At this stage, the crop is not heavily drawing on carbohydrates, and hay traits such as WSC, fibre content, and digestibility are near their peak. 

Practical tip – Spikelets ripen from top to bottom. Inspect top florets to determine cutting stage. Note that panicle emergence and grain filling may not follow a strict sequence due to the influence of both genetic and environmental factors. 

Choosing the optimal cutting time involves a careful balancing of quality, yield, panicle emergence, and current and forecasted weather conditions. The time to flowering of oats can affect hay quality. Climate conditions, particularly warm temperatures, can shorten this period. This can cause rapid maturation of oats, resulting in a higher ratio of stems to leaves and reducing hay quality due to its higher fibre content. It is vital to constantly monitor hay crops in the face of warmer conditions to ensure the Z71 stage is not missed, as the decline in quality will occur more rapidly. Weather considerations must be taken into account when selecting a cutting time, as rainfall events of 10 mm or more after cutting can drastically reduce quality. 

Cutting Before Z71: 

- **Pros:** Higher WSC, lower fibre and lignin, 

- thinner stems, improved digestibility. 

- **Cons:** Lower yield, poor panicle emergence, and a risk of missing quality targets. 

Cutting after Z71: 

- **Pros:** Higher yield. 

- **Cons:** Reduced hay quality due to increased fibre and lignin, lower digestibility. 

NHA trials demonstrated the effect of spring growing conditions on delayed cutting and the subsequent effect on hay quality. In 2020, WA trials at Muresk experienced a drier-thanaverage spring. Hay quality and quantity were optimised between full panicle emergence (Z59) and Z71. During this growth window, traits such as WSC, NDF, ADF, and leaf chlorophyll content plateaued but then deteriorated as the crop growth progressed beyond Z71. Crude protein progressively reduced following Z59. Further yield increases beyond Z71 were outweighed by lower quality, and profitability declined. 

Victorian trials at Rupanyup experienced a wetterthan-average spring, and most oat varieties held hay quality from Z59 to 14 days after Z71. Yield increased significantly as the crop matured. Cutting in the Z59 to Z71 window helps minimise curing time before quality rapidly declines. Slight delays in cutting can be tolerated when there are adequate spring moisture and mild conditions. 

**176 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

## **Tips for nitrogen fertiliser on export hay** 

N strategies for hay oats differ from those for grain oats, and excessive N rates can negatively impact both yield and quality. For hay delivery, the recommended strategy is to apply two-thirds of the target N at seeding and one-third at 6 WAS. In research conducted by DPIRD, hay produced using this timing strategy was greener and more digestible, with lower fibre and elevated WSC than other timing strategies while maintaining yield. To maximise quality, late N should be applied before stem elongation. 

The recommended N application timing for grain (one-third at seeding, two-thirds at 6 WAS) produced hay yields and hay greenness on par with the hay recommended treatment. However, this timing reduced the quality. Despite this, when high hay quality is not a concern, the grain-recommended treatment can still be a good strategy for dual-purpose crops in uncertain weather conditions. 

Hay research in WA found that: 

- **Lodging:** High N application results in more biomass and taller and greener plants with an increased risk of lodging (especially in susceptible varieties). 

- **Optimal yield:** The peak hay yield was achieved with 90 kg N/ha across all varieties and locations. In lower rainfall areas or where soil N exceeds 70 kg N/ha, 60 kg N/ha was often sufficient. 

- **CP:** N increased CP. 

- **WSC:** N decreased WSC. Applying more than 90 kg N/ha increased the risk of falling below the 22% WSC threshold for premium hay and raised lodging risk. 

- **Fibre:** N had neutral or negative effects on fibre traits (stem diameter, NDF, ADF, lignin) in NHA trials (2019–21) but increased these traits in DPIRD trials (2022–23). 

Varietal responses: 

- Hay quality traits responded similarly to increasing N across varieties. 

- Varieties with higher genetic WSC (e.g. Yallara) can tolerate more N before dropping a grade, unlike lower-WSC varieties like Koorabup. 

Care must be taken to avoid excessive N rates. Excessive fertiliser N can lead to nitrate accumulation in plant tissue, especially in years with low rainfall. This poses serious health risks to livestock, including digestive, respiratory, and reproductive issues, and can be fatal in extreme cases. 

## **Target plant density** 

Target plant density for oats varies depending on the intended end-use (grain or hay) and the rainfall zone. 

As with grain production, determining the correct seeding rate requires focusing on the target plant density (plants per square metre, plants/m²) rather than relying on a fixed machinery rate (kg/ha). Using a standard seeding rate can lead to inconsistent plant densities across seasons due to differences in seed size (by variety and source), seed viability, and establishment conditions. 

For hay production, a target density of 240 plants/m² is suitable in low rainfall zones, while 320 plants/m² is recommended in medium to high rainfall zones. 

To achieve 240 plants/m², the seeding rate should range from 85 to 130 kg/ha, assuming a 1000-kernel weight of 30–45 mg per kernel. 

To achieve 320 plants/m², the seeding rate should range from 110 to 170 kg/ha, based on the same kernel weight range. 

## **Lodging management in hay oats?** 

Lodging presents a logistical challenge for export hay producers, particularly in high-rainfall zones and for crops with vigorous early canopy growth due to high soil fertility. Lodging reduces hay yield, causes uneven crop ripening, complicates cutting at a uniform height, and affects curing time. 

One strategy to reduce lodging risk is the use of Moddus[®] Evo (250 g/L trinexapac-ethyl). This gibberellin biosynthesis inhibitor enhances stem strength and reduces plant height, though it may also delay flowering. The label rate for oats is 300–400 mL/ha, with application restricted to early stem elongation, specifically when the second node is detectable on the main tiller (Z32) and most other tillers have the first node detectable (Z30). 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 177** 

Oats 

An alternative approach is pre-stem elongation grazing, which can also reduce canopy bulk and lodging risk. 

While applying Moddus[®] Evo at Z30–32 can improve straw strength and reduce lodging, it carries risks. In some cases, it has led to yield reductions and affected panicle emergence, although hay quality remained unaffected. Because lodging risk cannot be reliably predicted at the time of application, careful consideration is needed before applying Moddus[®] Evo at the full label rate. 

Research from the National Hay Agronomy (NHA) project indicates that a lower rate of 200 mL/ha is safer for hay oats, offering improved straw strength and reduced lodging risk without significant yield penalties. The hay industry should consider seeking a label extension to permit this lower application rate, which would reduce both cost and risk. 

## **Gibberellic acid and stuck panicles in export hay** 

In dry seasons and low-rainfall environments, certain oat varieties may exhibit delayed or incomplete panicle emergence, where panicles remain partially enclosed in the leaf sheath until the top florets reach the watery-ripe stage. This presents a challenge for hay producers, who must choose between delaying cutting until panicles fully emerge or cutting at the optimal growth stage and enduring a longer curing time. Both scenarios can reduce hay quality due to: 

- Declining WSC levels. 

- Increased fibre content from later growth stages. 

- Extended environmental exposure during curing. 

- Additionally, stuck panicles increase the risk of saprophytic colonisation, further compromising hay quality. 

To address this, the National Hay Agronomy (NHA) project evaluated the use of gibberellic acid (GA), specifically ProGibb[®] SG, applied at Z30–32 (early stem elongation), Z37–39 (flag leaf emergence), and at both stages. 

GA application successfully elongated internodes, including the peduncle, resulting in taller plants. Notably, there were no adverse effects on hay yield or quality. However, later applications 

(post-flag leaf emergence) may be more effective in targeting peduncle elongation specifically, which is critical for panicle emergence. 

The most reliable long-term solution is breeding oat varieties with strong peduncle elongation capacity, ensuring panicle emergence under a range of seasonal conditions. Further research is needed to refine the timing and rate of GA and other growth regulators to selectively promote peduncle elongation and reduce the risk of stuck panicles. 

## **Using fungicides to protect hay quality in the swath** 

Rainfall and overcast conditions during windrow curing create ideal environments for saprophytic fungi such as _Alternaria_ spp. and _Cladosporium_ spp. to colonise dead or dying plant tissue. These fungi cause dark discolouration or spotting on oaten hay, significantly reducing its visual quality. Since visual appeal is a key determinant of market suitability in export hay markets, this staining can lead to lower economic returns for producers. 

The curing process itself becomes a risk factor when it coincides with or follows rainfall, as it extends the time hay remains exposed to environmental conditions that favour fungal growth. This not only affects appearance but also prolongs curing time, increasing the likelihood of further degradation. Current research suggests the variety of oat planted does not appear to influence susceptibility to post-cutting saprophytic colonisation or staining. 

Research conducted by DPIRD, with support from AgriFutures (projects PRJ-011029 and PRJ-016604), has explored the potential of foliar fungicides to reduce saprophytic colonisation after cutting. While these fungicides are primarily used for in-crop disease control, they may offer off-target benefits by limiting fungal growth on bleached leaf material in windrows. The findings suggest that fungicides should be applied during the season as needed to manage foliar diseases and maintain a healthy green leaf area, which indirectly reduces the substrate available for saprophytes. 

Late-season applications of Group 11 (QoIs) and Group 3 (DMIs) fungicides have shown off-target promise in reducing saprophytic growth. 

**178 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

Among these, strobilurins consistently provided the greatest reduction in fungal colonisation. Importantly, applications made 28 to 35 days before cutting were just as effective as those applied at the standard withholding period of 21 days, offering growers a wider cutting window and reducing the risk of chemical residues. 

It is essential that fungicide applications are made only when necessary and strictly in accordance with label recommendations, including rates and withholding periods. This ensures compliance with maximum residue limits (MRLs) and protects access to export markets. Growers are strongly advised to consult their fodder exporters regarding any concerns about chemical residues to confirm that treated hay remains suitable for export.

## **Disease and pest resistance** 

## **Manisha Shankar, Kylie Chambers, Geoff Thomas, Blakely Paynter, Carla Wilkinson and Daniel Huberli (DPIRD)** 

## **Foliar disease abbreviations:** 

- **BB** = bacterial blight 

- **BYD/CYD** = barley and cereal yellow dwarf 

- **CR** = crown rot 

- **OLR** = oat leaf (crown) rust 

- **OSR** = oat stem rust 

- **RLL** = red leather leaf 

## **Disease resistance abbreviations:** 

- **VS** = very susceptible 

- **SVS** = susceptible to very susceptible 

- **S** = susceptible 

- **MSS** = moderately susceptible to susceptible 

- **MS** = moderately susceptible 

- **MRMS** = moderately resistant to moderately susceptible 

- **MR** = moderately resistant 

- **RMR** = resistant to moderately resistant 

- **R** = resistant 

- _p_ = provisional rating 

_Refer to page 4 for interpreting resistance classification._ 

## **Adult resistance** 

Information on disease and virus resistance for oat grain and hay varieties is presented in Tables 13 and 14, along with the variety snapshots included in this guide. Ratings are based on adult plant stage resistance, which typically applies after flag leaf emergence. In some varieties and for certain diseases, these ratings may be relevant from late tillering to stem elongation. 

Resistance scores may change due to seasonal fluctuations in disease pressure, regional spread of diseases, climatic conditions, stubble retention practices, and the emergence of new pathotypes or races. In this sowing guide, there have been no significant changes in resistance scores due to the introduction of new pathotypes. 

## **Pathotype surveillance and fungicide resistance** 

Any oat varieties rated as MRMS, MR, or R that carry significantly higher levels of disease than expected should be sent for pathotype identification and fungicide resistance testing. Collect leaf samples before spraying the crop with a fungicide to ensure sample viability. 

Place samples infected with septoria, OLR, OSR or RLL in paper envelopes marked with the location, variety, disease and date collected. Fold the leaf in half so the infected area is on the inside. Please do not wrap leaf material in plastic or send it in plastic-lined envelopes. 

Send septoria-infected leaf material in paper envelopes to DPIRD, 1 Nash Street, Perth 6000, marked ‘Attention: Manisha Shankar’. For more information, contact Manisha Shankar via email at manisha.shankar@dpird.wa.gov.au or by phone at +61 8 9368 3533. 

Send OLR and OSR samples in paper envelopes directly to the Australian Cereal Rust Survey, The University of Sydney, Reply Paid 88076, Narellan NSW 2567. For more information on sample collection and submission, contact Matthew Williams (Australian Cereal Rust Control Program (ACRCP) Operations and Technical Officer) via email at matthew.williams@sydney.edu.au or by phone at +61 409 552 349, or go to the Australian Cereal Rust Survey website ( sydney.edu.au/ science/our-research/research-areas/life-andenvironmental-sciences/cereal-rust-research/rustreports ). 

**180 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

Samples suspected of infection with RLL can be sent to Kylie Chambers, DPIRD, 75 York Road (PO Box 483), Northam, WA, 6401, for inclusion in the DPIRD National Grains Diagnostic and Surveillance Initiative (NGDSI) project, supported by GRDC (project number DAW2305-004RTX). For more information, contact Kylie Chambers at kylie.chambers@dpird.wa.gov.au or by phone at 

+61 400 278 068. 

## **Oat septoria** 

Oat septoria, caused by _Phaeosphaeria avenaria_ f.sp. avenaria (asexual stage: _Stagonospora avenae_ f.sp. _avenaria_ ) is the most common oat disease in WA. Also known as septoria avenae blotch, it was detected in 90% of paddocks surveyed through the AgriFutures NHA project (PRJ-011029) and GRDC disease surveillance projects (DAW1907-002RTX and DAW2104003RTX). Oat septoria is host-specific; it does not infect wheat, and wheat septoria does not infect oats. 

Septoria begins as small, dark brown to purple oval or elongated spots on leaves. The lesions expand into larger light or dark brown blotches with yellow/brown borders. They can cover and kill entire leaves. They may also appear on panicles, leaf sheaths, and stems, forming greyish-brown or shiny black lesions. In severe cases, infection can lead to lodging. Septoria can cause a loss of up to 20% in grain yield, although 10% is more typical in high-rainfall zones. It reduces grain weight and increases screenings. In some varieties (e.g. Bannister), late-season rain can cause grain discolouration. Tall or slow-maturing varieties are generally less affected than short or fast-maturing types. Septoria can also reduce hay yield, quality, and appearance. It is a significant constraint to hay production. 

Almost all oat varieties planted in WA are rated as MSS or below to septoria. No milling variety has acceptable resistance to oat septoria, while the only hay-only variety with good tolerance is Koorabup (MRMS). 

## **Oat leaf (crown) rust (OLR)** 

Oat leaf rust (OLR), caused by _Puccinia coronata_ var. _avenae_ , is a significant foliar disease in oats. Commonly referred to as crown rust, the name derives from the shape of the spores, not the appearance of the symptoms. 

OLR appears as small, circular to oval pustules containing orange to yellow powdery spores. The pustules are primarily found on leaf tissue, but under heavy infection, it may also appear on stems and panicles. As the crop matures, pustules darken, producing black spores that are embedded in the leaf tissue. Spore masses are easily dislodged, aiding in disease spread. Disease develops most rapidly under moist conditions and at temperatures between 15 and 22 °C. OLR is host-specific; it does not infect wheat, and wheat leaf rust does not infect oats. Wild oats, however, do host OLR. 

OLR can cause losses of up to 50% in forage, hay and grain yield. It reduces forage and hay quality, affecting both nutritional value and palatability. It also affects grain quality, resulting in lower grain weight and increased screenings. 

Over 80% of the oat acreage is planted to varieties with moderate resistance (MRMS or better) to oat leaf rust (OLR), excluding Carrolup (VS), Kojonup (SVS), and Wandering (VS). Among hay-only types, Kingbale (SVS) has the weakest resistance. 

## **Oat stem rust (OSR)** 

Oat stem rust (OSR), caused by _Puccinia graminis_ var. _avenae_ , is a serious fungal disease of oats. It typically appears as elongated pustules containing reddish-brown powdery spores, primarily on stems, but can also affect leaves and panicles. The spore masses are easily dislodged, facilitating rapid spread. OSR is host-specific; it does not infect wheat, and wheat stem rust does not infect oats. Managing wild oat populations is vital as they host OSR. 

OSR thrives in warm (18–30°C) and humid conditions. Epidemics are more likely during wet spring seasons. The latent period (time from infection to new spore production) is 7–10 days under optimal conditions. Once a crop is uniformly infected, disease severity can increase rapidly. OSR can cause a yield loss of up to 90% in susceptible varieties. It also reduces hay yield, hay appearance, and nutritional value, as well as grain quality. While widespread outbreaks are rare, regional outbreaks are more common and can cause significant localised damage. 

Milling oats and most hay-only varieties are generally rated as MS or below to OSR, with Bilby (SVS), Kultarr (SVS) and Wandering (SVS) the most susceptible. Koala (MRMS), Mulgara (MR) and Wallaby (MRMS) have the best resistance to OSR. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 181** 

Oats 

## **Red leather leaf (RLL)** 

Red leather leaf (RLL), caused by _Neospermospora avenae_ , is a fungal disease of oats that typically appears during the tillering stage. It was first officially detected in WA in 2021, though it has likely been present for several seasons before that. Early symptoms include small, light grey to pale blue lesions with red or red-brown edges. From stem elongation to head emergence, red, irregular-shaped lesions spread across the leaves. Later in the season, the leaves develop a leathery texture, turning red to brown, and may appear slightly rolled. 

The impact of RLL on the yield and quality of hay and grain in WA is currently unknown. In eastern Australia, RLL has caused yield losses of up to 10% in susceptible varieties during favourable seasons. RLL is a significant concern for hay production, affecting both yield and appearance. 

RLL is most likely to affect WA oat crops in cooler, high- to medium-rainfall zones, particularly in the Great Southern region. Favourable conditions include high humidity, mild temperatures, and wet winters. RLL survives on crop residue, with infection arising from spores produced in infested residue. Secondary spread is from infected plants. 

## **Crown rot (CR)** 

Crown rot (CR), caused by _Fusarium pseudograminearum_ , is a fungal, stubbleborne disease most commonly associated with continuous cereal rotations. It affects the sub-crown internode, crown, and lower stems, and is typically not visible until after the plant has headed. Unlike wheat and barley, whiteheads are generally not observed in oats. The most reliable indicator of CR in oats is browning at the base of infected tillers. Infected tissue may also show pink discolouration under the leaf sheaths or around the crown. 

While wheat and barley can suffer average yield losses of nearly 20% under high CR pressure, milling oats have shown an average yield loss of just under 5%. No significant differences in CR tolerance have been observed among oat varieties tested to date. 

No in-crop fungicides are available to control CR once the crop is established. Registered seed dressings can help suppress early infection. To reduce inoculum levels, rotate with non-cereal crops (e.g. pulses, oilseeds, lupins, grass-free 

pastures, use inter-row sowing, and maintain effective grass weed control in break crops and fallows. Infected oat tillers can carry the pathogen into subsequent cereal crops; therefore, oats are not considered an effective break crop for crown rot. 

## **Barley and cereal yellow dwarf (BYD/CYD)** 

Both barley yellow dwarf (BYD) and cereal yellow dwarf (CYD) viruses are present in WA, with BYD occurring approximately twice as frequently as CYD. Resistance screening is conducted under field conditions, so varietal resistance scores reflect the combined impact of both viruses. Early seedling infection by BYD can result in yield losses of up to 80%. Later infections (post-tillering) may reduce yield by up to 20%. Infected plants often exhibit stunted growth, leaf yellowing, and reduced tillering. Oat plants are primarily infected by aphids, including those of the species _Rhopalosiphum padi_ (oat aphids) or _Rhopalosiphum maidis_ (corn leaf aphids). 

Varietal resistance helps reduce the impact of the virus, but does not prevent aphid feeding damage. Even in resistant varieties, aphid control is essential to prevent yield loss. Aphids must be sprayed once they reach threshold levels in the crop (50% of tillers with 15 or more aphids) to prevent yield loss from feeding damage. 

Most oat varieties are rated as MS or below to BYD and CYD. Carrolup is particularly susceptible and rated as SVS. 

## **Bacterial blight (BB)** 

Bacterial blight (BB) is a moisture-driven disease complex affecting oats, with stripe blight ( _Pseudomonas syringae_ pv. _striafaciens_ ) being the predominant form observed in WA. Halo blight ( _Pseudomonas syringae_ pv. _coronafaciens_ ) is less common. 

BB was present in 41% of paddocks surveyed over three years in the AgriFutures-supported NHA project (PRJ-011029). Disease severity was generally low, and damaging levels were rare. Risk is increased by high canopy humidity and wetness, sowing of BB-susceptible varieties (e.g. Bannister), and continuous oat cropping (oatson-oats rotation). Higher temperatures and lower spring humidity reduce upper canopy infection. 

**182 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Oats 

Stripe blight is found mainly on leaves and begins as water-soaked spots (no halo). The spots elongate into red-brown stripes, distorting the growth of young leaves. Stripes may develop yellow and red margins. The lesions can merge into irregular blotches, causing premature leaf senescence. If infection occurs at the boot stage, florets may appear rotten or stained, while emerged florets may be mottled brown to white and sterile. 

Halo blight causes oval, water-soaked spots that are pale green or yellow in colour. The centres turn yellow-brown, surrounded by a yellow-green halo. The lesions darken and merge into irregular blotches. Severe infection leads to premature leaf senescence. 

No registered products are currently available for BB control. As BB is a bacterial disease, fungicides are ineffective. Management relies on avoiding susceptible varieties, reducing moisture retention in the canopy, and rotating crops to break disease cycles. 

Most oat varieties are rated as MSS or below to BB. Bilby, Brusher and Kojonup are particularly susceptible and rated as SVS. 

## **Root lesion nematode (RLN)** 

Root lesion nematodes (RLN) caused by _Pratylenchus species_ , are microscopic, wormlike organisms that feed on plant roots, leading to yield losses in susceptible crops such as barley, wheat, and canola. RLN populations increase when susceptible crops and varieties are repeatedly grown, thereby raising the risk of economic damage. RLN infestations are found across approximately 6.25 million hectares, covering nearly 74% of WA’s winter cropping area. The most common species is _Pratylenchus neglectus_ (found in 70% of paddocks), followed by _Pratylenchus quasitereoides_ . Many paddocks contain multiple RLN species. Nematode populations potentially limit the yield of barley and wheat in over 50% of infested paddocks. However, yield loss in oat crops has not been investigated in WA. 

Additionally, incorporating non-host crops, such as pulses, oilseeds, lupins, or grass-free pastures, can help suppress RLN populations. Additionally, understanding the species-specific feeding preferences of nematode species allows for more effective and tailored crop rotations. 

The resistance scores in this guide are based on glasshouse and field trials conducted in WA. Varieties with fewer than 5 observations, or those for which there has been no field trial verification of the glasshouse rating, receive provisional ratings. All oat varieties tested caused RLN nematodes to increase over the growing season, with Williams being the most resistant milling variety and Mulgara the most resistant hay-only variety for both _P. neglectus_ and _P. quasitereoides_ . 

## **Cereal cyst nematode (CCN)** 

Cereal cyst nematode (CCN), caused by _Heterodera australis_ , is present in cropping regions around Geraldton, Esperance, and the Avon Valley, but can occur sporadically across the WA Wheatbelt. Oat varieties are less tolerant to CCN than barley. Yield loss can occur when oats are grown in paddocks infested with pests. CCN also poses a risk to future crops by increasing nematode populations. 

Choose a more resistant and tolerant variety if growing oats in a paddock infested with CCN. This should limit nematode multiplication for future crops and yield loss in the current oat crop. 

Planting CCN-resistant oat varieties slows nematode development, leading to lower nematode levels in the soil for subsequent crops. The milling oat varieties, Bannister, Durack, Goldie, Koala, Minnie and Yallara, can slow CCN numbers. Brusher, Kingbale, Kultarr, and Mulgara can slow CCN numbers among the hay-only varieties. 

Effective RLN management begins with identifying paddocks with yield-limiting nematode populations. Key strategies include rotating with resistant crops or varieties or incorporating fallow periods to reduce nematode numbers. 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 183** 

Oats 

## **Further reading** 

In Australia, fungal, bacterial, and viral pathogens can infect oats, impacting both grain and hay yield and quality. However, the overall effect of these diseases on oaten hay production remains poorly researched and understood, especially when compared to other cereal crops. 

The influence of disease on the nutritional quality of hay is not well defined. Export hay is assessed based on physical attributes (e.g. stem thickness, greenness) and nutritional metrics (e.g. WSC, ADF, NDF, and DMD). Diseases can reduce greenness and visual quality through lesions and chlorosis, as well as the growth of saprophytic fungi on dead tissue. These symptoms can lead to a downgrading of export hay, even if nutritional values are unaffected. 

A comprehensive 22-page review titled “Plant Diseases Impacting Oaten Hay Production in Australia” was published by DPIRD in September 2020 as part of the National Hay Agronomy (NHA) project, supported by AgriFutures (Project PRJ011029). 

This review encompasses disease symptoms and epidemiology, current control strategies, a list of registered seed dressings, in-furrow treatments, and foliar fungicides (as of August 2020), as well as withholding periods for grazing and harvesting after fungicide application. The review can be found online at agrifutures.com.au/product/plantdiseases-impacting-oaten-hay-production-inaustralia-a-review/ . 

Fact sheets on oat septoria, rusts (both OLR and OSR) and RLL published by the National Hay Agronomy Project are available on the AgriFutures - Knowledge Hub at agrifutures.com.au/knowledge hub .

Oats

> 1 Resistance rating: VS = very susceptible, SVS = susceptible to very susceptible, S = susceptible, MSS = moderately susceptible to susceptible, MS = moderately susceptible, MRMS = moderately resistant to moderately susceptible, MR = moderately resistant, RMR = resistant to moderately resistant, R = resistant, _p_ = provisional, # = may be more susceptible to alternate pathotypes over east, and – = no data available. _Refer to page 4 for interpreting resistance classification._ 

> 2 Pathotype: the strain of the pathogen used in evaluating the disease reaction of the different oat varieties, representing the most common pathotype in WA. Therefore, on-farm reactions of varieties may differ if the pathotype present differs from the pathotype used in testing. 

3 Growth stage: the seedling resistance score reflects resistance at the 2 to 3-leaf stage, and the adult resistance score reflects 

resistance after the emergence of the flag leaf. 

> 4 Barley and cereal yellow dwarf: plants become infected from infected oat and corn leaf aphids. Varietal resistance reduces the virus’s effect on plant growth, but does not mitigate the impact of aphid feeding on plant growth.

2 Growth stage: the seedling resistance score reflects resistance at the 2 to 3-leaf stage, and the adult resistance score reflects resistance after the emergence of the flag leaf. 

> 3 Root lesion nematode: oat varieties vary in the impact of root-lesion nematode on their growth. A resistant variety retards nematode development, leading to lower nematode levels in the soil for subsequent crops. Ratings are based on data collected in WA. 

> 4 CCN: oat varieties differ in their resistance (a resistant variety retards nematode development) and tolerance (tolerant varieties yield better in the presence of nematodes). CCN resistance and tolerance data are based on variety responses in SA.

Oats 

## **Variety snapshots** 

## **Blakely Paynter (DPIRD)** 

This document presents snapshots for oat varieties grown in Western Australia, grouped by their suitability for milling and export hay markets: 

- **Dual-purpose varieties (9):** Bannister, Carrolup, Durack, Goldie, Koala, Kojonup, Wandering, Williams, and Yallara. These are deliverable into milling oat segregations in WA and are also suitable for export hay. 

- **Grain-only varieties (2):** Bilby and Minnie. These are deliverable into milling oat segregations in WA but unsuitable for export hay. 

- **Hay-only varieties (7):** Archer, Brusher, Kingbale, Koorabup, Kultarr, Mulgara, and Wallaby. These are suitable for export hay but cannot be delivered into milling oat segregations in WA. 

Each snapshot includes key agronomic and quality traits, highlighting yield performance, strengths, and weaknesses. Yield comparisons are based on data from the Long Term MET Yield Reporter (available at NVT, nvt.grdc.com.au ) presented relative to a control variety, typically Bannister, rather than the site mean yield shown in Tables 3–8. Additionally, single-site MET data from Table 9 are used to indicate the likelihood of a variety yielding less, the same, or more than another when grown under identical agronomic conditions. 

Disease resistance ratings for grain varieties are primarily sourced from DPIRD under a service agreement with GRDC for the NVT system. Where data gaps exist, supplementary information may be obtained from InterGrain. Ratings for diseases and nematodes are presented for adult plant stages (where available) and are drawn from Tables 13 and 14. 

Phenology data is generated using DPIRD’s predictive model, FlowerPower oat ( fp.dpird. app/ ). This statistical tool estimates the date of the panicle emergence (Z49) for oats in 2 WA environments, Northam and Katanning, based on historical temperature data (sourced from the SILO database: longpaddock.qld.gov.au/silo/point-data/ ). 

Predictions are provided for 5 sowing dates: 10 April, 20 April, 10 May, 20 May, and 10 June. The phenology data shown in the snapshots reflect the median predicted date to Z49 (i.e. the date expected in 50% of seasons), based on FlowerPower oat version v2025.02.24. Varieties have been graded on their phenology relative to Carrolup, where early = -10 to -3 days, medium = -3 to +4 days, late = +4 to +11 days, very late = +11 to + 18 days, and very, very late = +18 to +25 days after Carrolup when sown on 25 April at Northam and Katanning. 

Agronomic characteristics are compiled from multiple sources, including SARDI newsletters, DPIRD trials, GRDC co-funded projects (DAW00107, DAW00227, DAW1901-002RTX), and InterGrain. Traits covered include: 

- **Plant type:** Based on the genetic background of the variety. Data sourced from SARDI. 

- **Coleoptile and coleoptile + mesocotyl length:** Short = 40–60 mm, medium = 60–80 mm, long = 80–100 mm, very long = 100–120 mm and extremely long = >120 mm. Oats are unique among winter cereals in possessing a mesocotyl, an organ between the seed and coleoptile that aids emergence from deep sowing. In contrast, wheat and barley rely solely on coleoptile elongation. At DPIRD, the coleoptile and mesocotyl lengths were measured after germinating seeds in rolled, moistened filter paper for 15 days at 15 °C in the dark. The data was sourced from DPIRD and InterGrain. 

- **Stem diameter:** Ratings based on data published by SARDI, where fine = <4 mm, moderate = 4–6 mm, thick = 7–8 mm and very thick = >8 mm. Data sourced from SARDI. 

Details on pedigree, seed licensees, trading restrictions, and End Point Royalties (EPR) are sourced from breeding companies, Variety Central Central ( varietycentral.com.au/ ) and IP Australia’s Plant Breeders Rights database ( ipsearch. ipaustralia.gov.au/pbr/ ). 

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 187** 

Oats 

## **Bannister**[A] 

**OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Bannister (tested as WAOAT2354) is a tall, late-maturing milling oat variety widely used for export hay. It was WA’s most sown oat in 2025, covering just under 60% of the area. Bannister accounted for half of export hay deliveries reported by AEXCO. It is susceptible to grain staining, so growers should avoid sowing in high-risk environments, oat-on-oat rotations, or areas prone to pre-harvest rain. With early May sowing, Bannister reaches watery ripe about a week and half later than Brusher and a week later than Mulgara. In breeder trials, it showed better hay quality than Carrolup and comparable quality to Brusher, though with slightly lower hay yield.

## **Carrolu p** 

**OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Carrolup (tested as 81Q:346) is a tall, medium-spring milling oat variety suitable for export hay. It has excellent hectolitre weight, the highest among current milling varieties, but tends to have high screenings, similar to Williams. Grain yield is significantly lower than Bilby, Bannister, Goldie, Koala, Minnie, and Williams. Carrolup is very susceptible to OLR and should be managed accordingly. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe a few days earlier than Brusher and a week earlier than Mulgara. Hay quality is comparable to specialist hay varieties, though yield is lower. Carrolup remains WA’s second most sown oat, occupying 16% of the area in 2025. Its planted area has remained stable between 16–20% since Bannister’s release in 2014.

Oats 

## **Durack**[A] 

## **OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Durack (tested as WA02Q302-9) is an early-spring, mid-tall milling oat variety suitable for export hay and deliverable into OAT1 segregations from the 2025–26 harvest. It has similar height, grain yield, and hectolitre weight to Carrolup and Yallara, but offers improved grain plumpness over Carrolup and similar screenings to Yallara. Durack is the earliest maturing oat variety among current milling and hay types. While early flowering supports large grain development, it may increase frost risk—so sowing between May and mid-June is recommended in frost-prone areas. With early May sowing, Durack reaches watery ripe just over a week earlier than Brusher and nearly two weeks earlier than Mulgara. It is suitable for export hay, though hay yields are generally lower than Carrolup and Williams. Durack is susceptible to oat septoria and OLR. It’s popularity with growers has slipped. In 2025, Durack occupied less than 0.5% of the total oat area.

## **Goldie**[A] 

## **OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Goldie (tested as 13009-18) is a medium-spring, tall milling oat derived from Bannister that is suitable for export hay. It is taller than Bannister but shorter than Carrolup, with a higher leaning risk than Bannister and a similar disease resistance profile. Goldie offers plumper grain and equal or better hectolitre weight than Bannister, improving its likelihood of meeting OAT1 specifications. It also shows higher yield potential below 5 t/ha, supporting stronger gross margins. With early May sowing, Goldie reaches watery ripe a few days earlier than Brusher and a week earlier than Mulgara. Its hay is shorter than Brusher’s, with lower lodging risk at cutting and moderate stem thickness. Panicle emergence was lower than Brusher in DPIRD (2022–23) and InterGrain (2021–23) hay trials. If Goldie hay meets export receival standards it will be received by export hay companies. The area planted to Goldie jumped in 2025. It is now the third most popular variety, occupying 12% of the total oat area.

Oats 

## **Koala**[A] 

## **OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Koala (tested as 09143-35) is a tall, very late-spring milling oat derived from Bannister that is suitable for export hay. It shares similar tolerance to oat septoria and OLR as Bannister but offers improved resistance to OSR. Grain quality is comparable to Bannister, with similar hectolitre weight and screenings. With early May sowing, Koala reaches watery ripe a week later than Brusher and Mulgara. Its hay is shorter than Brusher’s, with lower lodging risk at cutting and moderate stem thickness. Panicle emergence was lower than Brusher in DPIRD’s 2023 hay trials and appears similar to Bannister. If Koala hay meets export receival standards it will be received by export hay companies. There has been a modest update of Koala. In 2025, it occupied just under 1% of the total oat area. 

## **Kojonup**[A] 

**OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Kojonup (tested as 91Q291-23-23) is a medium-spring, medium-height milling oat suitable for export hay, though not preferred. It yields between Carrolup and Bannister and offers large seed size, high hectolitre weight, and low screenings. However, it is susceptible to oat septoria and OLR and not recommended for low rainfall regions (<200 mm GSR). While suitable for export hay, Kojonup’s hay yields are generally lower than Carrolup. It remains a minor variety, occupying less than 0.5% of WA’s oat area in 2025.

## **Williams**[A] 

**OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Williams (tested as WAOAT2332) is a mid-tall, late-spring milling oat suitable for export hay. It has grain yield similar to Bannister and Wandering but may lodge in high-yielding environments. Grain quality is lower than Bannister and Yallara, with reduced hectolitre weight and higher screenings, especially in low rainfall areas. It contains elevated grain β-glucan. With early May sowing, Williams reaches watery ripe a few days later than Brusher and is similar to Mulgara. Hay yields are 0.5–1.0 t/ha lower than specialist hay varieties like Brusher, Mulgara, and Winjardie, though hay quality is comparable, with slightly lower WSC and slightly higher crude protein. Due to thick stems, a target density of 320 plants/m² is recommended for export hay production. Williams was WA’s fifth most sown oat in 2025, occupying 4% of the planted area..

## **Yallara**[A] 

## **OAT1 grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Yallara (tested as SV97001-13-4) is a tall, medium-spring milling oat suitable for export hay. It has grain yield similar to Carrolup, with better disease resistance, including good tolerance to OLR and some resistance to oat septoria. Grain quality features include slightly lower hectolitre weight than Carrolup, improved grain plumpness, and bright, highly digestible grain, making it suitable for the horse racing industry. With early May sowing, Yallara reaches watery ripe at a similar time to Brusher and a few days earlier than Mulgara. Hay yields are slightly higher than Williams and comparable to Brusher, producing high-quality hay with moderately fine stems. In 2025, Yallara was WA’s fourth most popular oat, covering 4% of the grain area and 16% of hay deliveries of varieties with an EPR.

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 191** 

Oats 

## **Wanderin g** 

## **OAT2/OWAN grain and hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Wandering (tested as WAOAT2052) is a medium-height, very late-spring feed oat received as OAT2 and OWAN only. It has grain yield comparable to Bannister and Williams but performs less competitively at sites yielding above 3 t/ha. Wandering is suitable for hay cutting, though not preferred by the export industry. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe a few days later than Brusher and is similar to Mulgara. Hay yields are higher than Carrolup, with improved digestibility and WSC. It is very susceptible to OSR and was WA’s sixth most sown oat in 2025, occupying just under 2% of the planted area. 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

## **Bilb y**[A] 

## **OAT1 grain variety** 

## **Comments** 

Bilby (tested as 06204-16) is a short, medium-spring milling oat variety unsuitable for export hay. It offers higher yield than Bannister below 3 t/ha, and grain with a higher hectolitre weight, and similar to lower screenings. Bilby grain also contains high β-glucan. It is susceptible to oat septoria and OSR, and was WA’s seventh most sown oat in 2025, occupying 1% of the planted area.

Oats 

## **Minnie**[A] 

## **OAT1 grain variety** 

## **Comments** 

Minnie (tested as 15010-09) is a short, medium-spring grain oat derived from Bannister, designed for environments prone to lodging and well-suited to April sowing. It has improved straw strength, a similar disease resistance package to Bannister, and plumper grain (similar to Goldie). However, hectolitre weight is lower than Bannister and Goldie based on NVT and DPIRD data. Due to its short height, Minnie is likely unsuitable for export hay, though industry feedback is needed to confirm its acceptability at scale. Grower adoption of Minnie should begin in 2026.

## **Archer**[A] 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Archer (tested as GIA1803-040) is a medium-height, late-spring hay-only oat designed for use in paddocks with imidazolinone residues without yield loss and for IBS application with Sentry[®] herbicide. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe a few days later than Brusher and is similar to Mulgara. Archer is comparable to Yallara in panicle emergence, height, hay yield, and quality, but has a slightly later cutting date (up to one week) and improved tolerance to oat septoria. Has good early vigour and high hay colour retention. A target density of 320 plants/m² is recommended to manage its slightly thicker stems. CCN requires rotational management. Archer comprised 10% of AEXCO hay deliveries in 2024. Archer has high grain yield, aiding seed bulk-up for future hay crops. However, grain cannot be delivered to Bunge or CBH but may be used for domestic feed or on-farm consumption. Farmer-to-farmer trade of seed is not permitted. 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 193** 

Oats 

## **Brusher** 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Brusher (tested as SV87103-109) is a very tall, medium-spring hay-only oat and the most widely sown hay-only variety for export hay. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe a few days earlier than Mulgara and a couple of days later than Carrolup. Brusher hay is similar in height to Mulgara and Kingbale, but with thinner stems, lower fibre, and improved DMD, ME, and WSC compared to Kingbale. It delivers better hay yield and quality than Carrolup and is suitable for lower rainfall regions. Brusher grain has low hull lignin, enhancing feed grain quality. However, it is susceptible to OSR, and like other specialist hay varieties, requires more detailed management than dual-purpose types. Statistics on the tonnages of Brusher received for export hay are no longer kept by AEXCO following the expiry of its plant breeder rights.

## **Kin bale**[A] **g** 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Kingbale (tested as GIA17010-1) is a very tall, very late-spring hay-only oat designed for use in paddocks with imidazolinone residues and for IBS application with Sentry[®] herbicide. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe nearly two weeks later than Brusher and Mulgara, mirroring Wintaroo. It shares a similar profile to Wintaroo, with good early vigour, hay colour retention, high panicle emergence, CCN resistance, and a tall plant type. However, it is susceptible to lodging at cutting, and a larger seed area is needed for bulk-up due to poor grain yield (lower than Carrolup). Kingbale comprised 12% of AEXCO hay deliveries in 2024. Kingbale is best suited to April sowing or environments with rainfall extending into October. Grain cannot be delivered to Bunge or CBH but may be used for domestic feed or on-farm consumption. Farmer-to-farmer trade of seed is not permitted. 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

Oats 

## **Koorabu p**[A] 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Koorabup (tested as 05096-32) is a very late-spring hay-only oat developed for WA. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe a week later than Brusher and Mulgara and is just over a week and a half weeks later to cut than Carrolup. It has similar height and hay yield to Carrolup, but with better resistance to oat septoria. Koorabup offers comparable grain yield to Carrolup, supporting easy seed bulk-up for future hay crops. Hay yields are lower than Archer and Brusher, and similar to Carrolup. It has better lodging and shattering resistance than Wintaroo and Brusher and is similar to Mulgara. However, hay quality has tested poorly in breeder and NHA trials since 2017, and Koorabup remains a minor export hay variety.

## **Kultarr**[A] 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Kultarr (tested as 07423-18) is a very tall, late-spring hay-only oat with hay yields comparable to established varieties. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe nearly a week later than Brusher and is similar to Mulgara. It has good early vigour, moderate stem thickness, high panicle emergence, and a suitable hay quality profile based on small plot trial data. Kultarr offers valuable resistance to oat septoria, OLR and CCN but is sensitive to OSR. Grain yield is similar to Carrolup, supporting easy seed bulk-up for future hay crops. However, adoption for export hay production has been limited to date. Suits production areas north of Great Eastern Highway. Still a minor export hay variety

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 195** 

Oats 

## **Mul ara**[A] **g** 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Mulgara (tested as SV96025-7) is a very tall, late-spring hay-only oat with excellent resistance to OLR and OSR but rated S/MS to oat septoria. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe a few days later than Brusher and about a week after Carrolup. Mulgara offers improved lodging and shattering resistance, as well as stronger early vigour compared to Kingbale. Hay yield is higher than Carrolup and comparable to Brusher, with better digestibility (DMD and NDFDom30) and WSC, similar fibre, and excellent hay colour with resistance to brown leaf tipping. Mulgara is a strong alternative to Kingbale in environments with recurring OLR or lodging issues. Due to its large seed size, sowing rates should be adjusted to achieve 320 plants/m² in medium to high rainfall areas. However, area sown to Mulgara is declining. South Australian research indicates good tolerance to sodic and saline soils, better performing than Brusher. 



**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

## **Wallab y**[A] 

## **Hay variety** 

## **Comments** 

Wallaby (tested as 07079-9) is a medium to tall, very late-spring hay-only oat with hay yields comparable to established varieties. With early May sowing, it reaches watery ripe two weeks later than Brusher and just over a week later than Mulgara, offering flexibility in cutting programs. Wallaby has good hay quality, including high digestibility (DMD and NDFDom30), WSC, hay colour, moderate stem diameter, and reasonable panicle emergence (lower than Brusher in 2022–23 DPIRD trials). It provides strong resistance to OLR, OSR, and CCN. Grain yield is at least equivalent to Carrolup, supporting easy seed bulk-up for future hay crops. Wallaby remains a minor export hay variety, but its popularity is slowly growing.

**Pulse guide**

Pulse guide 

## **Pulse guide** 

**By** Mark Seymour, Stacey Power, Harmohinder Dhammu, Greg Shea, Martin Harries, Geoff Thomas, Jean Galloway and Ciara Beard (DPIRD) with contributions and edits from Stuart Nagel (SARDI), Joshua Fanning and Abeya Tefera (DEECA – Agriculture Victoria), Sam Catt and Jeff Paull (University of Adelaide), Kristy Hobson (DPIRD NSW) and Janine Sounness (GIA) 

## **Introduction** 

Pulses can be useful break crops to grow in rotation with cereals and canola. A well-managed pulse crop can reduce disease in following crops, control grass weed populations and fix nitrogen. Cereal yields and grain protein are usually maximised following a pulse, lupin or pasture legume. After peaking in the 1990s, pulse crop areas declined due to an expansion of canola and difficulties with in-crop control of broadleaf weeds and diseases. 

New varieties with improved herbicide tolerance and resistance to key pathogens are now available to address these challenges. 

## **Relative yield of crops in WA**

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 199** 

Pulse guide 

## **Picking a pulse** 

## **Inoculation of pulse crops** 

One of the key benefits of growing pulses is their ability to fix atmospheric nitrogen, resulting in high protein content in both the pulse grain and the stubble remaining in the paddock. To nodulate roots and fix nitrogen, pulses need to be inoculated with a specific species of rhizobia bacteria. Due to environmental factors limiting bacteria survival, it is recommended that inoculation of pulses happens each season, particularly where the soil is acidic or a pulse in the same inoculation group hasn’t been grown 

in several years. Previously, Group E (field pea, lentil, and vetch) and Group F (faba bean and broad bean) inoculants have been sold in combination. These groups are now sold separately, as new high-performing rhizobia strains for each species have become available. The new strains retain the ability of the old strains to nodulate roots in paddocks where acidity is not an issue, and they can effectively nodulate crop roots at lower pHs than previous strains, giving farmers more confidence in achieving optimal nodulation at the lower end of the suitable pH range. 



_Nicky Tesoriero, DPIRD Esperance, inspecting a faba bean trial_

**2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide 201** 

**Lupin** 

**202 2026 Western Australian Crop Sowing Guide** 

Lupin 

## **Lupin** 

## **Introduction** 

Narrow-leaf lupins are uniquely suited to the acid and sandy soils found across large tracts of the WA grainbelt and play an important role in breaking cereal disease cycles and adding fixed nitrogen to cropping systems. 

Increased use of canola as a break crop in recent years has seen lupin production in WA decline from a high of more than one million hectares in the late 1990s to about 300,000 to 400,000 hectares, with a current gross value of production of about $200 million. 

In September 2019 a new variety of narrowleaf lupin called Coyote was released. It is early maturing (similar to PBA Jurien), with metribuzin tolerance similar to Mandelup. Coyote is susceptible to phomopsis stem infection, so lupin stubbles should be grazed with care in high-risk environments. 

Tables 1 to 8 have data on varietal performance across Agzones as well as other important information such as disease and lodging ratings, and the seed quality of narrow-leaf lupins. 

## **Recent release** 

Australian Grain Technologies (AGT) released a narrow-leaf lupin variety, Rosemont, in September 2023, with an end point royalty (EPR) of $4.50 per tonne (GST exclusive). 

Rosemont has excellent early vigour, with taller plant height and better lodging resistance than PBA Jurien. It’s a slower maturing variety that is broadly adapted to all WA regions, including the south. It has a white flower and seed coat. 

Rosemont has had good yields in National Variety Trials (NVT), particularly in Agzones 1 and 2, although it has only been trialed since 2022. It has good tolerance of metribuzin. 

## **What variety should I grow?** 

Besides stable high yields, growers generally choose varieties with sufficient metribuzin tolerance for broadleaf weed control, as well as anthracnose tolerance and low pod shatter. In recent years the most widely grown variety has been PBA Jurien.
